Gold Level criteria 
Our goal is to create superior climate and biodiversity related benefits through The 
Pekanbaru Suntara Peatland Project (PSPP) under the Verified Carbon Standard 
(VCS) and Climate, Community and Biodiversity (CCB) standards. 
Climate: 
The PSPP will reduce emissions by an average of 582,443 tCO2e per year by 1) 
restoring and protecting threatened and degraded peatland areas; 2) preventing 
unplanned deforestation; and 3) reforesting non-forested areas. This will be done 
by restoring hydrological conditions in the project area by building dams to block 
the water channels that currently flow into the project area, preventing the peat 
soil from being decomposed by microorganisms and igniting fires. It will also 
increase CO2 removals by avoiding deforestation and reforesting through prior 
notification and forest patrols. For more information on climate benefits generated 
by project activities, please refer to Section 3. 
Community: 
There are three villages in the project area, namely Kelurahan Sungai Geniot, 
Kelurahan Batu Teritip and Kelurahan Basilam Baru. The project will target these 
communities with community development projects. The project will provide direct 
employment opportunities to these communities and promote the development of 
local communities by improving educational conditions, providing alternative 
livelihood activities, improving clean water sources and funding religious activities, 
and ensuring equal participation of women and vulnerable groups. 
Biodiversity: 
PSPP will protect critical habitats for 10 endangered species. Protected species in 
the project area include Sumatran tigers, white-handed gibbons, otters, Malayan 
crocodiles, sambar deer, Malayan sun bears, silver leaf monkeys and other 10 
endangered species that provide important habitats. PSPP will strengthen the 
protection of the project area, thereby protecting the endangered habitats on 
which these species depend for survival. Please refer to the final PD for detailed 
information on the biodiversity benefits generated by project activities. 
Expected verification 
schedule  
Initial verification monitoring period 2024-2025 
Prepared by 
Organization: Indacom, Pte, Ltd.

1 SUMMARY OF PROJECT BENEFITS 
This section highlights of this project’s important benefits. Section 1.1 (Unique Project Benefits) is 
aligned with a project’s causal model and is specific to this project. Section 1.2 (Standardized Benefit 
Metrics) provides the same quantifiable information for all CCB projects.  
1.1 Unique Project Benefits 
The Pekanbaru Suntara Peatland Project (PSPP) aims to generate a variety of project benefits 
throughout the lifetime of our project. This section outlines unique project benefits that are not 
captured in Section 1.2 below. 
 
Outcome or impact estimated by the end of project lifetime 
Section 
reference
 
1) Fire prevention and fire threat reduction, achieved through: 
⚫ Reducing illegal human activities within the PSPP area (which is the greatest 
risk for fire) to prevent fires; 
⚫ Fire tower-based monitoring for early detection and mitigation of fires; 
⚫ Forest patrols focused on detecting and identifying hotspots of fire threats and 
continuous monitoring of fire threats in the PSPP; 
⚫ Providing fire training to PSPP staff working within the project area;  
⚫ Providing fire training to nearby concessions and PSPP stakeholders. 
Through our project efforts, we aim to achieve a goal of zero fires within the project 
area and maximize fire resistance in the PSPP and surrounding landscapes. These 
efforts will allow forest ecosystems to recover, provide a wider range of habitats for 
key species, and reduce negative health impacts of fire smoke and haze on local 
communities. 
2) Peat rewetting and hydrological management, achieved by: 
⚫ Avoiding ongoing logging activities for palm plantations in the PSPP project 
area, which would lead to canal construction in the project area and cause the 
peatlands to dry up; 
⚫ Carrying out canal plugging activities according to the actual situation in the 
project area, taking into account channel demand, hydrological flow, priority 
restoration areas, etc.; 
2.1.17 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
5 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
⚫ Continuously monitoring groundwater levels and canal plugging. Through 
monitoring data, continuously optimize and improve the hydrological model and 
canal plugging in the project area; 
⚫ Carry out hydrological management cooperation with neighboring concessions 
to prevent the surrounding areas from negatively affecting the hydrological 
conditions in the project area. 
Canal plugging activities are essential to support the PSPP area to restore its 
natural ecological conditions. Raising groundwater levels reduces the risk of fire, 
prevents microbial decomposition and peat sedimentation, and provides the 
necessary hydrological conditions for the continuous regeneration of the forest. 
3) Strengthen community management and improve local livelihoods and economy, 
by: 
⚫ Create sustainable alternative livelihoods for people in the project community, 
increase local community income, reduce forest pressure, and improve local 
community resilience to climate change; 
⚫ Provide revolving fund assistance to vulnerable groups such as local low-
income families and women, which will help strengthen local communities' 
resilience to climate change; 
⚫ Provide educational opportunities for local community residents. 
By improving community management and development, establishing a strong 
support relationship with local communities, preventing illegal activities such as 
poaching and hunting by community residents from hindering the restoration of 
ecological functions in the project site, reducing the risk of future encroachment, 
and bringing tangible benefits to surrounding communities. 
4) Protect biodiversity, by: 
⚫ Through the implementation of the above-mentioned patrol, fire-fighting and 
canal blocking activities, the amount and variety of biodiversity will increase. 
This is due to the reduction of threats and the restoration of natural habitats; 
⚫ The biodiversity of the project area will be monitored by using standardized 
monitoring procedures, and field staff will be trained in plant and animal 
identification, monitoring procedures and data analysis; 
Through the management and monitoring activities of biodiversity in the project 
area, it will help prevent the risk of species reduction and habitat fragmentation in 
the project area.

1.2 Standardized Benefit Metrics 
 
1
 Land with woody vegetation that meets an internationally accepted definition (e.g., UNFCCC, FAO, or IPCC) of what constitutes a forest, 
which includes threshold parameters, such as minimum forest area, tree height and level of crown cover, and may include mature, 
secondary, degraded and wetland forests (VCS Program Definitions) 
2
 Reduced emissions from deforestation and forest degradation (REDD) - Activities that reduce GHG emissions by slowing or stopping 
conversion of forests to non-forest land and/or reduce the degradation of forest land where forest biomass is lost (VCS Program 
Definitions) 
3
 Afforestation, reforestation and revegetation (ARR) - Activities that increase carbon stocks in woody biomass (and in some cases soils) by 
establishing, increasing and/or restoring vegetative cover through the planting, sowing and/or human-assisted natural regeneration of 
woody vegetation (VCS Program Definitions) 
4
 Improved forest management (IFM) - Activities that change forest management practices and increase carbon stock on forest lands 
managed for wood products such as saw timber, pulpwood, and fuelwood (VCS Program Definitions)

2 PROJECT DETAILS 
2.1 Project Goals, Design and Long-Term Viability  
2.1.1 Summary Description of the Project (VCS, 3.2, 3.6, 3.10, 3.11, 3.13, 3.14; CCB, 
G1.2) 
The Pekanbaru Suntara Peatland Project (PSPP) is a peatland and forest conservation and restoration 
project located in Dumai, a subdistrict in Pekanbaru, Riau Province, Indonesia, implemented on forest 
concession land where PT. Suntara Gajapati holds a forest utilization management license, with a total 
area of 35,644.39 hectares. The PSPP is being developed in the scope of PT. Suntara Gajapati 
ownership by PT. Suntara Gajapati and Indacom, Pte, Ltd., and the PT. Suntara Gajapati (“Suntara”) 
and Indacom, Pte, Ltd. (“Indacom”) are the project proponent together. 
Prior to project implementation, land use activities in and around the project area included formal 
economic activities, such as acacia plantations and industrial oil palm plantations, and informal 
economic activities, such as smallholder oil palm plantations, rubber plantations, other crop 
cultivation, and residential settlements. Due to inadequate land management, the current approach 
has led to the gradual drainage of water in the Suntara peat project area, severely impacting the local 
environment. In 2024, the Suntara management team decided to revise the management approach, 
aiming to restore and protect the peatlands, while improving the hydrological conditions and 
enhancing the living and community environment in the project area. 
The project is expected to generate a range of climate, community and biodiversity benefits by 
protecting and restoring the integrity of peatland and forest landscapes and the rich biodiversity of the 
region, while improving the well-being of local communities. In terms of climate benefits, it is 
estimated that the PSPP project will achieve a net reduction and removal of 26,623,828 tCO
2
e over 
40 years of crediting period. This will be achieved through activities covered by the VM0007 REDD+ 
approach framework and VM0047 Afforestation, Reforestation, and Revegetation, including the 
restoration of wetland ecosystems of peatlands (RWE), Avoid unplanned deforestation (AUPD) and the 
restoration of vegetation (ARR). In addition, the project will provide climate change adaptation benefits 
by increasing the resilience of local communities and biodiversity to climate change. 
The project will bring tangible benefits to local communities living in the surrounding areas by working 
to improve local livelihoods, health, education and well-being. For example, the project will provide job 
opportunities for local villagers; support the establishment of women's groups in target villages to 
promote economic opportunities for women; and implement interventions to improve health and 
sanitation in target villages. 
The project's goals are to protect remaining forests, improve peatland hydrology, restore ecosystem 
functions, conserve biodiversity, and improve community well-being to withstand climate change. 
Overall, it is expected that the PSPP project will help protect the integrity and biodiversity of the 
region's peat forest landscapes while improving the well-being of local communities.

2.1.4 Project Eligibility (VCS, 3.1, 3.6, 3.8, 3.18, 4.1; CCB Program Rules, 4.2.4, 4.6.4) 
1. The Project conforms to the general and AFOLU specific requirements of section 3.1, 3.2 3.6, 3.18 
and 4.1 and is not excluded under Table 1 of the VCS Standard, version 4.7. 
2. The PSPP activities covered by the VM0007 V1.8 and VM0047 V1.0, including the restoration of 
wetland ecosystems of peatlands (RWE), Avoid unplanned deforestation (AUPD) and the 
restoration of vegetation (ARR), A detailed explanation of the choice of methodology and baseline 
scenario can be found in Section 3.1.2. 
3. This project implementation does not lead to the violation of any applicable law (Section 2.5) 
4. The Baseline Scenario conforms to the methodological requirements of the VCS Standard, version 
4.7. 
5. The lifetime of the Project is 60 years as permitted by section 3.9.3 of the VCS Standard,  
version 4.7. 
6. The Project will be validated within the five-year requirement as required in section 3.11.4 
VCS Standard, version 4.7. 
7. The Project has implemented the current and valid, approved, and up-to-date methodology  
to calculate its greenhouse gas emissions (GHG) reductions in a conservative manner as  
required by sections 3.1.2 and 3.1.3 of the VCS Standard, version 4.7. 
8. The Project has established legally mandated riparian zones adjacent to primary Peat Dome 
Forest in and around rivers, streams, and other significant water bodies within the Project Area. 
9. The Project has demonstrated its additionality requirements as set forth in section 3.14 of  
the VCS Standard, version 4.7.

2.1.9 Project Ownership (VCS, 3.2, 3.7, 3.10; CCB, G5.8) 
PT Suntara Gajapati, the proponent of the PSPP project, has the land tenure of the PSPP and the 
entire project area is officially recognized and zoned as a production forest area (Hutan Produksi or 
HP) nationwide. INDACOM, PTE, LTD, another proponent of the PSPP project, participated in the 
investment and development of the project as the investors of the project. The project is jointly owned 
by PT Suntara Gajapati and INDACOM, PTE, LTD. The project is jointly supervised and implemented by 
the above two parties during the development and maintenance process. 
2.1.10 Project Start Date (VCS, 3.8) 
Project start date  1-January-2024 
Justification  
Prior to the start of the project, Suntara was already conducting 
peatland and forest conservation activities and water management. 
However, due to the large size of the project area and the continued 
expansion of illegal smallholder palm plantations in Suntara as local 
residents pursue livelihood activities such as growing oil palm, the 
forests in the project area are still under threat of destruction by 
illegal logging. Therefore, Suntara’s leaders decided to increase 
protection efforts to better protect the Suntara project. The project 
start date corresponds to the date of the agreement to convert the 
project from the planned baseline to a conservation and restoration 
project, which was signed in the fourth quarter of 2023. Although 
Suntara has already carried out some work before the project starts, 
we conservatively define the project start date as January 1, 2024, 
to align with calendar year accounting. This start date is consistent 
with VCS standard points 3.8.2 and 3.8.4, as the project will seek 
verification in 2025.

There is no difference between the GHG emissions accounting and the community, and/or biodiversity 
assessment and periods.

2.1.14 Physical Parameters (CCB, G1.3) 
The Pekanbaru Suntara Peatland Project (PSPP) is located in the Negeri Sembilan River Subdistrict, 
Dumai Municipality, Riau Province, Indonesia. The project area was delineated by the Riau Forestry 
Department and covers an area of 35,644.39 hectares with a boundary length of 101,453.86 meters. 
The project area boundary delineates the area where quantified greenhouse gas emission reductions 
are to be achieved. The project area is located within the following coordinates: 01°49'31"N–
02°05'29" N and 101°03'46" E–101°18'04" E. 
⚫ Topography  
The project area is located in the S. Buluhala - Senepis - Santahulu forest group, which borders the 
Malacca Strait to the west.  The project area is a wetland area (peat land) so that this area has a 
relatively flat topography. Throughout the project area, the elevation is approximately 2-8 meters 
above sea level, which is in the less than 100 meters above sea level category. Within the project 
area, 100% of the area has a slope of less than 8° and is flat (0 - 8°). See Map 2-1 ＆Map 2-2. 
 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
17 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
 
Map 2-1 PT. Suntara Gajapati Slope Map 
 
 
Map 2-2 PT. Suntara Gajapati Elevation Map 
 
⚫ Soil 
Peat soils are formed by the slow composting of organic matter such as plant residues over a long 
period of time. According to the soil classification system of the Food and Agriculture Organization of 
the United Nations (FAO), peat soils are called Histosol soils. There are three levels of peat maturity: 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
18 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
fibrous (immature), semi-mature (medium maturity) and humus (mature). Peat soils are usually 
flooded throughout the year unless they are artificially altered and drained due to the construction of 
canals to develop industrial forest plantations or as irrigation systems. Naturally, peat soils are located 
at the uppermost layer, with mineral soils or alluvial soils below. The peat dome stores water and 
releases it back to the watershed at a slow rate. Therefore, during the dry season, a large amount of 
groundwater is discharged at a slow rate, ensuring that the peat soils remain flooded until the next 
rainy season. 
Peatlands are the most common soil type in the project area, accounting for 81.13% of the total land 
area, and the remaining 18.87% are mineral soils. The common soil type in peat areas is organic soil 
with low acidity (pH). The average pH value of the soil in PT. Suntara Gajapati is 3.57 ± 0.12 (actual 
pH value) and 2.60 ± 0.35 (potential pH value). Through field collection of peat soil samples and 
laboratory testing, the average bulk density of peat in the project area is 0.09g/cm
3
, the average 
organic carbon content is 53.34%, and the average water content is 34.03%. 
⚫ Climate  
According to the climate classification of Schmid and Ferguson, Riau belongs to Type A climate, which 
is characterized by more rainy seasons than dry seasons, evenly distributed rainfall in various regions, 
and generally dry periods from February to March each year. The overall rainfall is affected by El Nino 
and La Nina weather. In El Nino years, the temperature rises significantly, and it is dry and rainy. The 
rainfall data from 2021 to 2024 show that the average rainfall in 2024 is the highest (188.4 mm on 
average), followed by 2023, with an average of 173.6 mm per month. September is the month with 
the most rainfall in the past four years, with an average of 246.9 mm. See Table 2-2 for detailed data 
and rainfall charts around the company.

2.1.15 Social Parameters (VCS, 3.18; CCB, G1.3) 
⚫ Main settlements  
There are two sub-district governments in the project area, namely: Pasir Nang Baru Sub-district and 
Batu Driti Sub-district in Negeri Sembilan. Residents of these two sub-districts individually or 
collectively control agricultural land and agricultural production land, mainly plantations. Therefore, 
parts of the project area have been transformed from forest areas to residential areas, agricultural 
areas and plantations. The residents in the area are mainly Malay tribesmen who live along most 
rivers, as well as some immigrants. 
In terms of administrative areas, most of the residents living and cultivating land in the PT Suntara 
Gajapati area are distributed in three villages, namely Sungai Geniot, Batu Teritip and Basilam Baru. 
According to the results of the community survey in 2024, the number of households closely related to 
the project area is 2,056. The specific distribution data is shown in the following table:

⚫ Land use and economic activities  
The purpose/utilization of the land in the area is varied, namely: a) Settlements; b) Social facilities; c) 
Public facilities; d) Government offices; e) Oil palm plantations; f) Rubber plantations; g) Other 
plantations and crops. Based on the current situation, people in the area are usually growers of oil 
palm plantations. In addition, they are workers of surrounding companies. Sungai Negeri Sembilan is 
the largest agricultural area in Dumai after the expansion of the area from Bukit Japur in 2001. There 
is also the use of timber in the project area, such as the Acacia Industrial Forest of the Sinar Mas 
Group. In addition to this, there is currently a small number of residents in the surrounding 
communities who use the land to grow agricultural products, beekeeping for honey, and cultivating 
bird's nests. 
⚫ Relevant historic conditions 
Past and current land use activities in and around the project area include formal and informal 
economic activities including acacia plantations, industrial oil palm plantations, smallholder oil palm 
plantations, rubber plantations, other crop cultivation and residential areas. 
⚫ Socio-cultural information  
The most common informal groups in PT Suntara Gajapati and its surrounding areas are religious 
groups or recitation groups. Meanwhile, the groups that gather regularly are formal RT/RW groups or 
small villages, whose members usually live nearby. In addition, there are some formal social groups or 
organizations such as cooperatives, farmers' groups, mothers' groups or PKK groups, which are also 
active in village life. The main religion in the villages in the area and its surrounding areas is Islam, 
which makes the old religious traditions such as the tradition and sanctity of objects or areas no 
longer performed. However, there are still some residents who still believe in this and still care about 
and respect the holy places. The language of instruction used in Riau is usually Indonesian. For the 
people in Dumai itself, they usually use a mixture of Indonesian and Malay with a rather strong accent. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
22 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
In addition, the widely used language is Javanese, especially among immigrants and migrants from 
Java. In terms of customary use of natural resources, there is no norm for customary activities carried 
out by the community related to existing natural resources. In these three villages, namely Tanjung 
Penyembal, Basilam Baru, Batu Barnativ villages, there is no longer a culture or custom of using 
natural resources. The people living and settling in the Dumai area are immigrant communities from 
Kalimantan, Sulawesi and Java, including the Banjar, Bugis, Javanese and Sundanese tribes, who 
have lived and settled in the Dumai area for a long time and therefore no longer find customs in the 
management and use of natural resources. 
2.1.16 Project Zone Map and Project Location (VCS, 3.11, 3.18; CCB, G1.4-7, G1.13, 
CM1.2, B1.2) 
The project area is located in the eastern part of Sumatra Island. The project area is located in the 
Sungai Negeri Sembilan District area of Dumai City, 188 kilometers away from Pekanbaru, the capital 
of Riau Province (see Map2-3 below). The project area is located to the northwest of the Sungai 
Buluhala River. The project area is delineated by the Riau Provincial Forestry Bureau, covering an area 
of 35,644.39 hectares and a boundary length of 101,453.86 meters. The project area lies within the 
following coordinates: 01°49'31"N – 02°05'29" N and 101°03'46" E – 101°18'04" E. 
The project area includes established production forests (HP) within the PSPP, as well as protected 
areas located on the northwest boundary of the PSPP.  The project area will cover all settlement areas 
associated with Batu Teritip, Bsilam Baru and Tanjung Penyebal villages as these villages will be the 
beneficiaries of community development activities.

2.1.17 Project Activities and Theory of Change (VCS, 3.6; CCB, G1.8) 
The goal of the PSPP is to restore a large peatland ecosystem that is currently in a largely degraded 
state due to historical impacts of palm cultivation, logging, canal construction, and fire. Project 
activities are designed to maximize the restoration of the ecological function of the project area, and 
the wider peat dome in which it exists, while building strong supportive relationships with a wide range 
of stakeholders within the landscape.  
The project activities cover six core areas and are described in detail below: 
2.1.17.1 Forest patrolling 
Patrolling activities aim to reduce or eliminate risks to the project site that cause ecosystem 
degradation, including illegal logging, poaching, exploitative fishing, and further conversion for small-
scale agriculture. In addition to the potential removal of flora and fauna from the site, these activities 
create heightened fire risks and keep canals open and active. 
Patrolling activities include: 
• Physical demarcation of the project area; 
• Stakeholder engagement to educate on the project area boundary and permissible activities 
within and in the near vicinity of the project area; 
• Establishment of a series of guard posts around the perimeter of the project area and on the main 
roads into the reserve, strategically located at known key entry points;  
• Establishment of a radio communication system for effective monitoring and reporting on 
potential risks (including fire – see below); 
• Regular foot, bicycle, boat and vehicle patrolling along the perimeter and within the project area. 
Patrol teams consist primarily of community members from the two nearby villages. 
• Data collection and analysis on identified threats (i.e. evidence of illegal logging, encounters with 
poachers or animal traps, illegal entries to fish, etc.) to prioritize patrolling activities; 
• Regular drone monitoring across the project area provides additional information on potential 
risks in harder to access areas of the project site; and 
• Engagement with forest police and local law enforcement to prevent forest crimes. 
Limiting illegal incursions not only prevents the extraction of valuable flora and fauna, but also greatly 
reduces the risk of potential fire vectors that are known to have major negative impacts on peatland 
ecosystem restoration from entering the project area. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
24 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
 
Map 2-6 Forest patrol route map 
2.1.17.2 Fire prevention and monitoring 
Historically, fire has had the largest impact on the integrity and health of the PSPP area. Fires can 
either initiate within the project area or spread from neighboring concessions, which occurred in 
2015. Fires pose an especially dangerous risk to peatlands, typically during dry spells (i.e. in an El 
Niño year) and if water levels have been artificially lowered from canal construction. Peatland fires can 
spread quickly and burn for long periods of time underground. Therefore, maintaining fire 
preparedness and dispatching rapid response teams are critical means of avoiding fires and 
extinguishing them as quickly as possible.  
Fire prevention and monitoring activities include: 
• Patrol activities to prevent the access of fire vectors into the project area (see activity 1 above); 
• Establishment of fire towers to monitor surrounding fires in real time and quickly notify the project 
management team of potential fires; 
• Establishment of Fire Response and Suppression (FRS) teams. These are the field teams based at 
the project site who can respond in case of a fire alert. All members of the field teams, including 
forest patrol team members, biodiversity staff and the project site manager, have been 
professionally trained in peatland firefighting by the fire brigade of the Indonesian Ministry of 
Forestry. The training documents a Standard Operation Procedure (SOP) for handling fire, outlining 
communication logistics to inform the all-keys stakeholders in surrounding concessions and 
communities. PSPP teams are also fully equipped with the necessary firefighting equipment, such 
as personal protective clothing, pumps, hoses, and other safety gear; 
• Stakeholder engagement to train local individuals in the surrounding residents on best practices 
for avoiding and preventing fires, and information on how to signal help when fires arise. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
25 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
Fire prevention and monitoring activities contribute to the project objectives by greatly limiting GHG 
emissions due to peat fires, allowing natural forest ecosystems to recover and provide extended 
habitats for key biodiversity species, and limiting the negative health and livelihood impacts of 
excessive haze. 
 
Map2-7 Fire tower location, fire protection equipment and on-site work map 
2.1.17.3 Canal blocking and hydrology management 
The presence of canals in the project site has significantly contributed to distribution of the peatland. 
The extensive peatland drainage in surrounding areas due to logging and commodities production has 
further exacerbated drying throughout the landscape. Restoring the hydrological integrity of the project 
area is therefore of critical importance to its long-term recovery, as well as preventing ongoing GHG 
emissions from peat microbial decomposition and peatland fires.  
This includes the rewetting of partially drained peatlands in and around areas with existing canals, 
and the conservation of undrained and partially drained peatlands (CUPP) in those areas further away 
from the existing canal network. Existing canals are principally small logging canals (shallower than 1 
meter and narrower than 2 meters), with only a few medium sized canals (wider than 2 meters) 
present. 
Canal blocking activities include: 
• Conducting a technical assessment on the most feasible canal block strategy taking into 
consideration access needs, hydrological flows, priority restoration areas, etc. Careful attention 
will be placed on the need to avoid ecological leakage; 
• Development of a proposed canal block plan; 
• Installation of different types of canal closures depending on canal size, water flow, and terrain 
slope. Canal plugs will be constructed in a cascade to gradually raise the water level in the 
drainage area and limit the pressure exerted by the water flow on any one canal plug. Canal plugs 
will be constructed primarily using natural materials available on site (dead wood and peat). Plugs 
will be constructed using gates at different plug points; 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
26 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
• Ongoing monitoring of water table levels and canal block conditions. In combination with an 
iteratively refined hydrological model, ongoing monitoring data will allow for continued 
optimization of water table levels throughout the project area. Regular checks on canal block 
conditions will ensure maintenance can be administered quickly should repair be required; 
• Collaboration with neighboring concessions on hydrological management. The PSPP has and 
continues to actively engage with neighboring concessions to promote improved hydrological 
management, both for the PSPP area and the neighboring concessions. 
The canal blocking activities are essential in supporting the PSPP area to return to its natural 
ecological condition. Raised water table levels reduce the risk of fire, prevent microbial decomposition 
and peat subsidence, and provide the necessary hydrological conditions to allow for the successive 
regeneration of forests. 
 
Map 2-8 Different canal blocking methods and hydrological management 
2.1.17.4 Community development 
PSPP works to support community development activities in villages within project sites to 
demonstrate how large-scale land use projects can play a positive role in the wider landscape they 
operate in. In addition, building strong supportive relationships with local communities can prevent 
the ecological functions restored by the project from attracting illegal activities and creating risks 
associated with future encroachment. 
Key community development activities include: 
• Building wells + water towers + water treatment facilities to provide local residents with sufficient 
and clean domestic water 
• Providing allowances and student scholarships for local village school teachers 
• Providing community fire prevention equipment and budget 
• Providing forestry skills training 
• Providing jobs related to forest protection and restoration 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
27 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
• Providing jobs related to hydrological management 
• Providing jobs related to forest patrol and fire prevention 
• Providing cattle and sheep to residents to promote animal husbandry 
• Helping residents develop non-forest products 
• Designating unified agricultural planting areas in the project area and sharing economic benefits 
with community residents 
• Restoring the sanitary environment in the disaster area and providing food assistance to residents 
• Providing financial assistance for sports activities for community residents 
• Providing financial assistance for the construction of places of worship 
• Sponsoring religious festivals 
• Social assistance during religious festivals 
• Maintaining roads, cleaning bridges and ditches 
• Funding assistance for government office equipment 
• Cleaning rivers to prevent floods; 
Community investments are designed to be an integral part of PSPP’s operating expenses. The project 
aims to deliver tangible benefits to surrounding communities by through community-based business 
development programs and education initiatives. The project will continue to allocate a significant 
portion of its operating budget for community development throughout its long-term operations. 
 
Map 2-9 Community development activity site photo 
2.1.17.5 Biodiversity Management and Monitoring  
The project foresees an increase in the number and variety of biodiversity through the implementation 
of the above-mentioned patrolling, extinguishing and blocking canals. This will be due to the reduction 
of threats and the restoration of natural habitats. As a result, biodiversity-specific activities are limited 
to the current stage of the monitoring plan. The monitoring plan will track potential fluctuations in 
specific species and inform management activities across the PSPP area. This project aims to 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
28 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
dedicate significant resources to detecting, identifying, and understanding the characteristics, 
distribution, sensitivities, and vulnerabilities of HCV. 
 
Map 2-10 Plant monitoring work photos 
2.1.17.6 Cross-cutting  
This final group of direct interventions does not directly address specific threats. However, this cross-
cutting allows for the overall enabling conditions that support the effectiveness and sustainability of 
the other direct project interventions.   
Key cross-cutting interventions include:  
• Implementation of adaptive management: The effectiveness of project interventions will be 
periodically reviewed to determine if changes are necessary in the location, intensity, approach, 
capacity needs, and resourcing of activities. Evaluations will be primarily conducted by the Project 
Management Board and key technical staff; 
• Implementation of effective administration and human resources protocols: The backbone of any 
organization or project is an effective administrative system and qualified, well-trained staff to 
implement project activities. These systems and protocols will be continuously reviewed and 
revised to ensure effective project management and logistical planning; 
• Robust financial management: This includes the implementation of accounting systems, 
development of clear financial management SOPs and elaboration of annual budgets. Additionally, 
it requires the marketing and sale of VCUs to ensure the ongoing financial flows required to 
sustain the long-term sustainability of the project; 
• Ensuring legal compliance: To retain the legal rights associated with the PAN/RAP license, the 
project will need to abide by a number of reporting requirements at various levels of government, 
conduct various forest carbon management activities including monitoring, evaluating, and 
reporting, pay taxes, prepare work plans and performance reports for the Ministry of Forestry, and 
ensure ongoing compliance with all other requirements to maintain its active license status.  

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template

CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
2.1.18 Sustainable Development Contributions (VCS, 3.17) 
The project is aligned with the 2030 Agenda for Sustainable Development and aims to promote 
resilient and inclusive growth. Key activities include ending poverty, strengthening healthcare, 
improving education, supporting gender equality, ensuring clean water and sanitation, and providing 
jobs to boost local economic growth. The project also focuses on reducing inequalities, climate action, 
biodiversity conservation, and maintaining peace and justice. The Project aims to support the 
achievement of SDG as outlined in the Table 2-5 below.

healthy lifestyles and the right to access quality and 
reliable health services, especially among the most 
vulnerable groups such as women and children.

increasing pressure from palm oil production 
expansion, timber extraction and other socio-economic 
factors that have negatively impacted the natural

accomplished through the Avoiding deforestation and

conserving forests, natural peatland, and restoring

This will contribute to Indonesia’s SDG target to reduce 
deforestation and land-use diversion. 
 
SDG 15: Life on  
Land 
The Project aims to increase the abundance,  
diversity, and critical habitat of endangered and 
endemic wildlife by protecting natural forest and 
peatland, as well as restoring degraded peatland and 
revegetation of bare land.  
This will contribute to Indonesia’s SDG target to 
prevent a higher rate of forest degradation to ensure 
the survival of civilization in the future.  
The project will work with local communities in  
and around the Project area, thus increasing local 
participation in forest management and  
incorporating private and public participation in 
conservation programs. This will contribute to a more 
effective protection for Indonesia’s forests cover. 
 
SDG 16: Peace, Justice and 
Strong Institutions 
The PSPP project proponents firmly resist all forms of 
violent activities and provide fair and transparent 
channels for local community residents to reflect their 
opinions during the project by establishing a complaint 
and redress mechanism. Once discrimination, violence 
and illegal activities occur, the project party will stop 
them in accordance with laws and regulations and 
report them to government departments. 
2.1.19 Implementation Schedule (CCB, G1.9) 
Prior to the start of the project, Suntara was already conducting peatland and forest conservation 
activities and water management. However, due to the large size of the project area and the continued 
expansion of illegal smallholder palm plantations in Suntara as local residents pursue livelihood 
activities such as growing oil palm, the forests in the project area are still under threat of destruction by 
illegal logging. Therefore, Suntara’s leaders decided to increase protection efforts to better protect the 
Suntara project. The project start date corresponds to the date of the agreement to convert the project 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template

CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
from the planned baseline to a conservation and restoration project, which was signed in the fourth 
quarter of 2023. Although Suntara has already carried out some work before the project starts, we 
conservatively define the project start date as January 1, 2024, to align with calendar year accounting. 
This start date is consistent with VCS standard points 3.8.2 and 3.8.4, as the project will seek 
verification in 2025.

2.1.21 Benefit Permanence (CCB, G1.11) 
The project’s community development activities are designed to increase the quality of life of local 
communities and community groups. They are designed to build the capacity of the local communities 
in the long term and benefits are expected to endure beyond the lifetime of the project. The project will 
support alternative livelihoods by providing training and initial seed capital through a revolving fund, 
and by enhancing market access. It will also implement activities to improve health and sanitation and 
will improve access to higher education for local youth. These community development activities are 
outlined in more detail in Section 2.1.17. 
Regarding maintaining the project’s climate benefits, the project will rewet the peatlands by building 
canal blocks, which is a recognized method of restoring the hydrology of this degraded area. This will 
lower the risk of fire in the landscape and enable the restoration of natural vegetation over the course 
of the project. In addition, the project will increase forest cover in the region by protecting the 
remaining forests and planting trees. The restoration and protection of peat forests will increase the 
resilience of the ecosystem to future climate impacts. In addition, the project will help build the long-
term resilience of local communities. This activity is explained in more detail in Section 2.1.17.  
In terms of biodiversity benefits, the project will carry out forest protection patrols and biodiversity 
monitoring. Protecting the remaining forests from being cut down and converted to oil palm 
plantations will benefit the long-term survival of key species populations. At the same time, adaptive 
management will be adopted and local communities will be closely involved to ensure long-term 
benefits for biodiversity. Section 2.1.17 describes the project activities in more detail. 
2.1.22 Financial Sustainability (CCB, G1.12) 
The project sponsor is a visionary carbon sequestration company led by an experienced lead and 
supported by long-term partners and investors. The project sponsor has demonstrated its commitment 
to the project by developing a comprehensive long-term plan covering conservation, restoration and 
community development. The company’s financial forecasts are classified as commercially sensitive 
information and will be shared with the audit team in a confidential manner. 
2.2 Without-project Land Use Scenario and Additionality  
2.2.1 Conditions Prior to Project Initiation and Land Use Scenarios without the Project 
(VCS, 3.13; CCB, G2.1) 
Prior to the commencement of the project, the condition of the project area could be described as a 
highly degraded peatland area due to severe fires in 2015, the planting of acacia trees on part of the 
project site leased by Sinar Mas Group, and illegal logging activities by residents of nearby villages to 
plant palm trees. The condition of the project site was not a result of actions by the project or related 
parties to purposely create GHG emission potential for subsequent reduction.  

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
35 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
It is demonstrated that the project has not been implemented to generate GHG emissions for the 
purpose of their subsequent reduction, removal or destruction in Section 3.1.4 and the project’s 
baseline scenario and additionality is justified by using the VT0001 "Tool for the demonstration and 
assessment of additionality in VCS Agriculture, Forestry and Other Land Use (AFOLU) project 
activities". Version 3.0.  
As mentioned in Section 3.1.4, the most likely land use in the absence of the Project is to establish 
industrial acacia plantations and palm plantations. Part of the land within the Project site has been 
leased to Sinar Mas Group for acacia plantation activities, and the 2015 fire proved that the Project 
site is at risk of fire, which may be beneficial to the Acacia Plantation Company because it removes a 
large amount of above-ground biomass that the plantation company would otherwise have to remove 
mechanically. At the same time, most of the residents around the Project site make a living by picking 
palm fruits, and a large area of forest has been cut down by villagers to plant palm trees. Therefore, if 
there is not this project, the PSPP area will remain as an acacia and palm plantation area after 2024. 
2.2.2 Most-Likely Scenario Justification (CCB, G2.1) 
Justification of the most likely land-use scenario in the baseline is provided in Section 3.1.4. 
2.2.3 Community and Biodiversity Additionality (CCB, G2.2) 
Benefits to the climate, communities and biodiversity would be negatively impacted in the absence of 
the project. The most reliable baseline for the project is the continued development of industrial 
acacia plantations and palm cultivation, as there are no financial, technical, institutional or capacity 
barriers. 
In terms of biodiversity, the continued activities of industrial acacia plantations and deforestation by 
nearby villagers for palm plantations will lead to a continued reduction in the area of the project site, 
severe soil disturbance, and a continued decline in groundwater levels in peatlands, thereby reducing 
the habitat area and connectivity of the project site's organisms. 
The project will address the threat of high human disturbance (such as canals, logging, poaching) and 
recurring fires in the project area by carrying out canal blocking work in the acacia plantation area of 
the Sinar Mas Group, stopping illegal logging and palm planting by local villagers in the protected area, 
and carrying out forest patrols and fire prevention measures, thereby protecting the rich biodiversity of 
the area and improving habitat quality. 
In terms of community development, PSPP will have a more comprehensive community development 
program. The benefits of the project to the community include improved access to health service 
improvement programs through village health services, educational opportunities for children, adults 
in local villages through seed funds, livelihood improvement opportunities for local villagers, especially 
low-income people, through job provision, increased access to clean water through village water 
supply programs (i.e., drilling of wells), and improved hydrological systems to prevent fires and floods 
in residential areas (see Section 4.5). 
In terms of climate, the baseline scenario will emit millions of tons of carbon dioxide, further 
contributing to climate change. Compared to the baseline scenario outlined in Section 2.1.13, the 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
36 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
PSPP prevents carbon dioxide emissions through the implementation of project activities such as 
canal blocking, prevention of deforestation, and forest fire prevention. 
The no-project scenarios for communities and biodiversity are described in more detail in Sections 
4.1.4 and 5.1.3, respectively. 
2.2.4 Benefits to be used as Offsets (CCB, G2.2) 
The project does not intend to generate any community or biodiversity benefits to be used as offsets. 
2.3 Safeguards and Stakeholder Engagement   
2.3.1 Stakeholder Identification (VCS, 3.18, 3.19; CCB G1.5) 
Stakeholders identified for the project include the project’s communities and other stakeholders. The 
project’s communities are defined as the communities in the villages of the project zone; other 
stakeholders are defined as the persons or institutions or groups of communities that impact or 
influence the project activities.  
The identification of stakeholders was based on the following criteria:  
1. Economic activities: communities that implement or intend to implement economic activities 
related to the use of land in the project area, including agriculture and fisheries.  
2. Geographic location: communities that live in the surroundings of the project area.   
3. Use of natural resources within the project area and customary use rights: communities who  
depend on forest-related activities, such as fishermen looking for fish in canals located in the project 
site or other extractive livelihoods such as forest honey or rattan seekers.  
4. Other relevant stakeholders with interest in or influence over the project.  
The identification process was conducted through participatory meetings and interviews with key 
contact persons in the villages, such as heads of village and local public figures. The stakeholder 
analysis was conducted through internal discussion and confirmation with key contact persons. The 
aim of the stakeholder identification and analysis was to identify the key stakeholders in the villages, 
including in relation to the decision-making process. A detailed stakeholder list and description is 
provided in the following section.  
2.3.2 Stakeholder Descriptions (VCS, 3.18, 3.19; CCB, G1.6, G1.13) 
Identified stakeholders are classified into four main groups, namely "Community Members and Village 
Institutions" 、"Affected Livelihood Groups" 、"Businesses and Economic Stakeholders" and 
"Government and Regulatory Authorities" .The table 2-7 below shows the stakeholders. Stakeholders 
were identified as per the process outlined in Section 2.3.1.

2.3.3 Stakeholder Access to Project Documents (VCS, 3.18, 3.19; CCB, G3.1) 
To provide stakeholders with access to project documents, the PSPP Field Office will maintain a full, 
printed version of the project description (PD) document in English for public viewing. The executive 
summary will be translated into Bahasa Indonesia and will be given to community leaders to make 
available in public places in the surrounding villages. In addition, PSPP has established a WhatsApp 
group composed of community leaders and stakeholder representatives directly involved in the project 
as another means of obtaining project information. Through this WhatsApp group channel, 
stakeholders can directly access PSPP for any clarifications or inquiries about the project. At the same 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
39 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
time, there is a person in charge who is familiar with the local situation at the project site. Local 
community residents can give him feedback on any suggestions for the project, and the project 
implementer will give a reply within 15 days. 
2.3.4 Dissemination of Summary Project Documents (VCS, 3.18, 3.19; CCB, G3.1) 
Summary Project Documents can be obtained as follows: 
1. Office access: The PSPP field office will provide a complete English version of the project 
description (PD) for public access. 
2. Online disclosure: The project summary will be translated into Indonesian and handed over to 
community leaders and made public in public places in the village; the full project description and its 
summary (English and Indonesian) will be posted on PSPP's social media or official website. 
3. WhatsApp communication: PSPP has established a WhatsApp group composed of community 
leaders and stakeholders for communication and consultation of project information. 
4. Personal feedback: Appoint a person familiar with the local situation, and residents can directly give 
feedback or suggestions to him, and the project party will respond within 15 days.  
2.3.5 Informational Meetings with Stakeholders (VCS, 3.18, 3.19; CCB, G3.1) 
The project conducted social surveys in three villages within the project area during the project 
development phase and held information meetings with stakeholders. First, a social survey was 
conducted in the form of questionnaires and interviews in three villages: Kelurahan Sungai Geniot, 
Kelurahan Batu Teritip and Kelurahan Basilam Baru from December 26, 2024, to February 11, 2025. 
The survey included: the population, economy, public facilities, water sources, historical religious 
culture, traditional customs and attitudes towards the project of all local villagers. After analyzing the 
collected information, it was determined that these three villages have an important relationship with 
the project area and need to be involved during the project life cycle. 
After that, residents of the three villages in the project area were invited to conduct FGDS focus group 
discussion meetings in the lobby of Batu Teritip Village Office and Sungai Geniot Village Office on April 
16 and April 18, 2025, respectively. During the meeting, the community team briefed them on the 
project and the progress of implementation so far. By recording the content discussed in the meeting, 
the attitudes and suggestions of local residents on the project were collected again, and the project 
plan was adjusted according to the suggestions of project stakeholders. The purpose of these 
meetings is to build trust, build relationships with project stakeholders, and further work to 
understand the situation of the local community and potential community concerns. Stakeholders 
were notified by letter or phone at least three days before the meeting. 
This participation was extensive, and in addition to formal discussions, many meetings were informal 
conversations without specific written agendas. We have been consulting with local communities and 
stakeholders to ensure consensus on all project activities and potential impacts. Stakeholders were 
consulted on the development of the community development plan. During a series of meetings, the 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
40 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
community requested support for improving education in nearby villages, supporting existing health 
facilities, and supporting them to improve their livelihoods. Therefore, these procedures have been 
included in the design of the program. 
In order to provide village participants with the opportunity to consult and clarify project activities, 
collect more information about community stakeholder activities, monitor progress and discuss the 
next steps, the follow-up community team will continue to expand the scope of community contact, 
and one week before each village-wide consultation activity, posters promoting the activities will be 
hung in the public area, and the village coordinator and the community engagement team will make 
announcements. The community engagement team also contacted relevant stakeholders through 
WhatsApp or SMS to ensure that the meeting involved all relevant people. The village coordinator will 
pay special attention to ensure that more marginalized households are notified first during the 
consultation activities. 
We will continue to facilitate these meetings throughout the project. We recognize that the feedback 
and suggestions provided by these parties are crucial to the long-term success of the project, so all 
information collected in these meetings will be taken into consideration. Furthermore, this feedback is 
a valuable opportunity for us to enhance and adjust various aspects of the project design and 
implementation based on guidance from relevant identified stakeholders.

2.3.7 Community Costs, Risks, and Benefits (CCB, G3.2) 
One of the initial activities undertaken by the project was to network and explain to surrounding 
communities the potential impacts of the project’s ecosystem restoration activities, including the 
project’s costs, risks, and benefits. From the FPIC process, the project’s potential negative impacts on 
communities are related to local community rights, livelihoods, and culture, such as restrictions on 
traditional land and resource rights, increased concentration of forest management, and fairness in 
benefit sharing. The project has provided communities with initial information on the project’s costs, 
risks, and benefits, which are reflected in the FPIC report. As FPIC is an iterative and ongoing process, 
the project anticipates that there will be some adjustments to community benefits as community 
expectations of the project evolve over the project’s lifecycle. So far, the project has learned that 
communities expect benefits in terms of livelihoods, education, and health (see Section 4.2.1). The 
project will maintain active community engagement as a prerequisite for achieving community 
benefits and will regularly discuss expectations of the carbon project with communities. More detailed 
and explicit benefits will be defined in the project throughout the project’s lifecycle. However, so far, 
communities have understood and agreed to the potential costs, risks, and benefits they will receive 
as a result of the project. 
2.3.8 Information to Stakeholders on Validation and Verification Process (VCS, 3.18.6, 
3.19; CCB, G3.3) 
PSPP shared a summary of the CCB concept using easy to understand terms so that the community 
and other stakeholders understand the CCB concept, definitions, templates and necessary data. PSPP 
has presented the CCB verification and validation process at several community meetings and 
community resident surveys. In addition, PSPP will continue to communicate the verification and 
validation process through various methods, especially through regular meetings and broadcasts 
through WhatsApp groups to ensure that the community has full understanding of the verification and 
validation process, including VVB field visits. 
2.3.9 Site Visit Information and Opportunities to Communicate with Auditor (VCS, 
3.18.6; CCB, G3.3) 
PSPP staff will meet frequently with the identified project communities and residents of nearby 
villages and settlements. To prepare for the audit, project staff will inform relevant stakeholders one 
month in advance of the details of the audit process and allow ample time for local groups to 
communicate with the auditors during the auditors’ site visits. PSPP will propose an appropriate 
timeline to ensure full and direct discussions between stakeholders and auditors, and the project will 
schedule meetings with all relevant stakeholders during the validation and verification site visits, and 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template

2.3.10 Stakeholder Consultations (VCS, 3.18; CCB, G3.4) 
Date of stakeholder 
consultation 
26-December-2024 
Stakeholder engagement 
process  
Project consultations were held in all 3 villages within the project area. 
One week before each meeting, community representatives including 
women and young leaders were asked to select their representatives. 
The language used in these meetings was Indonesian. These 
consultation meetings were conducted as information workshops before 
entering into the formal consent process. During the workshops, the 
following information was presented and discussed: 
The first step was that the project team, after obtaining the necessary 
approval and opinions from the village committee, conducted door-to-
door visits and data collection through questionnaires and interviews in 
three villages: Kelurahan Sungai Geniot, Kelurahan Batu Teritip and 
Kelurahan Basilam Baru from December 26, 2024 to February 11, 
2025. During this stage, the research team not only visited the villagers' 
homes, but also went deep into the areas where these activities were 
carried out. In order to improve communication and build trust, we hired 
local census takers to conduct the survey. In addition, we distributed 
flyers in advance to inform the villagers of the survey schedule. 
The second step was to analyze the basic situation of the community 
and residents in the project area after the first phase of the survey and 
data collection work was completed, identify the stakeholders who may 
be affected and their attitudes towards the project, such as farmers, 
palm growers, etc. A preliminary assessment was also made on the 
possible economic, social, cultural and environmental impacts (including 
potential risks) and the fair and equitable distribution of benefits. 
The third step is that after obtaining the necessary approval and 
opinions from the village committee, the project team invited residents 
of the three villages in the project area to conduct FGDS focus group 
discussions at the lobby of Batu Teritip Village Office and Sungai Geniot 
Village Office on April 16 and April 18, 2025. Detailed communication 
was conducted with stakeholders during the meeting to fully understand 
the attitudes, suggestions and needs of local community residents 
towards the project. 
The fourth step is to determine the future activities of the project based 
on the above research results. This includes the implementation 
methods of project activities, the participation of local community 
residents, and the roles of company employees, research institutions, 
non-governmental organizations and government agencies. At the same 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
43 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
time, ensure that consensus is reached on all project activities and 
potential impacts. Stakeholders were consulted on the development of 
the community development plan. During a series of meetings, 
community residents requested support for improving education 
conditions, health facilities and livelihoods in nearby villages. Therefore, 
these requirements have been included in the design of the project. 
The comprehensive process ensured that the social baseline study was 
conducted in an ethical, respectful and effective manner, with active 
participation of the community at every stage. The results of the 
community survey and focus group discussions provided valuable 
insights to support future project planning and implementation in the 
villages concerned. 
Consultation outcome Community representatives who attended the consultation meetings had 
a deep understanding of the project objectives, project design, expected 
benefits, and potential risks to the community. Through open 
communication and active participation, the project team built strong 
relationships with the community, fostering trust and mutual 
understanding. The process also enabled villagers to learn about the 
project informally, which increased their awareness and interest. The 
social baseline study provided us with a deeper understanding of the 
community’s socioeconomic conditions, challenges, and aspirations. 
This engagement process guided the design of our future stakeholder 
interactions, helping us identify stakeholders that may be affected, 
optimize the project area, and plan project activities. 
In addition, it provided valuable insights into the best approach to 
ongoing engagement, ensuring that the Free, Prior, and Informed 
Consent (FPIC) process with the community was conducted in an 
appropriate and respectful manner.

regarding the risks, costs, and benefits for the community arising from 
project implementation.  
No changes were made to the project design following these 
consultation meetings, as the project had already been co-designed with 
input from the community and other stakeholders.

2.3.11 Continued Consultation and Adaptive Management (VCS, 3.18; CCB, G3.4) 
Throughout the lifetime of the project, we will maintain a direct line of communication with community 
members, and relevant stakeholders. This will establish a commitment to communication and 
consultation to keep stakeholders informed of project activities including restoration, maintenance, 
monitoring and the CCB validation and verification process. PSPP staff in the field will maintain 
communications with the fishermen and farming community groups and other stakeholders through 
in-person meetings. We will actively listen to recommendations made by any identified community 
members, or other stakeholder groups, and adapt and improve our methods as necessary.  
The project will host a meeting and site visit with impacted fishermen to assess the impact of the 
dams on their ability to continue subsistence fishing and receive input on how to improve conditions 
for them. The project will look for solutions that meet the farmer’s needs and work to implement them. 
The results of these impact mitigation activities will be reviewed in the quarterly meetings. 
Our project has an adaptive management plan to effectively grow as our project progresses, and 
systematically develop existing practices through project monitoring and evaluation. We will 
periodically review our plans, methods, goals and objectives, to incorporate new lessons learned, 
available technology, and scientific knowledge. These strategies will be in accordance with our 
project’s Standard Operating Procedures (SOPs) and our monitoring plans. 
2.3.12 Stakeholder Consultation Channels (CCB, G3.5) 
This project has conducted several stakeholder engagement and consultation sessions with identified 
project communities and other stakeholders from nearby villages and settlements. As part of the 
consultation process, our project staff conducted a participatory survey using the Participatory Rural 
Appraisal (PRA) method. This included focus group discussions (FGDs), interviews with men and 
women living in nearby settlements and villages, and observations and ground checks with local 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
46 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
residents, including participatory visits to various key areas and community centers. See Section 2.3.5 
for more information on conducting information sessions with stakeholders and Section 2.3.4 for 
more information on project information dissemination. 
2.3.13 Stakeholder Participation in Decision-Making and Implementation (VCS, 3.18, 
3.19; CCB, G3.6) 
Local communities play a crucial role in the activity planning and implementation process, ensuring 
that project interventions are effective, culturally appropriate, and sustainable over the long term. 
Active participation from community members helps ensure that activities genuinely reflect local 
needs and priorities.  
Regular meetings will be systematically organized through coordination with village leaders, following 
established local customs and traditions. Village leaders will take primary responsibility for mobilizing 
community members, ensuring widespread dissemination of meeting schedules, and encouraging 
active and inclusive participation. Efforts will be made to ensure every community member has the 
opportunity to openly voice their opinions, concerns, and recommendations. 
The project proponent will proactively encourage women's groups to attend and actively participate in 
meetings by scheduling gatherings at convenient times and providing additional support such as 
childcare or transport assistance if necessary. 
Broad-based community engagement contributes significantly to the project's long-term success by 
continuously improving local livelihoods, fostering greater community cohesion, and building capacity 
for collective self-management. Moreover, active involvement from community members, including 
targeted groups such as women, increases project transparency, builds mutual trust, and ultimately 
accelerates inclusive community development. 
2.3.14 Anti-Discrimination Assurance (VCS 3.19; CCB, G3.7) 
The project operates under the constitution, laws and regulations of Indonesia. With regard to 
discrimination, the project will comply with the constitution and relevant laws, among others:  
• Constitution 1945 (UUD 1945) especially section 27  
• Law No. No. 40/2008 on the elimination of racial and ethnic discrimination  
• Law No. 1/2023 concerning the criminal law code  
In addition, the project will comply with any regulations on manpower and safety of workers, among 
others Law No. 1/1970 on the Occupational Safety (Occupational Safety Law). All employees will 
receive an induction on work safety, employment and be provided with an employee pocketbook when 
they join the company.  

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template

CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
The company will implement best practices in recruiting employees, including implementing a policy of 
zero tolerance for sexual harassment and no discrimination to gender, religion, ethnicity, and sexual 
orientation while taking into account job availability, educational background and workforce skills. 
To ensure compliance, the project has a grievance system in place that will provide all project 
employees, identified community members, and other stakeholders with a formal system in the event 
that any discriminatory actions or sexual harassment does occur, to ensure grievances can be 
addressed quickly and appropriately.   
2.3.15 Feedback and Grievance Redress Procedure (VCS, 3.18.4; CCB, G3.8) 
Development process The project’s grievance procedure includes grievance registration, 
verification, identification of possible solutions with the 
complainant, taking action to resolve grievances, reporting and 
monitoring the result. All grievances will be processed within a 
specific timeframe (generally within 15 working days), in a 
transparent and accountable manner.  
The complainant should submit the grievance verbally or in writing 
to the project office. After receiving the grievance, the project will 
response through formal letter to the complainant. Based on the 
grievance, the project team will develop a necessary action plan, 
implement, monitor, evaluate and communicate the action and 
result to the complainant. 
Grievance redress 
procedure 
There was an agreement between the project proponent and the 
community on a conflict resolution mechanism consisting of:  
1. Project proponent and community will conduct an investigation 
regarding the related dispute.  
2. Project proponent and community will discuss to reach 
deliberations and consensus.  
3. If deliberation and consensus do not produce a meeting point, 
then a third party will be appointed as a mediator agreed by both 
parties.  
4. If all efforts to reach a consensus have not been able to resolve 
the dispute, then both parties agree to bring it to the level of law in 
force in Indonesia.  
The grievance procedure lays out the ways in which complainants 
submit or deliver grievances, as well as the ways in which the 
project handles the grievances. Unless the complainant requests 
anonymity, the complainant should provide clear identity (name, 
address, ID number, phone) and description of the grievance as 
well as the background, expectation, and evidence. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
48 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
2.3.16 Accessibility of the Feedback and Grievance Redress Procedure (VCS, 3.19; 
CCB, G3.8) 
The feedback and grievance redress procedure will be communicated to the local communities 
through community meetings and village boards and will be accessible to all villagers. Complainants 
can submit grievances verbally or in writing to the project office. The grievances and project responses 
will be documented and reported every six months by the project team and, unless confidential, made 
available for stakeholders to view at the project office upon request.  
2.3.17 Worker Training (VCS, 3.19; CCB, G3.9) 
The PSPP aims to employ 90% of the field staff among residents of nearby villages and settlements, 
with an expected full-time and part-time workforce of 300. Of these, 40% are expected to be women. 
As part of the on-boarding process, the project provides all staff with an orientation program where it 
provides overviews of the project's Standard Operating Procedures (SOPs) and policies with specific 
attention to health and safety policies. The employees also receive training in the use of technology 
such as GPS units, computers, and firefighting equipment which will expand employee knowledge 
increasing their prospects of future employment. Local capacity is constantly being supported through 
training programs and recruitment drives for full-time and part time employment opportunities to 
ensure strong local capacity despite staff turnover.  
2.3.18 Community Employment Opportunities (VCS, 3.19.13; CCB, G3.10) 
The PSPP is an equal opportunity employer committed to working with identified community groups 
and local stakeholders on employment opportunities. To achieve this, the project has a policy that 
90% of its employees will be from the local area to promote community and stakeholder interests. The 
full-time staff is expected to be 90 people. In addition, the project aims to have 40% of its employees 
be women to ensure greater gender balance in a traditionally male-dominated field. To create 
opportunities for marginal groups, the project also has positions available for staff who have not 
obtained high school diplomas to ensure these vulnerable groups have access to meaningful 
employment opportunities. The project also works with staff to increase their education levels through 
continuing adult education programs.  
Job opportunities are publicly advertised in similar channels where other project news is publicized, 
such as community sign boards and announcements via community leaders. Meetings are convened 
within project stakeholder villages and settlements as part of the recruitment process in order to 
spread the word and attract candidates. Job applicants are selected for interviews based on their 
skills and experience required for the advertised positions. Successful candidates are then selected in 
accordance with the project’s equal opportunity policy. Applicants who live in the local villages and 
settlements are given preference in the case where two applicants show the same capacity for a given 
position whereas one is from the local area and the other is not.  
2.3.19 Occupational Safety Assessment (VCS, 3.19; CCB, G3.12) 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
49 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
In the proposed project activities, there are certain risks associated with the activities, which may 
have a negative impact on the health and safety of the participants, as the project is located in a peat 
forest ecosystem. Potential risks include: 
• Accidents due to improper operation of tools 
• Insect and spider bites 
• Snake bites 
• Wild animal attacks (bears, wild boars) 
• Exposure to mosquito-borne diseases 
• Falls, which may result in contusions, fractures, sprains, etc. 
• Capsizing of boats 
• Drowning 
• Exposure to fire and smoke during fire management activities 
• Getting lost during patrol activities 
• Performing strenuous activities in wet and hot conditions 
In order to reduce the risks associated with each activity, the project will hold meetings and trainings 
before the implementation of the activities, in addition to discussing technical issues, the risks and 
risk mitigation measures will be discussed. The measures proposed to minimize the risks are 
designed in a way that is consistent with the cultural practices of the communities, so that these 
safety measures are easily accepted and followed by the communities concerned. For example, the 
project will provide navigation tools and markings on clothing, work under a buddy system, require the 
use of personal protective equipment (PPE), and provide antivenom. A Work Safety and Health Manual 
including standard operating procedures will be provided, aiming at achieving zero accidents. Accident 
reporting forms and emergency plans are also provided in accordance with legal requirements to 
ensure occupational safety during activities carried out within the framework of the project.

INDACOM, PTE, LTD. is a renowned carbon sequestration specialist company, with comprehensive

remote sensing technology, forest and biomass inventories, biodiversity mapping and monitoring, as

management and design, manages field data collection and analysis, ensures ongoing monitoring and

2.4.6 Avoidance of Corruption and Other Unethical Behavior (VCS, 3.19; CCB, G4.3) 
As a private company engaged in carbon sequestration, the project sponsor strives to continuously 
improve the quality of its performance and conducts its work in accordance with applicable laws and 
regulations. The project sponsor establishes a workplace anti-corruption policy and culture by 
preventing, anticipating and eliminating bribery or illegal extortion and gratuities in all work operations 
to promote cooperation and long-term partnerships. The following are the specific contents of the anti-
bribery framework that has been implemented: 
1. Reduce the possibility of bribery and extortion in all work operations that affect them. 
2. Avoid giving and accepting gratuities in all work areas. 
3. Assure service users that there is no bribery, extortion or gratuity in any construction engineering 
service provided. 
4. Enhance the confidence of partners (goods/service providers) to ensure that there is no bribery, 
extortion and bribery in the procurement process. 
5. Ensure the integrity of the project sponsor’s employees.
