well as the provisions of the employment agreement between the employer and employee. Workers 
will be informed about their rights during the recruitment and induction process.  
2.5.3 Human Rights (VCS, 3.19)  
The PSPP Project focused on empowering community groups while adhering to the principles of 
gender equality and inclusivity across various segments of society. The program emphasizes the 
importance of involving all stakeholders without discrimination against any particular group. Tangible 
evidence of this approach is seen through the empowerment of women's groups, considering local 
customs, as well as the empowerment of men's groups, encouraging active participation from all 
community members. PSPP refers to Indonesia Law Number 26, 2000 on Human Rights to ensure 
that the project does not infringe on any rights. The Project establishes effective grievance 
mechanisms to address any violations of the rights of local people and customary right holders and 
provides access to remedy for any adverse impacts of the project.  
2.5.4 Indigenous Peoples and Cultural Heritage (VCS, 3.18, 3.19) 
The Project Proponent conducted an HCV 6 assessment to identify areas critical to maintaining the 
cultural identity of local communities. This includes areas that are culturally important and sacred or 
have religious significance.  The Project Proponent recognized and incorporated the critical cultural 
heritage of indigenous people in the Project design and its social and environmental management 
system guidelines to ensure culturally sensitive and sustainable conservation efforts. By respecting 
indigenous knowledge, practices, rights, and participation, the Project implementation can be more 
effective in achieving both ecological and cultural preservation goals.  

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
57 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
2.5.5 Statutory and Customary Property Rights (VCS, 3.18, 3.19; CCB, G5.1) 
There are no external legal claims within the project area as the project has duly obtained all legal 
management rights under the permit and land ownership belongs to the PT. Suntara Gajapati, but the 
customary property rights of local villagers will be respected. 
2.5.6 Recognition of Property Rights (VCS, 3.7, 3.18, 3.19; CCB, G5.1) 
The Project Proponent conducted land tenure mapping, and HCV 5 and 6 assessments to identify 
overlapping tenure areas and customary rights before the commencement of the Project. The Project 
uses a participation approach and consultation based on PRA principles as described above. The 
Project aims to respect all customary and traditional rights and will not affect any continuing use of 
the land by Farmer Groups. However, there are cases where traditional use of the land is inconsistent 
with the Project’s objectives of conservation, for example clearing natural forests for palm plantations 
and extraction through man-made canals. These instances were discussed in detail during the FPIC 
process outlined and specific solutions to these cases were agreed in the MOUs signed with the 
villages. 
2.5.7 Free, Prior and Informed Consent (VCS, 3.18; CCB, G5.2) 
Description of process 
for obtaining consent 
The Project Proponent implemented the following process to obtain 
consent from the communities: 
1. Scoping and Dissemination. This step identified communities which 
are active in the Project Area. Identification includes general information 
such as village name, administrative boundaries, demographics, and 
livelihood, particularly economic activities conducted in the Project Area. 
This process was followed by initial socialization of the Project and 
community consent was obtained to conduct a full assessment of peat, 
forest inventory, biodiversity, and social interests in and around the 
Project Area.   
2. Planning, Research and Assessment. This step involved a series of 
discussions with provincial, district and sub-district governments 
regarding the Project design. A social economy impact and land tenure 
assessment was carried out to provide basic information on potential 
conflicts, risks, and vulnerable groups in the Project Area. The Project 
Proponent also developed programs which could be carried out in 
collaboration with local communities, a procedure for feedback and 
complaints, and the framework of a benefit sharing mechanism. During 
this process it was identified that all relevant communities were part of a 
village for administrative purposes, and it was decided that formal 
consents should be obtained from each of the villages surrounding the 
Project Area.  
3. Discussion and Negotiation. This process was conducted over a period 
of several months to socialize the results of the planning, research and 
assessment with local communities, with a particular emphasis on the 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
58 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
positive and negative impacts that the Project may bring. Discussions 
focused on addressing community concerns and developing specific 
proposals to mitigate any actual or perceived negative impacts. 
Negotiations included discussion of treatment of areas with overlapping 
tenure, local community aspirations and how sustainable livelihoods 
could be developed to replace any unsustainable activities. This resulted 
in specific proposals to include in a draft Memorandum of 
Understanding (“MoU”).   
4. Points of Agreement. Matters agreed in the discussion and 
negotiation process were then included in the MoU draft, which included 
specific consent to proposed Project Activities. Several meetings were 
then organized with the villages to review and discuss the MoU draft. All 
sections of the community were involved in the MoU process, and the 
text of the MoU was read out and explained both in Bahasa Indonesia 
and the local languages. Discussions with communities were conducted 
directly with village traditional leaders, as well as ‘town hall’ discussions 
with local neighborhood offices. This enabled participation at all levels of 
the community to reflect a more diverse selection of opinion.  
5. Consultation. Following development of the draft MoU, the community 
was provided sufficient space and time to consult with outside advisers 
including local governments, NGOs, community and religious leaders 
and other parties they considered credible regarding the draft MoU. 
Feedback was received and reflected in the MoU and discussions 
continued until a final text was agreed. Once agreement was reached, 
the MoU (consent) was signed by both parties.   
6. Signing. Once the agreed text of an MoU has been finalized, a formal 
village meeting is organized to arrange a formal signing of the MoU. 
Project Activities that affect the relevant villages are specifically set out 
in the MoU and are not conducted until the MoU is signed. 
Outcome of FPIC process The project is designed in a participatory manner to ensure that impacts 
on rights are minimized. FPIC is an ongoing process throughout the 
project lifecycle. It is expected that future activities, especially canal 
blocking and prevention of unplanned deforestation, will affect some 
local communities. These stakeholders have been identified and will 
participate in FPIC as the project progresses and activities expand to 
new parts of the project area. 
The project work plan is carefully managed to ensure that planned 
activities are not carried out in any area where potentially affected 
stakeholders are located. The project is committed to ensuring that 
activities do not encroach on land, relocate people without consent, and 
force people to be displaced or economically displaced. At the same 
time, the project has provided job opportunities for residents in local 
communities, such as recruiting local villagers to work in forest patrol, 
fire prevention and hydrological management teams. And, where 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
59 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
appropriate, fair and transparent benefit-sharing mechanisms will be 
established to ensure that project activities have a net positive impact 
on local communities and indigenous peoples. In the unlikely event that 
project activities displace local people, appropriate compensation will be 
determined through the above-mentioned FPIC process. 
2.5.8 Benefit Sharing Mechanisms (VCS, 3.18, 3.19;) 
The table below illustrates the Project Proponent’s approach to benefit sharing with the local 
communities.  
Process used to 
design the benefit 
sharing plan  
In meetings with residents of the project site, representatives of the project 
proponent explained the proposed project activities and the benefits that the 
community would receive. The results of the discussions were recorded as 
points of agreement, which were included in the draft Memorandum of 
Understanding between the project proponent and the affected villages. The 
draft Memorandum of Understanding was then discussed in detail with the 
community and the results were incorporated into the agreed text. After the 
final text was agreed upon, the project proponent and representatives of the 
affected communities signed the Memorandum of Understanding and copies 
were provided to the community and the village government. If the 
community wishes to submit changes or reviews in the future, the grievance 
and redress mechanism options described in Section 2.3.15 above can be 
used. 
Summary of the 
benefit sharing plan 
The profits from the project will be paid to the communities affected by the 
project implementation in the following four ways. 
1. Corporate Social Responsibility Fund: (i) Build wells + water towers + water 
source treatment devices to provide local residents with sufficient and clean 
water for daily life; (ii) Provide local village school teacher allowances and 
student scholarships 
2. Capacity building: (i) Provide community fire prevention equipment and 
budget; (ii) Provide forestry skills training 
3. Improve governance solutions: (i) Provide jobs related to forest protection 
and restoration; (ii) Provide jobs related to hydrological management; (iii) 
Provide jobs related to forest patrol and fire prevention 
4. Economic benefits to community residents: (i) Providing cattle and sheep 
to residents to promote the development of animal husbandry; (ii) Helping 
residents develop non-forest products; (iii) Demarcating a unified agricultural 
planting area in the project site and sharing economic income with 
community residents 
5. Promoting social and cultural development of the community: (i) Restore 
the sanitary environment in the disaster area and provide food assistance to 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
60 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
residents; (ii) Provide financial assistance to community residents for sports 
activities 
6. Religious activity fund: (i) Provide financial assistance for the construction 
of places of worship; (ii) Sponsor religious festivals; (iii) Social assistance 
during religious festivals 
7. Infrastructure construction fund: (i) Road maintenance, bridge and ditch 
cleaning; (ii) Financial assistance for government office equipment; (iii) 
Clearing rivers to prevent floods. 
Approval and 
dissemination of 
benefit sharing plan 
The project implementation and benefit sharing plan have been discussed in 
detail with the community. 
The results of the discussion have been recorded. Currently, 75% of 
stakeholders agree to the project implementation, and the remaining 25% 
are still waiting. The project party will continue to engage and communicate 
with stakeholders to strive for 100% agreement. If the community wishes to 
submit changes or reviews in the future, they can use the appeal and redress 
mechanism described in Section 2.3.15 above. 
2.5.9 Property Rights Protection (VCS, 3.18, 3.19; CCB, G5.3) 
The project activities do not lead to involuntary removal or relocation of property rights holders from 
their lands or territories and does not force rights holders to relocate activities important to their 
culture or livelihood. If in the future any relocation of activities needs to be undertaken, it will take 
place with a written agreement that demonstrates that the agreement was made with the free, prior, 
and informed consent of those concerned and includes provisions for just and fair compensation.  
In terms of territorial and resource rights, Indigenous and local communities in Indonesia can hold 
customary rights to land and its natural resources through formal or informal rights. Formal rights can 
take the form of land title, government concessions or documented rights of use. In addition, some 
village administrative districts overlap with a portion of the Project Area. 
Informal rights are often based on longstanding traditional use and stewardship of the forest area. 
These customary and collective rights can include access to forests for subsistence activities such as 
hunting, gathering, and shifting agriculture, as well as culturally or religiously related purposes within 
their customary territories. Informal rights often operate alongside the formal state land registration 
and concession grant system. This can lead to conflicts between formal law and traditional rights. 
While Indonesia’s legal system does recognize customary law, it can be difficult to ascertain the legal 
situation in cases where the state grants concession rights over an area (such as the Project Area) in 
which there are customary rights of indigenous communities. 
2.5.10 Illegal Activity Identification (VCS, 3.19; CCB, G5.4) 
Based on the biodiversity and livelihood assessment and discussions with the community, some illegal 
activities were identified as follows: 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
61 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
• Illegal logging. Local residents illegally cut down forests to plant palm trees, which is harmful to the 
environment in the project area. 
• Illegal hunting. Villagers use traditional traps to hunt. 
The project team has identified the driving factors and developed mitigation measures. The activities 
are as follows: 
• Conduct patrols and include illegal logging and hunting in the list of monitoring activities. 
• Provide alternative livelihoods for palm growers and hunters.  
The project includes livelihood training, revolving funds and other support activities to empower these 
community groups.

There are no ongoing or unresolved disputes regarding land, territorial and resource rights in the 
project area.

2.6 Additional Information Relevant to the Project  
2.6.1 Leakage Management (VCS, 3.11, 3.15) 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
63 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
In general, nature-based solutions projects are subject to risks from market leakage and activity 
displacement. This is likely due to the greater scope of possible leakage sources, in addition to the 
difficulty in estimating an accurate leakage belt. Leakage risks must be considered by the Project with 
assessments of both top-down and bottom-up risks, alongside project-specific safeguards. Project 
assessment of leakage risks looks at both the possible sources of leakage and any safeguards put in 
place by a project to mitigate them.  
Three leakage modules are applicable to this Project:  
• VMD0010 Estimating emissions from activities shifting from unplanned deforestation and 
unplanned wetland degradation (LK-ASU) 
• VMD0054 Estimating leakage from ARR activities 
• VMD0044 Estimating emissions from activities shifting from unplanned deforestation and 
unplanned wetland degradation (LK-ECO) 
The Project will implement three strategies to manage potential leakage. 
The first strategy of the Project to manage leakage is done at the national level by being an advocate 
for conservation and restoration projects. This accords with the ambition of the GOI to reduce 
deforestation of peatland so the Project Proponent expects the GOI to be aligned in this respect.  
The second strategy is implemented at the regional level by engaging with relevant stakeholders from 
Government and the non-government sectors. The objective is to make the local population aware of 
the importance of fire control, maintaining natural forest cover and conserving biodiversity.  These 
activities are undertaken by the Project Proponent and carried out with the support and involvement 
from the local provincial government bodies.   
The third strategy is implemented at the Project level, covering the activities as follows:   
• Working with the local communities to promote the benefits for health and the environment of the 
Project and the threats to health and the environment of illegal activities which lead to deforestation.  
• Taking active steps to reduce and remove the threat of leakage from any displacement of planned 
deforestation activities and ecological leakage. The specific steps will be devised following 
consultation with local communities.  
• Gathering production and water table data from the Project Area which will be collected, analyzed, 
and compiled upon each verification event to estimate activity shifting and ecological leakage.

3.1.4 Baseline Scenario (VCS, 3.13) 
3.1.4.1 REDD+ Baseline Scenario 
This section provides justification for the project’s baseline and additionality using the “VT0001 Tool 
for the demonstration and assessment of additionality in VCS Agriculture, Forestry and Other Land Use 
(AFOLU) Project Activities (version 3.0)” which was adapted from the “combined tool to identify the 
baseline scenario and demonstrate additionality in A/R CDM project activities: Version 2”. 
This tool is applicable to the project as the project fulfils the following conditions: 
a) AFOLU activities the same or similar to the proposed project activity on the land within the proposed 
project boundary performed with or without being registered as the VCS AFOLU project do not lead to 
violation of any applicable law even if the law is not enforced; 
b) The project used the tool to determine additionality as the tool provides a stepwise approach 
justifying the determination of the most plausible baseline scenario. 
Step 1. Identify Alternative Land Use Scenarios for the Proposed VCS AFOLU Project Activity. 
Sub-Step 1a. Identify Credible Alternative Land Use Scenarios for the Proposed VCS AFOLU Project 
Activity. 
Table 3- provides a list of realistic and credible alternative land use scenarios that could occur in the 
project area in the absence of the project. These scenarios were developed by analyzing pre-project 
land use, current land use and land use classification in similar ecosystems in Riau Province, land use 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
80 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
trends in other similar areas in Indonesia, and similar activities observed in geographic areas with 
similar socio-economic and ecological conditions as the proposed project activity. All identified land 
use scenarios are considered realistic and credible because the land uses within the proposed project 
boundary currently exist or existed at some time beginning ten years prior to the project start date but 
no longer exist. 
Table 3-5. Identified land use scenarios in the absence of the project. 
Land Use Scenario Description 
Industrial acacia 
plantations and 
smallholder palm 
plantations-Continuation 
of pre-project land use  
Industrial acacia plantation is another common land use in lowland 
Indonesia, which also requires maintaining the water table of the peat to 
below 70cm.Acacia plantations have been expanding in Indonesia 
between 2011-2020, from 9.6 million ha in 2011 to 11.2 million ha in 
2020, and more than 50% are located in peatland. 
Currently, 12,809.81 hectares of land in the project area have been 
leased to Sinar Mas Group for the planting of industrial acacia trees for a 
total of 48 years, starting in 2002 and ending in 2049. At the same time, 
local residents have been cutting down and planting palm trees in the 
project area's protected areas since 1999, leading to the expansion of 
smallholder palm plantations in the project area. Although such activities 
are illegal under Indonesian law, they were not stopped before the project 
started due to poor supervision and historical reasons. This shows that 
this situation is very likely to continue even without project activities. 
Industrial acacia 
plantation 
Industrial acacia plantation is another common land use in lowland 
Indonesia, which also requires maintaining the water table of the peat to 
below 70cm.Acacia plantations have been expanding in Indonesia 
between 2011-2020, from 9.6 million ha in 2011 to 11.2 million ha in 
2020, and more than 50% are located in peatland. 
In the project landscape, acacia plantations are already widespread and 
have been established in the peatland areas to the east, west and south 
of the project site, as shown in Map 3-1. At the same time, 12,809.81 
hectares of peatland in the project site have been leased to Sinar Mas 
Group for industrial acacia plantations, indicating that there is a clear 
threat of conversion in the project area. The large-scale expansion of 
acacia plantations in Indonesia has led to high levels of drainage and 
complete conversion of large areas of peatland, indicating that this is a 
highly potential land use in the project area. 
Industrial oil palm 
plantation 
Industrial oil palm plantations are a very common agricultural land use in 
Indonesia’s lowland peatlands, with crops grown on rotations of 25 to 35 
years, with harvests available after 4 to 5 years, despite the peat soil 
being unsuitable for cultivation. Palm oil plantations, like industrial 
acacia plantations, require peat to be continuously drained to below 70 
cm. Indonesia’s oil palm industry has grown rapidly over the past few 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
81 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
decades. Legal industrial oil palm expansion occurs almost entirely in 
non-forested areas following legal forest clearing (designated as Other 
Land Use, Penggunaan Lain Area or APL) or in areas within forest areas 
designated for conversion (designated as Conversion Production Forest, 
Hutan Produksi Konversi or HPK). There are areas designated as APL or 
HPK within the project area, with industrial palm plantations also located 
to the east, west and south of the project site. However, due to the 
issuance of “Presidential Instruction No. 8 of 2018 on the Suspension 
and Evaluation of Oil Palm Plantation Permits and Increasing Productivity 
of Oil Palm Plantation”, industrial palm plantation is not a feasible land 
use option. 
Smallholder Oil Palm 
Plantations  
Oil palm plantations are not legally permitted as oil palm cannot legally 
be established on land designated as Hutan Produksi or production 
forest. Although some small-scale palm cultivation activities do currently 
exist on the project site, it is also untenable due to legal barriers and 
therefore, it was excluded from further consideration as an alternative 
land use option. 
Forest with commercial 
logging  
Currently, none of the production forests on peatlands in the project area 
landscape are subject to active commercial logging today, as most timber 
companies have closed operations due to changes in the industry, 
making logging unprofitable. One major change has been the tightening 
of regulations on management practices, taxation, and labor 
management. Historically, timber companies were not required to 
manage these areas long-term and harvested timber at unsustainable 
levels, which resulted in natural forest areas with very little timber 
remaining within the allowed diameter size. With little timber left, the 
focus has shifted to conversion to oil palm plantations. 
Protected Forests 
Without benefit of 
Carbon Crediting  
Forest land could be legally converted to some type of protected or 
conservation forest. However, this is a legally complex and challenging 
process that is governed and regulated by a wide variety of laws in 
Indonesia. The likelihood of this process being successful remains fairly 
low, but it can be done legally.   
Smallholder agriculture  As production forest, the project area cannot be legally converted to 
smallholder agriculture. Nevertheless, smallholders often target forest 
land that is neglected and not subject to active concession licenses or 
commercial exploitation. In the absence of a commercial license, 
smallholders may seek to assert ownership of the occupied land. 
In addition to these main plausible land use scenarios, a number of other land uses were analyzed, 
such as fish pond development, industrial aquaculture, infrastructure and settlements. However, all 
cases were deemed to lack sufficient plausibility or priority to be included in the analysis. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
82 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
 
Map 3-1. Location of industrial acacia plantations and industrial palm plantations near the project site 
3.1.4.2 ARR Baseline Scenario 
According to the applied VM0047 v1.0, the baseline scenario has been established using the 
performance benchmark approach outlined in Appendix 1 of the methodology. This baseline scenario 
reflects the expected business-as-usual growth of new vegetative stocking, as determined through 
observations conducted outside the project area in designated representative sample plots (control 
plots) with comparable land use/land cover conditions. The baseline vegetation growth in these 
control plots is assessed remotely.   
Performance Benchmark  
The performance benchmark is defined in VCS Program Definition v4.5 as a benchmark against which 
the performance of individual projects is assessed to determine additionality and/or the crediting 
baseline. The performance benchmark is set equal to the average cumulative increase in estimated 
vegetative stocks from their initial state, observed in designated control plots, relative to the project 
area. At this stage, the project estimated the performance benchmark through exante estimation to 
assess additionality. An ex-ante estimate of the performance benchmark is calculated by referencing a 
value of ∆SI
control, t 
and ∆SI
wp, t
 using the stocking indices for the historic period t= -10, t= -6, and t=0 
for the selected control plots and project plots. The performance benchmark comparing the baseline 
scenario to the project scenario provides a clear and quantifiable measure of the project’s impact on 
net GHG emissions.  
Define the Performance Benchmark 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template

Thirty (100 m x 100 m) project plots were selected for the non-forested area strata, while control plots 
were identified by spatial analysis in the donor pool area. The donor pool area was determined as per 
the requirements of VM0047 v1.0. The donor pool area was used to estimate the control plots, 
provided that the control plots were non-overlapping units with a size not exceeding ±20% of the 
average size of the project plots. Given that the average size of the project plots was 1 ha, the control 
plot area for the project should be 1 ha. This could be achieved by dividing the donor pool area into a 
grid of size 100 m x 100 m. 
 
Map 3-2 Donor Pool Analysis Based on 100 km Buffer 
The process continues with the assessment of the project plots and matching analysis between the 
control plots and the project plots. The project used stocking index data from the NDFI (Normalized 
Difference Fraction Index), a remote sensing indicator based on Landsat imagery. 
The stocking index is historical data calculated at t=-10, t=-6, and t=0. In addition, to match the 
control plots and project plots, we derived the multivariate Euclidean distance between the control 
plots and the project plots. 
Based on these data sets, we used the K nearest neighbor method (see Appendix 5) for matching 
analysis to select the control plots that match the project plots. The analysis results generated 30 
control plots that matched the project plots (see Map3-3 ).

Baseline Scenario  
Based on the current VCS standards for the project area, the most realistic and credible alternative in 
the absence of reforestation project activities is that the change in aboveground biomass in the 
control plots remains normal. The most credible alternative is to continue with pre-project activities as 
this is typical for the project area. 
According to VM0047, the project evaluates the baseline scenario by considering a historical 
performance baseline that represents the normal change in vegetation cover before the project start 
date. 
The performance baseline for land conditions in the 10 years before the project start date is 1, 
∆SI
control
 is -0.0176, and ∆SI
wp
 is -0.0177. These values reflect the baseline conditions in the project 
and control areas under the business-as-usual scenario in non-forest areas over the past decade. 
Based on these results, the project target area (non-forest area) is likely to remain in the pre-project 
condition based on the performance baseline results. Therefore, without ARR activities, the project 
target area will remain a non-forest area with sparse biomass.

Sub-step 1b. Consistency of credible land use scenarios with enforced mandatory applicable laws and 
regulations 
The main plausible land use options identified under sub-step 1a were assessed from a legal 
perspective based on Indonesian laws and regulations. Since the project area was essentially 
designated as “production forest” or “Hutan Produksi”, the analysis focused on what was legally 
permissible for such land areas. 
Table 3-6. Alternative land use options consistent with laws and regulations. 
Land Use Scenario Description 
Industrial acacia 
plantations and 
smallholder palm 
plantations-Continuation 
of pre-project land use  
Acacia plantation is consistent with applicable laws and regulations, 
including the Forestry Law No. 41/1999 and No. 19/2004 and the 
Ministry of Forestry Regulation No. 31/2014 and supporting regulations 
including the latest MoEF Regulation No. 7/2021.  
Industrial acacia 
plantation 
Acacia plantation is consistent with applicable laws and regulations, 
including the Forestry Law No. 41/1999 and No. 19/2004 and the 
Ministry of Forestry Regulation No. 31/2014 and supporting regulations 
including the latest MoEF Regulation No. 7/2021.  
Industrial oil palm 
plantation 
The legal name of the project area is Production Forest or Hutan 
Produksi(HP). Legally, commercial oil palm plantations cannot be 
established in areas designated as Production Forest. A commercial palm 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
88 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
oil plantation can only be legally established if the area is first removed 
from the forest area in accordance with Government Decree PP No. 
60/2012. Furthermore, only forest areas that are formally designated as 
Conversion Production Forest (Hutan Produksi Konversi or HPK) can be 
removed from the forest area. The project area does not include any 
forest areas designated as HPK, so the option of commercial conversion 
to oil palm is not considered a legally viable option. 
Smallholder Oil Palm 
Plantations  
Oil palm plantations are not legally permitted as oil palm cannot legally 
be established on land designated as Hutan Produksi or production 
forest. Although some small-scale palm cultivation activities do currently 
exist on the project site, it is also untenable due to legal barriers and 
therefore, it was excluded from further consideration as an alternative 
land use option. 
Forest with commercial 
logging  
Logging concession is also legally a possibility in line with the Forestry 
Law No. 41/1999 and No. 19/2004 and the Ministry of Forestry 
Regulation No. 31/2014 and relevant regulations including the latest 
MoEF Regulation No. 7/2021.  
Protected Forests 
Without benefit of 
Carbon Crediting  
Forest land could be legally converted to some type of protected or 
conservation forest. However, this is a legally complex and challenging 
process that is governed and regulated by a wide variety of laws in 
Indonesia. The likelihood of this process being successful remains fairly 
low, but it can be done legally.   
Smallholder agriculture  As per Forestry Laws No. 41/1999 and No. 19/2004, and Ministry of 
Forestry Decree No. 31/2014, smallholder agriculture is not a legally 
permissible land-use on areas designated as production forest. However, 
unexploited forest land which is not actively managed, is often targeted 
by smallholders via a number of quasi-official routes. 
 
As a result of the legal analysis, commercial oil palm plantations are not considered a credible 
alternative land-use scenario in the project area as it is not legally permissible. While smallholder 
agriculture is not legally permissible in the project area, it frequently occurs across Indonesia and 
therefore merits further consideration as part of the barrier analysis. 
 
Step 2: Barrier Analysis 
Step 2a. Identify barriers that could prevent implementation of at least one alternative land use option 
In this section, we evaluate the remaining barriers to the remaining 6 plausible land use options to 
identify the most plausible land use option. The results of this analysis are shown in Table 3-7. 
Table 3-7. Identification of barriers that could prevent implementation of each option 
Land Use Scenario Description 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
89 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
Industrial acacia 
plantations and 
smallholder palm 
plantations-Continuation 
of pre-project land use  
Currently, 12,809.81 hectares of land in the project area have been 
leased to Sinar Mas Group for the planting of industrial acacia trees for a 
total of 48 years, starting in 2002 and ending in 2049. At the same time, 
since 1999, local residents have been cutting down and planting palm 
trees in the project site's protected area, resulting in the continuous 
expansion of smallholder palm plantations in the project site. Although 
this activity is illegal under Indonesian law, it could not be stopped before 
the project started due to inadequate supervision and historical reasons. 
This shows that this is a scenario that is very likely to continue without 
project activities. 
Industrial acacia 
plantation 
This land use plan will face the question of whether Sinar Mas Group will 
plan the remaining land for industrial acacia planting. Because when 
selecting acacia planting sites, it is considered whether the soil is 
peatland and the thickness of the peat. The peat thickness of the part of 
the project site that is not leased to Sinar Mas Group is generally not 
high, so there are obstacles in fully converting it into an industrial acacia 
plantation. 
Smallholder Oil Palm 
Plantations  
This type of land use often involves smallholders burning the landscape 
to prepare for the planting of oil palm seedlings, which is highly 
destructive to flora and fauna. This activity is illegal, so it cannot be truly 
converted to smallholder Oil Palm Plantations. 
Forest with commercial 
logging  
The primary barrier to this land-use is the lack of commercial-sized 
timber due to the majority of the site having been logged between 1970-
2000. The business model around commercial logging has also changed. 
When commercial logging first started in the 60’s and 70’s, a 
combination of loose regulations on tax collecting, cheap labor, and 
minimal labor laws allowed companies to operate profitably on marginal 
forest sites. The market was also conducive as timber prices were high 
and export markets were very open. This land-use is considerably more 
difficult as high value export markets are now difficult to access without 
following strict certification standards and this would be very difficult to 
obtain for production coming from peatlands. 
Protected Forests 
Without benefit of 
Carbon Crediting  
If the project is not registered as a VCS AFOLU project, there are 
investment barriers that will prevent the project from being implemented. 
In the absence of carbon financing revenue, private entities will not 
receive any income from the project. At the same time, establishing a 
protected forest in the project area will not receive local political support 
because it will require the local government to use the existing national 
budget to pay for forest protection in the area. The government prefers a 
land use method that can generate tax revenue and create local jobs. 
Smallholder agriculture  In general, peat soils are unsuitable for growing crops and are therefore a 
major barrier to smallholder agriculture, which is further supported by 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
90 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
the low levels of smallholder agriculture in the region and across 
Indonesia’s peatlands. Secondly, the project area is prone to recurring 
fires and farmers are reluctant to invest in areas where their farms will 
continue to be destroyed. Expansion of smallholder agriculture in areas 
designated as production forests is rare, mainly when there is no active 
management permit in the area. As there are no barriers to the 
development of commercial plantations in the project area and the 
likelihood that the area will remain undeveloped forest for a long time is 
low, expansion of smallholder agriculture in the project area is not 
considered credible. 
 
Step 2b. Elimination of land-use scenarios that are prevented by the identified barriers 
In conclusion, there are significant barriers that prevent the realization of all the previously listed 
scenarios leaving a single credible land-use scenario: Industrial acacia plantations and smallholder 
palm plantations. Thus, this alternative was identified as the baseline scenario. 
Step 3: Investment analysis 
Because a single credible land-use scenario was identified through the analytical steps above, a 
detailed investment analysis is not required by the VT0001 tool. 
Step 4: Common practice analysis  
It is not a common practice for the Indonesian government to have intact forest on land designated for 
production that is not being commercially exploited and generating tax revenues. However, there are 
some examples of this that tend to be small donor-backed conservation projects or where large 
corporates in the extractive industries are paying restoration costs of sites as reparations for areas 
damaged as part of their operations in other locations. These are not widespread and are typically 
very small in size. 
Conclusion 
The project is considered additional, with the most likely and plausible business-as-usual scenario 
being conversion to Industrial acacia plantations and smallholder palm plantations.

3.2 Quantification of Estimated GHG Emission Reductions and Removals 
3.2.1 Baseline Emissions (VCS, 3.15) 
3.2.1.1 General Procedures and Assumptions 
The project has used VM0007 (version 1.8) REDD+ MF, VM0047 (version 1.0), VMD0007 (version 3.3) 
BL-UP, VMD0042 (version 1.1) BL-PEAT, and VMD0041 (version 1.3) E-BPB to assess the project’s 
baseline emissions. 
These emissions sources included:  
1. Changes in aboveground biomass stocks (Characterized by conversion to smallholder palm 
plantations);  
2. Peat microbial decomposition;  
3. Peat burning; and   
4. Dissolved organic carbon from bodies of water. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
94 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
All emission assumptions and omissions described in the project are conservative efforts that result in 
lower emissions to maintain the validity of calculations and estimates for baseline scenarios. 
It is assumed that no additional rewetting events will occur in the baseline scenario. Examples of 
natural events excluded in the analysis may include canal collapses, gradual subsidence that can lead 
to changes in relative water table depth, or reduced CO
2
 emission rates as the aerobic soil layer 
becomes thinner.  
It is also assumed that the regular canal maintenance activities, which are normally used for drainage 
and transportation, will remain unchanged.  
As information on the amount and frequency of dredging is not available (e.g., if dredging activities 
cause peat to be exposed to aerobic decomposition), these emissions are omitted from this project. 
This is a conservative omission, as the discharge of the water body is lower than that produced by the 
microbial decomposition of peat, and therefore the discharge estimate of the water strata is lower 
than that of the strata in the same location in the project proposal. 
In the baseline scenario, the project calculated the annual emissions for carbon dioxide (CO
2
) and 
methane (CH
4
) as these emissions are impacted directly by changes in land cover classes and 
drainage status over time. However, the project conservatively omitted nitrous oxide (N
2
O) and GHG 
emissions derived from biomass burning.

According to the methodology, the reference area must meet the following criteria:  
1. The main agent(s) of deforestation in the RRD at the start of the historical reference period must be 
the same as those expected to cause deforestation in the project area during the project term. Such 
determination can be accomplished by:  
⚫ A qualitative assessment, opinion of local experts or literature sources to demonstrate the 
proportion of agriculturalist versus ranchers is the same (±20%) in the reference region as in the 
project area  
⚫ Rapid assessment techniques for determination of lack of legal rights to use land is the same 
in the reference region as in the project area, and   
⚫ Rapid assessment techniques for determination of proportion of agent’s resident in the local 
area (lived in area >5 years) versus immigrants (lived in area <5 years) is the same (±20%) in the 
reference region as in the project area. 
2. Landscape factors of forest types, soil types, slope and elevation classes for both the project area 
and RRD meet the criteria below. These factors can be determined by analysis of spatial databases 
(e.g., vegetation map, soil suitability map, DEM for slope and elevation) in a GIS.    

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
96 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
⚫ Forest classes must be present in the project area in the same proportion as in the RRD 
(±20%) at the start of the historical reference period    
⚫ Soil types that are suitable for the land-use practice used by the main agent(s) of deforestation 
must be present in the project area in the same proportion as in the RRD (±20%)  
⚫ The ratio of slope classes “gentle” (slope <15%) to “steep” (slope ≥15%) in the project area 
must be the same as the ratio in the RRD (±20%)  
⚫ Elevation classes (500m classes) in the project area must be in the same proportion as in the 
RRD (±20%) 
3. Transportation networks and human infrastructure, such as roads, navigable rivers and 
settlements, that increase the likelihood of deforestation and that exist historically in the RRD must be 
directly comparable to those that are expected to exist within the project area during the project term. 
The following conditions must be met:  
⚫ Where navigable rivers are present in the project area, navigable river/stream density (m/km
2
) 
for the RRD is the same, less than, or does not exceed by more than 20% that of the project area.  
⚫ Road density (m/km
2
) for the RRD is the same, less than, or does not exceed by more than 20% 
that of the project area, including a buffer around the project area of at least 1 km, at the start of the 
historical reference period,  
⚫ In non-forested areas around the RRD, settlement density (at the start of the historical 
reference period) is the same, less than, or does not exceed by more than 20% that of non-forested 
areas around the project area (at the start of the historical reference period). The analysis must be 
performed in 1 km buffer zones around parcel(s) in the RRD and a 1 km buffer around the project 
area. Settlement density is expressed as settlements/km
2
. 
Social factors having an impact on land-use change patterns within the RRD and the project area must 
be the same or have the same effect at the start of the historical reference period. Examples can 
include presence of gangs or guerillas, or the ethnic composition of local populations.  
5. Policies and regulations having an impact on land-use change patterns within the RRD and the 
project area must be of the same type or have an equivalent effect at the start of the historical 
reference period, taking into account the current level of enforcement. This means that where 
subnational administrative units are governed by a different set of land-use regulations, it is 
necessary to ensure that the boundary of the RRD does not cross into another subnational unit that 
does not have equivalent policies or regulations.   
6. Exclusion of planned deforestation. Areas of planned deforestation must be excluded from the 
reference region boundaries were evident.

Map 3-5 Reference Area 
Part 3: Calculating the annual areas of unplanned baseline deforestation in the RRD  
The project used a simple historic method to estimate the annual area of unplanned deforestation. 
1. Analysis of historical deforestation  
This step consisted of quantifying the historical deforestation rate during the historical reference 
period (2015-2024) within the selected RRD. 
Collection of appropriate data sources 
The selection of pre-existing Land Use/Land Cover (LULC) products for this project is a deliberate 
methodological choice driven by several key factors rooted in the practical challenges associated with 
utilizing historical satellite imagery, particularly within tropical environments. A primary impediment to 
relying on raw satellite data for diachronic analysis in such regions is the pervasive issue of persistent 
cloud cover. Tropical areas are frequently characterized by high atmospheric humidity and convective 
activity, leading to extensive cloud formation that can obscure the land surface, rendering significant 
portions of satellite image archives unusable for detailed land cover mapping or change detection. 
Identifying cloud-free or minimally cloudy imagery across extended historical periods often proves to 
be a time-consuming and potentially limiting endeavor, potentially introducing temporal gaps or 
requiring compromises in image quality. 
Furthermore, even when suitable historical satellite imagery can be acquired, the resolution of publicly 
or readily available datasets from earlier periods may be lower compared to contemporary sensors. 
Lower spatial resolution can introduce significant challenges related to spectral mixing, where the 
reflectance values of different land cover types within a single pixel become averaged, leading to 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
99 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
ambiguities and potential misclassifications during image analysis. This issue is particularly pertinent 
in heterogeneous landscapes where fine-scale variations in land cover are prevalent. Traditional image 
classification workflows necessitate rigorous accuracy assessments, often involving the collection of 
ground truth data or the use of high-resolution validation datasets. Conducting such assessments for 
historical imagery can be logistically complex and resource-intensive, if not entirely infeasible in 
certain situations due to changes in land cover or access limitations over time. 
In contrast, leveraging existing LULC products offers a streamlined approach that circumvents many of 
these inherent difficulties. These products, often generated by reputable organizations using 
sophisticated methodologies and incorporating various data sources, provide readily available and 
spatially explicit information on land cover distribution. By utilizing these pre-processed datasets, the 
project team can avoid the complexities and uncertainties associated with raw satellite image 
selection, atmospheric correction, geometric rectification, and image classification. Moreover, the 
reliance on established LULC products inherently addresses the traditional accuracy assessment 
concerns that would typically accompany a project-specific classification effort. These products have 
generally undergone independent validation processes by their producers, providing a degree of 
confidence in their thematic accuracy. 
 
Beyond the practical advantages in terms of data processing and accuracy considerations, the 
adoption of existing LULC products significantly enhances the transparency of the project's data 
foundation. The explicit use of publicly available and well-documented datasets allows for greater 
scrutiny and reproducibility of the research findings. Stakeholders and other researchers can readily 
access and examine the underlying land cover information used in the analysis, fostering trust and 
facilitating independent verification. This commitment to data transparency directly mitigates the 
potential for concerns regarding data manipulation or subjective interpretation that might arise from a 
project team's in-house processing of raw satellite imagery. The use of established LULC products 
provides a consistent and standardized representation of land cover, contributing to the overall rigor 
and credibility of the project's outcomes. 
Mapping of historical deforestation  
Due to the spatially fragmented distribution of Rapid Rural Development (RRD) areas, classifying 
individual RRD plot data through remote sensing proves challenging. Utilizing existing project data is 
more efficient than conducting image classification. 
As indicated in Table 3-11, three distinct time points, corresponding to Map3-6, were selected as 
historical reference periods. For the 2015 initial historical reference period, the Copernicus Global 
Land Service Dynamic Land Cover map (accuracy exceeding 80%) was employed to identify 100% 
Forest RRD data. Subsequently, in 2017, 2020, and 2024, the ESRI Sentinel-2 10-Meter Land 
Use/Land Cover data (overall accuracy of 86%) was used to extract forest features for the project. This 
choice was driven by the data's high resolution and improved accuracy in land feature classification.

Calculation of the historical deforestation rate  
The results of the historical deforestation analysis give the forest and non-forest areas (cropland and 
other uses) at the beginning and end of the historical period (2015, 2017, 2020 and 2024, 
respectively). Based on these values, the deforestation area was calculated for each historical 
reference interval (i.e. 2015-2017, 2017-2020 and 2020-2024). The results are presented in Table 3-
11. 
Map accuracy assessment  
The post-classification classes were unified to obtain the final three-year classification. To validate the 
post classification, Cohen's Kappa coefficient of 0.93 was used. An overall accuracy (AAu) of 96.5% by 
2024 was reached. Since at the time of classification there were no field data for the entire RRD and 
no aerial photographs or high-resolution images were available at time of analysis, it was assumed 
that the classification algorithm used for the most recent image is applicable to past images and will 
achieve the same accuracy (minimum accuracy level required of 80%).

Part 5:  Location and quantification of unplanned deforestation threat 
Step 3.0 Determination of whether location analysis is required  
Whether or not a location analysis is required is determined by the initial configuration of the RRL 
landscape:  
a. Mosaic Configuration  
In the case of a mosaic configuration, location analysis is not required. Location analysis can still be 
elected to avoid the conservative approach with regard to carbon stocks. If location analysis is not 
elected, proceed directly to Step 3.4.  
b. Transition Configuration  
In the case of a transition configuration, location analysis is not required where it can be shown that 
≥25% of the project geographic boundary is within 50 m of land that has been anthropogenically 
deforested within the 10 years prior to the project start date. If this criterion is not met, location 
analysis is always required. Location analysis may always be elected to avoid the conservative 
approach with regard to carbon stocks. If location analysis is neither required nor elected, proceed 
directly to Step 3.4.  
c. Frontier Configuration  

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
108 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
In the case of a frontier configuration location analysis is always required.  
A location analysis is always required when using the population driver approach for projecting rate of 
deforestation.  
Considering that historical deforestation in the reference region (RRL) and the PA has a mosaic 
configuration, location analysis is not required. 
Step 3.4 Mapping of the locations of future deforestation 
The project scenario assumes that deforestation occurs first in the strata with the lowest carbon 
stocks (among all relevant carbon sinks). To estimate carbon stock changes and GHG emissions, we 
followed Part 4 of the BL-UP tool. 
Based on the X-STR module, pre-deforestation strata were defined for the total area affected by 
deforestation in the project area and seepage zone. The forest strata were constructed using available 
spatial data such as digital elevation models (DEM), soils, land cover types, climate, and topography. 
The results showed that only one forest type was identified in these areas, namely tropical mixed 
evergreen broadleaf forests. 
Part 6: Baseline carbon stock change 
Based on the X-STR module, pre-deforestation strata were defined for the total area affected by 
deforestation in the project area and seepage zone. The forest strata were constructed using available 
spatial data such as digital elevation models (DEM), soils, land cover types, climate, and topography. 
The results showed that only one forest type was identified in these areas, namely tropical mixed 
evergreen broadleaf forests. 
Carbon stock changes per stratum was define according modules CP-AB and CP-S. Carbon pools 
excluded from the project have counted as zero (DW and L). Estimation of post deforestation carbon 
stocks are assumed to be the long-term average stocks on the land following deforestation. 
Determination of carbon stocks by land use using a simple approach: A list of possible post-
deforestation land uses is established, taking into account land uses in the reference area that were 
deforestation-affected during the historical reference period. The land use with the highest long-term 
carbon stock is conservatively considered to represent the future post-deforestation land use 
category. Carbon stocks are calculated for the highest carbon stocking land use category and used as 
a representative of all post-deforestation carbon stocks in that land use during the project period.  

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4

CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
110 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
 
For AUWD-REDD and RWE-REDD project activities, use Equation 23 of VMD0007 BL-UP for the terrestrial 
carbon pools.

Note that wood product stocks, non-tree vegetation/biomass, dead wood and litter are not included in 
this  project.  Soil  organic  carbon  is  not  calculated  for  the  AUWD-REDD  project  according  to  this 
methodology, but is accounted for in the CIW and RWE emissions estimates. 
Baseline net carbon stock change according to BL-UP is equal to pre-deforestation carbon stock minus 
the long-term average carbon stock of the post-deforestation land use (oil palm). 
The  carbon  stock  before  deforestation  is  equal  to  the  average  carbon  density  estimated  from  the 
biomass plots in the project area. The long-term average carbon stock is based on the land use for oil 
palm cultivation after deforestation. Module BL-UP states that the average carbon stock over the entire 
period should be used to estimate the carbon stock after deforestation. 
The long-term average carbon stock for oil palm is calculated using the method described in the "AFOLU 
Guidelines:  Example  of  Calculation  of   Long-term   Average  Carbon  Stock  for   ARR   Projects   with 
Deforestation". The calculation method is shown in the following table:

The GHG emissions in the baseline within the project boundary were estimated as Equation 15 below.  
1. According to VMD0007, the N
2

direct-N,i,t
 must be calculated using the latest AA/R CDM tool 
“Estimation of direct nitrous oxide emission from nitrogen fertilization” (Module E-NA). However, the 
tool is not applicable when A/R CDM project activities are implemented on wetlands which is the case 
in this project. Hence, the GHG emissions from fertilizer application is excluded in the baseline.  
2. According to VMD0007, the E
BiomassBurn,i,t
 must be calculated using Module E-BPB. Where used in 
the baseline, accounting must occur under both the baseline and project scenarios in both the project 
area and in the leakage belt. As this project assumes that the fire prevention program is successfully 
implemented by the project, it is assumed that no fires will occur after the project initiation in the 
project scenario. Hence, the GHG emissions from biomass burning (not the peat burn) in the baseline 
were conservatively omitted.  
3. According to VMD00076, the E
FC,I,t
 must be calculated using Module E-FFC. All fossil fuel 
combustion associated with a project may be accounted, including fossil fuel combustion of 
subcontractors that are conducting (parts of the) work to implement the project. Fossil fuel 
combustion in all situations is an optional emission source. This project assumes that the use of fossil 
fuel will be de minimis under the project scenario. Hence, the GHG emissions from combustion of 
fossil fuels in the baseline were conservatively omitted.

3.2.1.3 Baseline emissions from peat soils 
Part 1: Stratification of peatland and non-peatland 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
116 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
Stratification of areas in peat versus non-peat areas, as well as the peat thickness was determined 
through a field peat inventory, drone lidar scanning, and subsequent peat depth modeling. This 
sampling design fulfilled the requirements described in the VCS methodology VM0007 modules M-PEAT 
and X-STR. The result showed that 81.83% of the project area constituted peat thickness ≥ 50 cm.  
Part 2: Stratification of peatland based on peat thickness and peat depletion time (PDT)  
Drained peatland is subject to microbial decomposition and uncontrolled burning. Therefore, peat at 
some locations in the project area may be depleted before the end of project period in the baseline 
scenario. The project calculated the amount of time it would have taken for the peat in the project area 
to become depleted (peat depletion time; PDT) without the project based on the following parameters: 
 1. Peat thickness;  
2. Drain ability elevation limit;  
3. Surface elevation; and  
4. Subsidence related to microbial decomposition and burning. 
Peat thickness 
The peat thickness in the project area was layered with a resolution of 50 cm as required by the VCS 
module X-STR. The project area is divided into 12 levels, as shown in Table 3-16 and Map 3-10.

Maps 3-13 Stratification of peat carbon stock at the project start 
Eligible area for WRC crediting  
This project uses the total inventory method to identify credit-eligible areas in accordance with the VCS 
rules set forth in Section 5.4 of VM0007 Module X STR. Both the PDT assessment and the project 
scenario peat depletion assessment were used to identify areas eligible for inclusion in the WRC 
peatland activity baseline. The credit period for GHG emission reduction or removal of this project is 
closely related to the credit limit within the project area. 
Eligible credit areas were delineated in the project by taking a three-step approach (see VCS Method 
VM0007 Module X-STR, Section 5.4 for more details). The steps are as follows: 
Step 1. Successive changes in peat carbon stocks in each formation over 100 years under the 
baseline scenario were calculated. At the same time, the remaining carbon stocks of each layer in the 
project area at t = 100 are obtained;

Part 5: Assessing Baseline Greenhouse Gas Emissions from Terrestrial Carbon Pools from Peatland 
Rewetting and Conservation Projects  
Emissions from microbial decomposition  
The project assumes an annual conversion rate of undrained to drained land based on forest 
conversion of 2.49% under the baseline scenario. Temporal changes in emissions from microbial 
decomposition of peat are directly related to changes in land use and drainage under the baseline 
scenario. 
The CIW activities cover undrained landscapes that will be avoided from drainage under the project. 
Under the baseline scenario, canals will be built and the area will be drained, resulting in greenhouse 
gas emissions from peatland degradation and greenhouse gas emissions from drainage ditches. 
The area of wetland avoided from drainage under the CIW activities starts at 0 hectares and increases 
to 6,583 hectares. 
The RWE activity encompasses landscapes that are drained at the commencement of the  
project and will continue undrained under the Baseline Scenario. Under the project scenario,  
the drainage canals and ditches will be blocked, and the landscape rewetted. 
The area of RWE activity starts at 22,063 hectares and remains constant.

Emissions from water bodies 
The only type of open water bodies present in the baseline scenario are river and drainage canals, as 
we assumed that the baseline agent would not create ponds and/or lakes. The area of river and canals 
in the baseline scenario was determined based on common practice observed in the adjacent Acacia 
concessions, as well as direct measurements from high-resolution satellite imagery. The water bodies 
were temporally and spatially stratified, as set out in Section 3.2.1.4. In short, primary canals were set 
as existing canals before new conversion, whereas secondary and tertiary canals were to be 
constructed when new canals appeared starting in Year 1 and when converting an area into a planting 
zone, respectively. IPCC does not provide default emission factors for CO
2
 and CH
4
 emissions from 
water bodies, thus we used the default values for dissolved organic carbon (∆ DOC).

Emissions from peat burning 
According to Module E-BPB, GHG emissions from biomass burning can result from:  
1. Conversion of forest land to non-forest land using fire;  
2. Periodic burning of grassland or agricultural land after deforestation;  
3. Controlled burning in forest land remaining forest land;  
4. Uncontrolled fire in drained peat swamp forest; and   
5. Uncontrolled peat burning in (abandoned) drained peat sites. 
The project assumed that there was no controlled peat burning during plantation activities, as burning 
to clear forests on Acacia plantations is illegal. Therefore, the project only accounted for emissions from 
unintentional or uncontrolled burnings in the baseline scenario. In addition, the project omitted AGB 
lost by combustion.  
The Project is a rewetting project with historical incidents of uncontrolled (unplanned) burning. The 
baseline fire frequency and impact are assessed using annual historic data in the project area (in case 
of rewetting projects) over a period of minimum 10 to maximum 15 years ending 1 years before the 
project start date. The map below shows fire hotspot from 2013 until 2023 within and around project 
areas (Map 3-18). 
The fire risk assessment incorporates several key environmental and anthropogenic factors that 
influence peatland fire susceptibility. These factors include land use and land cover (LULC), distance to 
settlements and roads, depth to the water table, and topographic features such as elevation and slope. 
Particular attention was paid to the water table depth because dry peatlands are more susceptible to 
fires, which can lead to large-scale carbon emissions. 
Historical burned area data from MODIS were analyzed to map and assess fire patterns in peatland 
areas over the past decade (2014-2023). The data showed that fires were often concentrated in 
degraded peatlands or areas where human activities were prevalent. 
To further quantify fire risk, the Analytical Hierarchy Process (AHP) was used to rank the importance of 
various factors that influence peatland fire susceptibility. These rankings were incorporated into a 
weighted overlay analysis to create a fire risk susceptibility map for the project area. In addition, a 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
137 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
Python-based fire risk simulation model was implemented to predict the potential burned area over a 
100-year period. 
Historical burnt area data were downloaded from the Google Earth Engine Terra and Aqua combined 
MCD64A1 version 6.1 burnt area product. The original spatial resolution of this dataset is 500 meters, 
and data from 2014 to 2023 were analyzed. By counting the number of pixels, we obtained the burned 
area (in hectares) for each year. The analysis results are summarized as follows: (i) the burned area 
was the highest in 2014, with 86052 pixels burned, equivalent to 7744.68 hectares; (iii) the burned 
area decreased sharply in 2015 and 2016, with 4177 pixels (375.93 hectares) burned in 2015 and 
13497 pixels (1214.73 hectares) burned in 2016; (iii) no burned area was found in 2017. The average 
area burned during the ten-year period was 1024.05 hectares, while the median area burned was 
210.20 hectares. The trimmed mean (excluding extreme values) was 311.97 hectares, and the 
standard deviation of the data set was 2267.66 hectares. Approximately 2.95% of the total project area 
was burned during the entire study period. 
 
Map 3-18 Fire probability map 
Meanwhile, if a plot had no hotspot records in 2006-2015 and was randomly selected in the first 
simulation, it was labelled as “Catch Burn 1.” If it had a hotspot record and was randomly selected 
once in 100 years, it was labelled as “Catch Burn 2,” and so on. The peat burn baseline will be 
reassessed every 10 years based on observations of the frequency and extent of burns in reference 
areas and/or based on the latest scientific findings on “recurring burn” patterns. 
The calculation results show that the annual burn rate is 2.95%

3.2.1.4 Baseline emissions from ARR 
The change in carbon stocks in the baseline scenario is calculated by applying the baseline performance 
benchmark value to the estimate of CO
2
 removals (see Section 3.1.4). The performance benchmark is 
defined as the increase in vegetation storage under normal circumstances relative to the project, and is 
set based on data obtained from representative control plots outside the project area through remote 
sensing and spatial analysis. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
144 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
To measure future baseline emissions, we will use statistical methods to compare the changes in the 
vegetation storage index between the project area and the control plots. The detailed performance 
benchmark is explained in Section 3.1.4. 
Ex-ante estimation for the baseline scenario 
As described in Section 3.1.4.2, the project plots are gridded on a 100 x 100-meter grid with an average 
plot size of 1 hectare. The control plots are gridded on a 100 x 100-meter grid. Both the control and 
project plots will be combined with the NDFI dataset to calculate a stocking index for each plot. This 
stocking index will be used to estimate an ex-ante performance baseline based on the values of ∆SI
control 
and ∆SI
wp
. 
During the ex-ante estimation process, the performance baseline must be estimated 10 years after the 
start of the project in accordance with VM0047 and then extrapolated to cover the 40-year crediting 
period and the full project life cycle estimation. According to VM0047, the derivation of ∆SI
wp
 in the ex-
ante estimation requires the project to use a regression model to establish the relationship between the 
stocking index and total carbon (tC) in biomass. For the control plots, the project must use a regression 
model based on the historical performance of the stocking index to generate ∆SI
control
. 
Based on ex ante estimates, the performance benchmarks for the period t0 to t40 are shown in the 
table3-26 below. 
Table3-26 Performance benchmark for the period t0 to t40 
Year T PB
t
 
2024-2033 
0-10 0 
2034-2043 
11-20 0 
2044-2053 
21-30 0 
2054-2063 
31-40 0 
3.2.2 Project Emissions (VCS, 3.15) 
3.2.2.1 General Procedures and Assumptions 
Future emissions from the project area (including any changes in emissions) will depend in part on site-
specific data. In the absence of site-specific data, the necessary calculations can rely on IPCC default 
emission factors, proxy analyses, and any national greenhouse gas allometric model based on 
published scientific data. 
Regarding the peatland rewetting aspect of the project scenario, calculations only consider emissions 
from legacy canals. The project scenario does not include peat drainage and peat trenches from oil 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
145 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
palm canals. The assumption in the project scenario is that canals opened for oil palm cultivation can 
be avoided. 
Also, in the project scenario, it is assumed that no uncontrolled burning will occur in the project area 
during the project period, as rewetting and fire prevention are carried out, and therefore no greenhouse 
gas emissions are expected. Nonetheless, any emissions caused by fires will be accounted for within 
the broader fire management activities (using VMD0013 (E-BPB) version 1.3) based on the established 
fire monitoring plan. 
As no fertilizer is applied in the project, N2O emissions are not considered. Additionally, if the water 
table is within 20 cm of the surface, the rewetting process of organic soils rapidly reduces N2O 
emissions to almost zero as the water table rises with depth. CH4 emissions may be higher for a brief 
period after the rewetting activity ends before stabilizing to levels seen in undrained or never drained 
areas. CH4 emissions from rewetting activities at the relevant formation sites will be directly monitored 
from the project start date. Once sufficient data has been collected from these sites, methane 
emissions will be reassessed and any changes will be considered in the quantification of greenhouse 
gas emissions based on site-specific data. 
For areas designated as “drained” or “undrained” at the project start date (see above), the 
annual rewetting effect is defined as a proxy measure calculated as the ratio of the annual groundwater 
level rise from the initial groundwater level to the maximum total groundwater level increase. This ratio 
is the basis for adjusting the emission factor. The hydrological model estimates that the maximum 
groundwater level will be reached in the fifth year since rewetting. Therefore, all “drained” areas are 
expected to become “undrained” after this time. 
At the same time, the project will collect site-specific hydrological monitoring data to predict spatially 
and temporally accurate estimates of groundwater levels and flows under the project scenario. 
Sufficient direct measurement data from the sites will serve as a proxy for calculating GHG emissions. 
For strata with the most dynamic groundwater levels (i.e., rewetting strata that were drained at project 
start-up), rewetting effects may change. 
If any changes occur, non-forest strata will be restratified using a land cover stratification approach 
based on updated land cover classification, change detection from remote sensing imagery, and site-
specific emission proxies. Strata with less dynamic groundwater levels (i.e., undrained forest strata at 
project start-up) will not be restratified (unless significant changes in emission characteristics are 
observed), and the GHG quantification approach remains unchanged. 
3.2.2.2 Project Emissions Modelling Approach  
Separate Project emissions calculations have been prepared for four separate Project Activities:  
1. REDD - Restoration of natural forest with avoiding planned deforestation.  
2. CIW – Avoiding wetland degradation with no avoiding planned deforestation.  
3. RWE – Restoration of wetland Ecosystems.   

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
146 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
4. ARR – Afforestation, Reforestation, Revegetation 
All the areas developed into plantations under the baseline are protected under the respective Project 
Activity. Existing legacy canals will be blocked in the first year of operations and the drained areas 
rewetted.  
For each stratum in the Project Scenario has been subdivided into the smallest parcel units of land or 
water body and have relatively uniform spatial variables. Temporal variability for Project emissions is 
accounted for by organizing calculations into discrete timing 1-year units. The table below outlines each 
of the following: 
● Variables that determine strata change sequences,   
● Temporal variability of parameters for GHG emissions, and   
● Temporal restrictions on GHG emissions.  
This schematization provides the assurance that GHG emission parameters at the correct spatial 
location and the correct time have been appropriately used.

3.2.2.3 Emissions calculations for REDD  
Project emissions are defined as emissions from biomass combustion, fossil fuel combustion, and 
fertilizer use. According to Indonesian regulations, there are no potential emissions from biomass 
combustion as the government prohibits burning activities in forest areas. Therefore, emissions from 
biomass combustion are conservatively set to zero in the ex-ante. However, forest fires may occur 
during the implementation of project activities. In this case, emissions from biomass combustion will be 
considered and monitored. 
Meanwhile, emissions from fossil fuel combustion and emissions from nitrogen fertilizer application are 
conservatively excluded in the baseline scenario and will also be excluded in the ex-ante and ex post 
scenarios of the project scenario. 
Based on the above explanations, the ex-ante estimate of net greenhouse gas emissions in the project 
scenario (∆C
WPS-REDD
) results in a value of 0. In the ex-post scenario, emissions from deforestation, 
degradation, and natural disturbances will be monitored.

Note under the Project Scenario, it is assumed that no fires will occur. The area of peat burnt and the

contributor to net GHG emissions from CIW will be GHG emissions from:  
• Microbial decomposition of peat soil  
• Water bodies  
The net GHG emissions from WRC under the Project Scenario for CIW will be zero. The net GHG 
emissions from WRC under the Project Scenario for RWE will be calculated accordingly until the 
rewetting is conducted in the first year – in practice this means there are no net emissions prior to RWE 
start date.
