Emissions from Uncontrolled Peat Burning   
Uncontrolled burning is assumed not to be present under the Project Scenario, since rewetting of 
peatland and best-practice fire management (such as fire control and prevention, zero burning policies, 
and others as determined by the relevant authorities) are implemented as Project Activities.   
Since the Project Start Date, the Project Proponent has maintained a comprehensive Fire Management 
Plan with respect to the Project. Should uncontrolled burning occur during the Project lifetime, any area 
of fire scares and burn scar depth should be mapped within three months of the fire event. Historical 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
152 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
hotspot tracking and direct observation data will determine the repetition of burning events in the 
Project Area, with maps generated of burning areas during the Project lifetime. Deductions from 
emissions reductions will be undertaken for equivalent and quantified GHG emissions from 
uncontrolled burning.  
GHG emissions from peat burning will be calculated considering:  
• dry mass loss based on burn scar,  
• burn depths,  
• combustion factors,  
• peat bulk density, and  
• GHG potential of specific GHG species.  
GHG emissions from the loss of biomass because of burning are quantified based on:  
• type of land cover,   
• combustion factors, and   
• GHG potential of specific GHG species.  
Procedures for the quantifying of peat burning GHG emissions are undertaken in line with the VCS 
methodology VM0007 and Module VMD0013 E-BPB.  
Emissions reductions from peat combustion, using fire reduction premium.  
Uncontrolled burning is assumed not to be present under the Project Scenario (or the Baseline 
Scenario for that matter), since rewetting of peatland and best-practice fire management (such as fire 
control and prevention, zero burning policies, and others as determined by the relevant authorities) 
are implemented as Project Activities. The emissions from this source from peat combustion are 
assumed to be zero.

3.2.2.5 Emissions calculations for ARR 
The project will promote forest restoration through forest planting in eligible ARR project areas in 
accordance with methodology VM0047. Forest restoration refers to activities aimed at increasing 
forest cover, structure, function and/or species composition. The project plans to plant 
Dipterocarpaceae species in 2,036 hectares of eligible non-forest areas, with a specific planting plan 
of 400 hectares per year and a planting density of 625 trees per hectare. Ex ante estimates of tree 
species to be used in project activities are tentative and will be adjusted during implementation based 
on field conditions, community consensus and availability of seedlings in the field. 
The project's ARR activities are divided into 5 planting phases, covering a total eligible area of 2,036 
hectares. Table 3-31 below provides detailed information on the phased planting of ARR activities.

Because the project's planting activities occurred in non-forested areas within the project area, the

fire that occurred in the area in 2015, which was the result of natural disturbance and had nothing to 
do with the project proponent or the landowner. 
According to VM0047, the project description must include an ex-ante estimate of emission removals. 
This requires an estimate for 10 years from the verification time t to t+10. However, in this project, the 
estimate was extended to the entire crediting period (40 years). The ex-ante estimate took into 
account several parameters, including DBH, volume per tree, AGB, and BGB to calculate emission 
removals from ARR activities. The carbon stocks estimated for this project based on these parameters 
were derived from several growth models from scientific papers, and the IPCC. The detailed growth

3.2.2.6 Future approaches for calculating Project emissions 
For determining GHG and carbon fluxes resulting from microbial decomposition of peat under the 
Project Scenario, different approaches will be used in the future. Within the Project lifetime, site 
specific measurement approaches (IPCC TIER 2 and TIER 3) will be conducted, and data collected to 
minimize uncertainties in estimated emissions in relation to water table temporal and spatial 
variations. This will enable the gathering of site-specific data sets for quantifying Project emissions for 
all non-forest strata and strata with the most dynamic water table. For stratum that remains 
unaffected by deforestation and drainage, the IPCC factors approach will be applied throughout the 
Project lifetime since water table depths are less dynamic unless substantial changes in emission 
characteristics are observed.  

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
169 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
In the future, estimates of Project emissions will be calculated using two TIER 3 methods. As these 
methods are complementary, they can be used effectively for minimizing uncertainty and allow cross-
checking.  
● Method 1: Monitoring soil subsidence - over the long-term, soil subsidence provides a reliable proxy 
for estimation of peat soil carbon losses.  
● Method 2: Measuring direct CO
2
 emissions (CH
4
 to follow) - data will be used to establish empirical 
models specific to site and strata by combining proxies such as soil temperature, soil moisture, and 
water table depth.  
Water table depths and soil subsidence must be established and operated through the Project lifetime 
over the next 40 years.  
3.2.3 Leakage Emissions (VCS 2.5, 3.2, 3.6, 3.15, 4.3) 
According to VM0007 and VM0047, leakage shall be considered for all project activities using the 
following  
• leakage modules, including:  
• Planned deforestation / degradation: module LK-ASP  
• Unplanned deforestation / degradation: module LK-ASU  
• Fuelwood/charcoal collection: module LK-DFW  
• Ecological leakage: module LK-ECO  
• Estimation of emissions from market-effects: module LK-ME 
• Estimating leakage from ARR activities: Module VMD0054 
Module LK-ASP is not relevant as the project does not avoid planned deforestation. 
Modules BL-DFW and LK-DFW are not relevant as there is no history of unsustainable fuelwood 
collection before the project. 
The baseline activity is defined as drainage of peatlands resulting from (i) unplanned deforestation 
and (ii) continued development of acacia plantations and illegal oil palm cultivation. Therefore, the 
project is classified as Avoided Deforestation (AUD) combined with Undrained and Partially Drained 
Peatland Protection (CUPP) and Rewetting of Drained Peatlands (RDP) activities. 
Potential sources of leakage emissions include:  
• Transfer of unplanned deforestation activities;  
• The displacement of pre-project agricultural activities on non-forest land, and;  
• The displacement of fuelwood collection activities, and;  
• Ecological leakage due to possible alterations of mean annual water table depth in adjacent 
areas.   
• Diverted leakage due to ARR activities 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
170 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4

3.2.3.2 Estimation of Emissions from Ecological Leakage (LK-ECO)  
The applicability of VM0007 module LK-ECO requires that no ecological leakage will occur that affects 
the soil (peat) carbon pool. Ecological leakage is not expected to occur in the project scenario through 
a robust design and implementation plan to block the existing canals by constructing compacted peat 
dams. 
The project will monitor the effectiveness of rewetting activities to confirm that no ecological leakage 
has occurred during each monitoring event. Monitoring of ecological leakage will be carried out by 
installing monitoring wells near the canal blockage locations within and at the boundaries of the 
project area. 
3.2.3.3 Leakage Estimation from ARR Activity 
Emissions from leakage are accounted by applying VMD0054 V1.0 Module for Estimating Leakage 
from ARR activities. This module accounts for leakage related to the displacement of pre-project 
agricultural activities. The module proposes a stepwise methodology to determine the leakage that are 
summarized in the next steps:  

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
173 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
a. Step 1: Determine foregone production in Project Area  
b. Step 2: Determine the impact of leakage mitigation activities 
c. Step 3: Determine amount of new land brought into production 
d. Step 4: Determine change in carbon stocks in new lands brought into production 
e. Step 5: Determine leakage emissions 
Step 1: Determine Foregone Production in Project Area   
The baseline of agricultural and firewood commodity production in the project area must be 
demonstrated using historical data production records for each commodity during the reference 
period. 
Based on the field survey assessment and land use/land use age analysis, the main commodity 
planted by the community is: oil palm. However, since the ARR project activities do not count the 
planted palm oil area, it is not expected that there will be any displacement of palm oil outside the 
project area. 
Also, within the project area, communities in remote villages rely mainly on firewood as household 
energy due to lack of access to government subsidized LPG. However, firewood is not planted and 
commercialized, but comes from remaining fallen tree trunks and existing branches. Since there is no 
formal firewood production, it is not expected to cause firewood substitution. 
Also in the project proposal, the ARR activities will retain the existing rice fields and 100 hectares of 
open space for rice production. Therefore, it is not expected that there will be any displacement of rice 
fields within the project area. 
Result of Step 1:  No foregone production identified in project area. 
Step 2: Determine the Impact of Leakage Mitigation Activities  
The project proponent may implement activities outside the project area that reduce leakage in 
designated leakage mitigation areas. However, since the result of step 1 shows no foregone 
production identified in project area, step 2 is no longer applicable. Furthermore, leakage mitigation is 
optional.   
Result of step 2: The impact of leakage mitigation activities is not accounted for.  
Step 3: Determine Amount of New Land Brought into Production  
Since the displaced production of paddy, as determined in Step 1, is zero, no new land is expected to 
be brought into production outside the project area.   
Result of step 3: The amount of new land brought into production is zero.  
Step 4: Determine Change in Carbon Stocks in New Lands Brought into Production  

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
174 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
Since the amount of new land brought into production is zero (as determined in step 3), this step is no 
longer applicable. Consequently, the change in carbon stocks in the new land that is brought into 
production is also zero.   
Result of step 4: Change in carbon stock in new land brought into production is zero.  
Step 5: Determine Leakage Emissions  
Since there is no new land brought into production, and no change in carbon stocks, leakage 
emissions are consequently also zero.  
Result of step 5: Leakage emissions are zero.   
In line with the ex-ante scenario, ARR leakage emissions are considered as zero demonstrated by the 
steps above. Nevertheless, the ARR leakage shall be monitored in the ex-post scenario to 
accommodate possible leakage occurrence. The detail ARR leakage monitoring plan is available in 
section 3.3.3.

3.2.4.6  Uncertainty Analysis  
Per VMD0017 (X-UNC), uncertainties were calculated for the project’s REDD and WRC components in 
both the Project and baseline scenarios.  
Uncertainty in REDD estimates 
According to the X-UNC module, the project's baseline deforestation rate is based on a long-term 
average, so the uncertainty in the project's baseline deforestation rate is zero. The total uncertainty of 
carbon stocks and greenhouse gas sources in the REDD baseline is calculated as 10.75%. Therefore, 
the cumulative uncertainty in the REDD baseline scenario is 10.75%. As no ex-post (re)measurements 
of carbon stocks or greenhouse gas sources are made, the uncertainty in the REDD project scenario is 
set to zero according to the X-UNC module. 
Uncertainty in WRC estimates 
To calculate the uncertainty in WRC-Peat emissions in the baseline and project scenarios, the project 
used the standard errors of the default factors provided by the IPCC (IPCC Wetlands Supplement 
2013). The uncertainty in CH4 emissions was set to zero because CH4 emissions were conservatively 
ignored in both the baseline and project scenario calculations. The uncertainty in uncontrolled peat 
burning in the project scenario was also set to zero because it was assumed that no fires would occur 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
184 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
after the project was initiated. The cumulative uncertainty in WRC-Peat in the baseline and project 
scenarios was calculated as 6.63% and 1.41%, respectively. The cumulative total of all WRC emissions 
was 6.63% for the baseline scenario and 1.41% for the project scenario. 
Total error in the REDD+ project activity is equal to the combined uncertainty in baseline and project 
estimates for the REDD and WRC activities and was calculated to be 12.71%. The value is below the 
allowable threshold of 15% as stated in the VM0007 REDD+MF methodology, therefore no VCU 
deduction is necessary. 
Uncertainty in ARR estimates 
Uncertainty in area estimation is assumed to be zero and is addressed via complete (and accurate) 
GIS boundaries of the project area, and by applying QA/QC procedures specified in the parameter 
table for A. The performance benchmark is assumed to have zero uncertainty. Uncertainty is 
calculated by propagating errors associated with estimates of included pools as: 
 
   
According to VM0047 v1.0, for ex-ante estimations the project shall use a minimum uncertainty 
deduction of 10 percent.  
3.2.4.7  Calculation of the VCS non-permanence risk buffer withholding  
The combined non-permanence risk buffer for the project was determined as 14%. Per VCS 
methodology VM0007, the annual buffer withholding for all activities was determined as a percentage 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
185 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
of the total carbon stock benefits which excludes emissions due to leakage. As the project does not 
account for emissions from fossil fuel combustion, direct N
2
O emissions and emissions from biomass 
burning were also omitted from calculations.

3.2.4.8  Calculation of Verified Carbon Units 
The Verified Carbon Units (VCU) are calculated as per project equation from equation 18 of VM0007 
REDD+ MF. 
 
The calculation range for the baseline scenario and the project scenario is the calendar year from 
January 1 to December 31. The calculation range for VCU is from the project start date (January 1, 
2024) to the project end date (December 31, 2063):

Data / parameter Project Forest Cover Monitoring Map  
Data unit Dimensionless 
Description Map showing the location of forest land within the project area at the 
beginning of each monitoring period. If within the Project Area some 
forest land is cleared, the benchmark map must show the deforested 
areas at each monitoring event   
Source of data Remote sensing in combination with GPS data collected during ground 
truthing  
Value applied 0 
Justification of choice of 
data or description of 
measurement methods 
and procedures applied 
The minimum map accuracy must be 90% for the classification of 
forest/non-forest in the remote sensing imagery.  
If the classification accuracy is less than 90% then the map is not 
acceptable for further analysis. More remote sensing data and ground 
truthing data will be needed to produce a product that reaches the 
90% minimum mapping accuracy.  
Frequency: Must be monitored at least every 5 years or if verification 
occurs on a frequency of less than every 5 years examination must 
occur prior to any verification event 
 Purpose of data Calculation of forest cover 
Comments Where forest land contains more than one forest class, the map must 
be stratified into forest classes using module X-STR. Parameter is from 
M-REDD

Information System (GIS), which allows for integrating data from 
different sources (including GPS coordinates and remote sensing 
data). Applied techniques must follow international standards of 
application or local standards as laid out in pertinent scientific 
literature or handbooks. The area of peat burnt (A
peatburn-WPS,i,t
 for the 
project scenario from Module M-PEAT and A
peatburn-BSL,i,t

scenario from Module BL-PEAT) and area of peatland (not open water, 
not burnt) (A
peatsoil-WPS,i,t 
for the project scenario from Module M-PEAT 
and A
peatsoil-BSL,i,t
 for the baseline scenario from Module BL-PEAT) 
determine the difference between the remaining carbon stock in the 
project scenario and baseline scenarios after 100 years.

Data / parameter Depth
peat-BSL,i,t0
 
Data unit m 
Description Average peat depth in the baseline scenario in stratum i at project 
start 
Source of data Existing peat depth maps and/or field assessment and/or in 
combination with remote sensing data. 
Value applied N/A For specific values, see section 3.2.1.3 
Justification of choice of 
data or description of 
measurement methods 
and procedures applied 
Procedures for monitoring peat depth are given in Module M-PEAT and 
in this module. Peat depths can be derived from • Existing peat depth 
maps • Literature datasets involving the project or similar areas. • 
Field measurements, e.g., using a peat corer Remote sensing to derive 
height of the peat surface above datum. For the purpose of 
determining the PDT, where relevant, peat depth may be determined 
as the depth of the peat layer down to a level where no further 
oxidation or other losses occur (e.g., the average water table depth). 
 Purpose of data Calculation of baseline emissions 
Comments In the absence of peer-reviewed data sources, the project proponent 
must justify that the data used are representative and that standard 
methods have been used. Parameter from module X-STR

3.3.3 Monitoring Plan (VCS, 3.16, 3.20) 
3.3.3.1 Monitoring Methods 
The Project is required to undertake periodic monitoring of the Project and leakage area.  
The Project Monitoring Reporting and Verification (MRV) system uses a dynamic system that seeks to 
identify, assess, and create a mitigation plan for potential risks that might arise concerning the 
Project. The system is established to ensure a process for monitoring Project progress and 
documenting lessons learned or corrections that may be needed and incorporating them into Project 
decision-making for future monitoring periods.  

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
209 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
The Project Proponent has a set of procedures and systems in place that regulate the management of 
documents, the procedures for verification and validation, etc. The company has developed quality 
assurance and quality control (QA/QC) systems. These procedures will establish how the company will 
handle complaints or non-conformities, etc.   
The MRV Plan will be prepared during the project implementation; it is based on 10 main activities:  
1. Remote Sensed Monitoring – Natural Disturbances/Anthropogenic Disturbances  
2. Field Monitoring  
a. AGB C Stock   
b. Fire Hotspots and Burnt Areas  
c. Peat Water Table and Subsidence  
d. GHG Emissions  
3. Leakage Monitoring  
4. Uncertainty Monitoring  
5. Non-Permanence Risk Assessment  
6. Quality Assurance/ Quality Control  
7. Training   
8. Data and Document Management  
9. VCU Calculations  
10. Stakeholder Access to Online Selected Documents  
3.3.3.2 MRV Plan Content  
Remotely Sensed Monitoring   
Remote sensing approach will be used to monitor land cover change and burnt areas (if any fire 
incidences occur within the project area) and will be conducted annually. Satellite imageries with 
resolution of ≤ 30 meters (e.g., Planet Lab, Sentinel-1, Sentinel-2, ALOS/PALSAR, etc.) with cloud 
cover <10% will be used.  Any deforested areas within the project area and project zone will be 
groundtruthed by the field team by directly visiting the location and/or using drones if the location is 
inaccessible. Overall classification accuracy is targeted at >90%.  
Field Monitoring  
a. AGB C Stock 
To monitor the C stock within the project area, the project will establish 20 permanent 
vegetation/biomass plots in the same locations that were used for the baseline assessment. The plots 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
210 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
will be monitored annually for tree growth (DBH). To estimate the C stock from DBH, we will use the 
same allometric equations that were used when determining the baseline AGB C stock. DBH will be 
the main parameter measured in the field and species specific allometric equations will be used to 
estimate the C stock (in accordance with the species that will be planted). Field measurement will be 
conducted annually. In addition, the project will create a logbook that records the number of seedlings 
planted by species, survival rate, DBH, and height for active planting activities. 
b. Fire Hotspots and Burnt Areas  
The project will develop a Fire Danger Rating System (FDRS) to serve as an early warning system of 
fire risk in the project area. Automatic Weather Stations (AWS) will be installed on the ground to collect 
climate data that correlate with fire risk (e.g., temperature, precipitation, soil moisture, and peat water 
level) and these data will be supplied into the FDRS to calculate the Peat Fire Vulnerability Index 
(PFVI). The FDRS will then provide a 7-day forecast of fire risk in the project area. The FDRS will 
categorize the fire risk based on PFVI’s value as Safe / Alert / Dangerous / Extreme. 
In the field, the project will monitor fire hotspots through daily monitoring from fire watch tower and 
fire prevention patrols during the dry season when the FDRS rates the fire risk as Alert / Dangerous / 
Extreme. When a fire is reported, the fire patrol team will verify fire hotspots on the ground by directly 
visiting the location and/or using drone if the location is inaccessible. 
The burned area in the project area will be mapped and calculated using high-resolution satellite 
imagery remote sensing methods within one month of the fire. Areas of repeated burning will be 
analyzed using historical burn records. Greenhouse gas emissions from biomass and peat burning will 
be calculated annually according to the E-BPB module. 
c. Peat Water Table and Subsidence  
The project plans to install dip wells and subsidence poles along the planned canal blocks in the 
project area to monitor peat water level and peat subsidence. However, the numbers of monitoring 
stations may be adjusted depending on field conditions. Peat water level will be measured 
automatically (daily) or manually (weekly), while peat subsidence will be monitored manually every 
three months.  
The project implements a detailed approach to monitor peat water levels and subsidence, crucial for 
evaluating its impact on peatland conservation and carbon sequestration. Through both automatic 
(daily) and manual (weekly) measurements of water levels, as well as quarterly manual checks of peat 
subsidence, it captures essential data on hydrology and land stability. This is followed by data analysis 
on peat water levels and subsidence to provide further understanding of this influential factor on 
greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions. Water-level fluctuations in peat, in fact, determine the rate of 
decomposition. Generally, low water levels imply more decomposition, hence higher emissions of 
GHGs. Equally, peat subsidence data provide information on the long-term stability in the peat and 
also the storage of carbon in peatland. Summed with other data points, the project can then very 
accurately infer GHG emissions from peat decomposition in tropical peatlands.  

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
211 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
d. GHG Emissions  
The project is exploring future installation of greenhouse gas (GHG) monitoring equipment aimed at 
providing long-term data on atmospheric GHG flux in peatlands, including carbon dioxide (CO
2
), 
methane (CH
4
), among others. Atmospheric monitoring of GHG flux enhances the understanding on 
the sequestration of carbon from the atmosphere by vegetation and its subsequent integration into 
the soils. The project may leverage atmospheric-based monitoring, potentially employing GHG flux 
towers. 
Leakage Monitoring  
Appropriate production and water table data from the Project Area and Project Zone will be collected, 
analyzed and compiled upon each verification event to estimate activity shifting and ecological 
leakage.  
Uncertainty Monitoring 
Uncertainty linked to the parameters monitored will be automatically calculated using excel models. 
Non-Permanence Risk Assessment 
The non-permanence risk tool will be reviewed upon each verification event and updated with respect 
to any relevant data collected.  
Quality Assurance/ Quality Control 
The Project will implement a rigorous QA/QC system to ensure the long-term accuracy of the data that 
is collected, to ensure a robust data storage system and to create a systematic data management 
structure. This system will be reviewed annually to guarantee the accuracy of the MRV program. 
Training  
All new personnel participating in PSPs inventory/control, water table monitoring, etc. will be trained in 
following PSP Inventory/Control/Monitoring Procedures specifications and standard Project practices, 
as much as is reasonably possible. Training will be provided to ensure consistent data collection of all 
monitoring equipment and techniques.  
Data and Document Management 
All data and documents are reviewed and controlled, managed, and stored in a manner that complies 
with standard Project practices, or as much as is reasonably possible. Data will be stored and 
managed following the Project’s data management procedures and will be kept for at least 10 years.  
VCU Calculations  
For each verification event, VCU vintage is estimated and updated based on the parameters 
monitoring results.  
Stakeholder Access to Online Selected Documents 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
212 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
The role of stakeholders is significant for contributing to the performance of PSPP project. Having 
access to selected monitoring documents would help stakeholders to participate in the monitoring 
platform. The Project will report to stakeholders at least annually, including the local community 
through annual reporting which will be available online. The PSPP Community teams will also provide 
reporting directly to the local communities.  
3.3.3.3 Afforestation, Reforestation and Restoration Monitoring (ARR)  
Quantification Approach  
As the project is using the area-based quantification approach, data will be measured using a 
combination of direct on-the-ground measurements and remote sensing methods and collated during 
each monitoring period. Direct on-the-ground measurements will be conducted for project plots and 
then combined with remote sensing analysis. Whereas control plots will use remote sensing analysis 
for monitoring. A database for those plots will be maintained and made accessible for monitoring 
purposes. This data will relate to:  
a. A list of project plots including unique IDs, locations, size and configuration and time series of 
stocking index values from t =-10 to t = 0 and t = 0 to time t  
b. A list of control plots including unique IDs (referencing unique ID of corresponding project plot to 
which they are matched), locations, size and configuration, weights, and time series of stocking index 
values from t =-10 to t = 0 and t = 0 to time t  
c. Vegetation Indices (DVI) and time stamps obtained from remote sensing analysis, to derive stocking 
index values. Remote sensing and field measurements will be conducted to obtain all project data and 
key parameters as per VM0047 requirements. All data will be aggregated, recorded and stored in the 
project database. At each monitoring event the data will be reported in the VCS Monitoring Report 
Template. 
Accounting Boundary  
The extent of the accounting boundary monitored is described in section 3.1.3.  
Parameters to be Measured  
The DBH and volume (for ex ante estimation) of trees are calculated using allometric equations 
(Isnaini, Hikmah Nur et al., 2011) and (Ari Ardelina et al., 2011). In the measurement of year t, the 
required variables such as DBH and height (H) of all trees in the designed plot need to be measured. 
DBH should be measured using a diameter tape, tape measure or any other technique that can 
measure accurately. For height (H), it is recommended to use an inclinometer, laser rangefinder or any 
other technique that can measure this variable accurately. 
Data to be collected and data collection techniques 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
213 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
As described in the VCS-ARR methodology, this project uses estimates for the year t=0. In this 
estimate, 1 tier has been developed: afforestation. The stratification scheme for ARR activities for this 
monitoring is based on the afforestation planting design shown in Table 3-30. 
The planting activities under the project implementation will be divided into 5 planting phases, each 
phase will be carried out once a year. According to the project planting design, the afforestation 
density is 625 trees/ha. 
The tree growth monitoring will be carried out using sample plots, and the sample plot area should be 
consistent with the proportion of ARR eligible areas. 
The number of sample plots is determined based on the target accuracy of biomass stock estimates 
outlined in VM0047 v1.0. This project will use random or systematic methods to select sample plots to 
ensure accurate representation of project activities. For monitoring purposes, all parameters used to 
calculate biomass stocks in sample plots (e.g., root-to-shoot ratio, wood density) are considered fixed 
constants. Likewise, all models used to calculate biomass stock in the plots (e.g., allometric equations 
for estimating aboveground biomass (AGB)) are considered exact models. The plots are not only used 
to measure biomass stock, but will also be used in analyses along with control plots to estimate 
performance benchmarks. Referring to Section 3.3.2, the performance benchmark assessment will 
combine field measurements and remote sensing analysis, using the Normalized Difference Fraction 
Index (NDFI) as a stock index based on satellite imagery. 
Monitoring Frequency 
Project implementation monitoring will be conducted every two years after project registration or prior 
to verification activities to ensure continuity of benefits. Regular verification and quantitative 
monitoring of the project will not exceed five years. 
Schedule and procedures for regular acquisition, archiving and processing of remote sensing data to 
derive resource index 
Monitoring activities will be scheduled every five years during the project crediting period. In order to 
derive the resource index and assess the performance benchmark in each monitoring activity, 
seasonal conditions around the project area must be considered. Monitoring should be conducted 
during the dry season to obtain more consistent data and avoid large amounts of cloud cover on 
satellite imagery. 
The following steps will be taken for each monitoring: 
a. Review and remove any control plots that are considered invalid because their location no longer 
matches the project area. If a control plot is considered invalid, it will be excluded from the selected 
donation pool and replaced. 
b. Find satellite imagery (e.g. Landsat, Sentinel) acquired during the dry season, perform 
quality control on the imagery, obtain NDFI values, and calculate the average NDFI (stock 
accumulation index) based on time series data for each project and control plot. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
214 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
c. Perform a linear regression of the accumulation index to obtain and evaluate the slopes: ∆SIcontrol 
and ∆SI
wp,t
 
d. Test the significance of the difference between ∆SI
control
 and ∆SI
wp,t
 
e. Derive the performance benchmark. 
Detailed description of the stocking index and its derivation process 
Description of the process of obtaining the stocking index using satellite images: 
a. Satellite image archives (e.g. Landsat, Sentinel) are explored to find suitable data. Orthophoto 
surface reflectance products, combined with atmospheric and radiometric corrections, will be used to 
derive remote sensing indicators. 
b. For each monitoring, satellite images taken during the dry season are selected to reduce cloud 
cover. 
c. To ensure the inclusion of high-quality pixels, the input images will be carefully masked to exclude 
cloud, shadow or saturated pixels. 
d. For each selected image, the NDFI (Normalized Difference Fraction Index) value is calculated, and 
from the set of NDFI images obtained in the previous step, two NDFI composites will be generated: 
one for the current monitoring year and the other for the middle year between the current monitoring 
year and the previous monitoring year (t=0 in case of the initial monitoring period). The creation of 
these composites will use the median method, which combines multiple individual NDFI images into 
one composite image by determining the median pixel value at each corresponding pixel location. 
f. NDFI (stocking index) values will be calculated for each control and project plot to derive and assess 
the slope of the stocking index time series. 
g. If SI values are not available for plots in the matched set at time t (e.g. due to cloud cover or 
temporary sensor problems), the matched set of project and control plots shall not be used to derive ∆
SI
control
 and ∆SI
wp,t
 for each time t. To derive ∆SI
control
 and ∆SI
wp,t
, the slopes of the weighted linear 
regressions of the cumulative time series of SI values for the corresponding groups of plots shall be 
calculated. The weights for SI control and project plots in the time series are calculated using 
Equations A3 and A4 of VM0047:

Data Collection and Management Process  
The project’s data collection and management process ensure the integrity of data from field 
measurements to the final calculation of carbon stock. This is achieved through systematic steps that 
transform raw data into meaningful information, with integration into a Relational Database 
Management System (RDBMS). The following outlines the core components of the process:  
1. Field Data Collection Field data collection is performed by teams utilizing various tools such as GPS 
devices, biomass measurement tools, remote sensing datasets, and other tools.  
2. Parameter Processing The raw data collected is processed into specific parameters. The processed 
data is prepared for entry into a structured system, where each piece of information is associated with 
an identifiable parameter.  
3. Assignment of Unique IDs Each parameter is assigned a unique identifier (ID) that serves to classify 
and track data systematically. The hierarchy of IDs progresses from general to specific, starting from 
broad elements like a project formula parameters (APD/ARR) ID, parameter ID, grid ID, stratum ID 
(e.g., vegetation type), and location or time-specific IDs. This structure allows for precise tracing and 
referencing of each data point in the database.  
4. Data Verification After assigning IDs, data undergoes a rigorous verification process to ensure 
accuracy and completeness. This step is crucial for maintaining the integrity of the information before 
it enters the database. If the data passes the verification, it proceeds to the next stage. However, if 
discrepancies or issues are found, the data is flagged, and further action is taken:  
5. The team in the field is notified to confirm or correct the data.  
6. Data may need to be re-collected or corrected before it is re-verified.  
7. Data Input into RDBMS Verified data is input into a cloud-based spreadsheet system if collected by 
field teams in project site, or directly into the RDBMS if sourced from satellite data. The integration of 
field data into the RDBMS ensures that the information is securely stored and organized. Tables hold 
the structured data, and relationships between them are established to enable efficient querying and 
analysis.  
8. Data Retrieval for Carbon Stock Calculation The RDBMS is utilized to retrieve and query the stored 
data for carbon stock calculations. This includes pulling relevant information from various tables (e.g., 
biomass and strata) to compute carbon stocks based on established methodologies, such as those 
outlined by Verra Methodologies.  
9. Carbon Stock Calculation Data from the RDBMS is processed using a set of algorithms or formulas 
to estimate carbon stocks for the project area. The calculation incorporates collected data/parameters 
to generate accurate carbon estimates.  
10. Reporting and Dashboard Integration The final carbon stock values are compiled into reports and 
dashboards. The data is displayed through visual tools that allow project managers and stakeholders 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
220 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
to monitor carbon performance, ensuring transparency and accessibility of the project’s carbon 
outcomes. 
3.3.4 Dissemination of Monitoring Plan and Results (VCS, 3.18; CCB, CL4.2) 
For all stakeholders, summaries of project documents, including monitoring plans and results, will be 
shared and accessible through regular coordination, printed documents in project offices and village 
information boards, and online resources through Verra and the project website. 
Specifically for communities, the project documents will be shared through village meetings and 
village information boards. Regular communication will occur during the project lifetime, as the project 
will closely involve the community in the project activities and monitoring. Formally, at least two village 
meetings will be conducted annually to disseminate project activities updates and reports.  
At the same time, the project party will standardize the data through standard operating procedures 
(SOP) and perform multiple quality assurance and quality control (QA/QC) assessments. All collected, 
recorded, stored and reported data are subject to review and approval by the data analyst, data 
manager, technical project leader and project team leader, with reference to data management at 
each level. To ensure the security and traceability of data input and QA/QC, all data at the original, 
intermediate and final stages have predefined access levels for different types of users. Short periodic 
monitoring reports will be generated to evaluate the quality of data received from the field and 
investigations of any potential outliers. Any possible failure of monitoring equipment will be performed 
to ensure that the data passes QA/QC. The climate monitoring plan will be made available for public 
review at the project office. The complete results of the initial climate monitoring included in this 
project document will be publicly available in the project area. In addition, a summary of the project 
document is prepared in English and Indonesian and provided to communities throughout the project 
area. 
3.4 Optional Criterion: Climate Change Adaptation Benefits  
The project area and adjacent communities are vulnerable to climate change impacts. The project will 
implement strategies to help local communities (especially vulnerable groups) and biodiversity adapt 
to climate change. Overall, project interventions are expected to increase the resilience of local 
communities and biodiversity to climate change. The project aims to achieve Gold Level certification to 
gain benefits from climate change adaptation. 
3.4.1 Regional Climate Change Scenarios (CCB, GL1.1) 
Climate change and climate variability scenarios and impacts 
Riau is experiencing significant climate change, with impacts in many ways. According to research, the 
average annual temperature in the region has risen by about 0.5°C to 1°C over the past few decades. 
For example, the average annual temperature in 1990 was 26.5°C, while it reached 27.5°C in 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
221 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
2020
13
. This warming trend has led to changes in precipitation patterns, with precipitation decreasing 
by 10%-20% between 2000 and 2020, for example, annual precipitation in some areas dropped from 
3,000 mm in 2000 to 2,400 mm in 2020
14
. This has been accompanied by an increase in extreme 
weather events, such as an increase in the frequency of droughts and floods. According to the local 
meteorological bureau, the number of dry days experienced by Riau has increased to an average of 30 
days per year. 
In terms of forest cover, the forest cover in Riau has dropped from 60% to about 40% over the past 30 
years. This has resulted in an estimated 200,000 tons of carbon dioxide emissions per year
15
. In 
addition, the rate of sea level rise is about 3 mm/year, posing a particular threat to low-lying areas, 
which may lead to more flooding and soil salinization
16
. 
In terms of biodiversity, the number of endemic species in Riau Province has decreased by about 30% 
in the past 20 years, especially some endangered species such as the Kalimantan orangutan and 
certain birds
17
. The production of major crops such as rice and palm oil has decreased by about 15%-
20% in the past decade. For example, rice production in 2020 was 4 million tons, while it was 5 
million tons in 2010
18
. This has not only affected the livelihoods of local residents, but also put 
pressure on the economy. 
Climate change and climate variability scenarios and impacts 
Climate change will affect the land use scenarios in Riau Province and surrounding communities in 
various ways. The expansion of oil palm plantations in the region is one of the main drivers of 
deforestation and changes in land use, leading to the loss of forest cover, biodiversity, and ecosystem 
services. Changes in temperature, rainfall, and other climatic factors will impact the productivity and 
yield of oil palm plantations as well as other crops grown in the area. For instance, changes in 
precipitation patterns, such as increased rainfall or flooding, may put pressure on the yield and quality 
of crude palm oil (CPO). The effects of climate change will also impact the livelihoods of local 
communities, especially rice farmers and fishermen who rely on natural resources and ecosystem 
services affected by climate change and land use changes. Therefore, in the long term, the impacts of 
climate change may lead to changes in land use, such as adopting alternative crops or increasing 
planted areas. However, in the medium to short term, land use scenarios are unlikely to change 
significantly due to the effects of climate change.

3.4.2 Climate Change Impacts (CCB, GL1.2) 
3.4.2.1 Community well-being 
Livelihoods 
The livelihoods of residents in the project area mainly depend on agriculture, especially oil palm and 
rice. Climate change may lead to higher temperatures and changes in precipitation patterns, which in 
turn affect the productivity of crops. For example, extreme weather events such as floods and 
droughts may damage crops and lead to reduced food production, which will directly affect 53.7% of 
farmers and 25.7% of farm workers. 
Income 
There are 343 poor households (16.68%) in the project area, and the proportion of commercial 
activities is low (6.8%). This shows the vulnerability of the community to economic changes. Climate 
change may exacerbate poverty, especially in the case of unstable climate, the uncertainty of 
agricultural income will increase, affecting farmers' livelihoods, and the economic resilience of the 
community to the impacts of climate change is also limited. 
Education 
The level of education is relatively low, and 74.42% of residents have not received higher education. 
Climate change may affect the availability of education, especially when agricultural production is 
affected, and families may keep children at home to help with labor instead of continuing their 
education.  
Health 
The project area has limited health facilities and relies on community health centers and clinics. 
Climate change may affect the availability and sanitation of water sources. For example, with changes 
in rainfall patterns, clean water sources may become unreliable, leading to water shortages and 
sanitation issues. This situation may exacerbate health risks in the community, especially for children 
and the elderly. 
In summary, the impact of climate change on the project community in Riau Province is multifaceted, 
mainly reflected in the areas of livelihoods, income, education and health. The community's high 
dependence on agriculture, low economic diversity and education level make it vulnerable to climate 
change. Therefore, comprehensive measures are needed to enhance the community's adaptive 
capacity and resilience, such as improving agricultural technology, increasing educational 
opportunities and strengthening health services. 
3.4.2.2 Biodiversity conservation status 
Habitat degradation and loss caused by rising temperatures and changes in precipitation 
Rising temperatures and changes in precipitation patterns caused by climate change pose a serious 
threat to peat swamp forests and mangrove ecosystems. Peat swamp forests rely on stable humidity 
and water levels. As temperatures rise, soil moisture may decrease, leading to vegetation decline. 
Mangrove ecosystems may be affected by rising sea levels and changes in salinity, which directly 
affect species that rely on these habitats, such as Sumatran tigers and Bornean orangutans, whose 
special habitat requirements reduce their adaptability under climate change. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
223 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
Decreased biodiversity and increased risk of species extinction 
According to the Global Biodiversity Outlook, the combination of climate change and human activities 
has put many species at an unprecedented risk of extinction. In the project area, especially for species 
that are highly dependent on specific habitats, such as white-handed gibbons and sambar deer, 
climate change will make their habitats more vulnerable, which may lead to a sharp decline in 
population numbers. In addition, species migration may also lead to the disruption of ecological 
balance, further exacerbating biodiversity loss. 
Changes in species distribution caused by changes in ecosystem dynamics 
As climate conditions change, some species may migrate to more suitable habitats. For example, as 
temperatures rise, tropical species may migrate northward, resulting in a reduction in the living space 
of native species. This migration not only affects the distribution of species, but may also change the 
original food chain and ecological interactions, leading to increased competition and changes in 
predator-prey relationships. These dynamics may affect the structure and function of the entire 
ecosystem and weaken its ability to resist external pressures. 
Damage to ecosystem functions and weakening of ecosystem services 
The decline in biodiversity caused by climate change will directly affect the services provided by 
ecosystems, such as water conservation, soil protection, and climate regulation. These services are 
essential for agriculture, fisheries, and water resource management for local residents. For example, 
peat swamps can effectively absorb carbon dioxide and regulate water levels, but as they degrade, 
this function will be significantly reduced, which will in turn affect the sustainability of agricultural 
production and water resources. 
Impact on community livelihoods 
The livelihoods of local communities mainly depend on agriculture and the use of natural resources. 
The reduction of biodiversity and the weakening of ecosystem services caused by climate change will 
pose a threat to these livelihoods. Farmers may face the risk of reduced crop yields and depletion of 
fishery resources, which will affect household income. In addition, competition for resources within the 
community may intensify, leading to social instability and conflict. This economic pressure may cause 
some residents to further rely on illegal activities (such as poaching) to make a living, forming a 
vicious cycle and further exacerbating the loss of biodiversity. 
Overall, the impact of climate change on biodiversity in the project area in Riau Province is far-
reaching and complex. To meet these challenges, comprehensive conservation measures must be 
taken, including restoring degraded habitats, establishing ecological corridors, enhancing community 
environmental awareness and participation, and formulating policies to address climate change to 
ensure the sustainability of biodiversity and its ecosystem services. This will not only help protect the 
unique local ecological environment, but also provide a more stable livelihood foundation for local 
residents. 
3.4.3 Measures Needed and Designed for Adaptation (CCB, GL1.3) 
As described in the Theory of Change in Section 2.1.17, the project aims to conserve the remaining 
forest, restore ecosystem function, protect biodiversity, and improve the well-being of communities to 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
224 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
become resilient to climate change. Among others, it aims to achieve improved ecosystem quality and 
resilience, biodiversity conservation and protection, and sustainable, resilient livelihoods.  
To achieve these benefits of increased resilience and adaptation of ecosystems, biodiversity, and 
livelihoods to climate change, the project will implement the following measures that will assist 
communities and biodiversity to adapt to the projected climate change impacts. These activities have 
been integrated into the project’s causal model as reflected in its Theory of Change.  
3.4.3.1 Ecosystems and Biodiversity 
Conservation and restoration of habitats of threatened species and their connectivity 
The project will enhance ecosystem resilience and biodiversity by protecting and restoring peatlands. 
These ecosystems naturally regulate hydrological flows, buffer coastal flooding, filter freshwater 
lenses, and reduce fire risk—functions that will become increasingly important under climate change. 
In particular, the project will improve the adaptive capacity of 10 Threatened, Limited, and Protected 
(TTD) species in the project area, such as the Sumatran tiger (Panthera tigris sumatrae), white-
handed gibbon (Hylobates agilis), Sumatran otter (Lutra sumatrana), adjutant stork (Leptoptilos 
javanicus), sambar deer (Cervus unicolor), and pig-tailed macaque (Macaca nemestrina), by 
protecting and increasing habitat connectivity. The project will prevent the continued development of 
acacia industrial plantations in the project area and prevent residents in the project area from clearing 
forests for palm plantations, which will restore degraded ecosystems and habitats. These activities will 
help protect and increase habitat for the endangered Sumatran tiger, white-handed gibbon, Sumatran 
otter, and other threatened species. 
Fire Prevention 
The project will implement various fire prevention and management activities that will help protect 
mangrove and peat ecosystems that provide habitats for endangered species. As part of forest fire 
mitigation, the project proponent will install an Automatic Weather Station (AWS) to measure and 
monitor weather changes such as; precipitation, temperature, soil moisture and water levels, and will 
establish a Fire Danger Rating System (FDRS) for the project’s early warning system. In addition, the 
project will establish a fire patrol team that will work closely with community fire brigades based on a 
patrol program. The project will reactivate these volunteer groups and provide them with the skills 
needed to fight fires. The project will also provide firefighting equipment and fire lookout tower 
infrastructure. 
Biodiversity Conservation and Monitoring 
The project will implement biodiversity conservation and monitoring activities. In particular, it will 
establish patrols and will employ the SMART patrol system to collect and monitor data on the three 
trigger species and general biodiversity in the project area. This will ensure that the project identifies 
any threats to biodiversity, monitors the effectiveness of its conservation strategies, and is able to 
make adjustments as needed and adapt to changes. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
225 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
3.4.3.2 Local Communities 
Livelihood diversification and other adaptive practices  
The project will implement several strategies to diversify livelihoods and income sources and 
implement other adaptive practices related to community livelihoods. The project will develop 
alternative and resilient livelihoods for affected communities, especially vulnerable groups such as 
women and the poor. It is expected that this will enhance the resilience of local communities to 
climate change. Initial options for sustainable livelihood development have been identified in 
consultation with local communities, including agroforestry, honey, bird's nests, duck and chicken 
rearing, and small fish ponds. These activities include training, start-up funding and technical 
assistance. When selecting agroforestry crops in consultation with local communities, the project will 
consider climate change factors based on scientific and local knowledge. For example, flood- and 
drought-resistant tree species will be selected. 
The project will also build on existing adaptive practices at the community level, such as home 
gardens and intercropping in rice fields, which help diversify community livelihoods and food and 
income sources. The project will help increase marketing opportunities for agricultural products. The 
project will support enhanced marketing and market access for agricultural products such as honey 
and bird's nests. This will enable local communities to increase and diversify their sources of income. 
The project will promote sustainable, resilient agricultural practices. Best practices such as crop 
rotation, integrated pest management, agroforestry and tillage management will be introduced. This 
will support the ability of agricultural systems to adapt to accelerating environmental change and will 
increase resilience to drought, pests and diseases, and other climate change impacts. Finally, as part 
of an adaptive management approach, the project will regularly discuss the impacts of climate change 
with local communities and identify additional measures that can be integrated into project activities 
to ensure they are resilient to climate change. 
Farmers and Community Organizations 
The project will contribute to the adaptive capacity of local communities by strengthening community 
organizations such as farmers’ groups and women’s groups. Project proponents will assist 
communities in establishing micro, small and medium enterprises (MSMEs) and women’s groups as 
additional sources of household income. This increased community organization is expected to 
enhance the capacity of local communities to generate, receive and disseminate knowledge and 
information to support appropriate adaptation options and influence their ability to adapt to changing 
shocks, stresses and trends. 
Access to finance 
The project will increase access to financial resources through a revolving fund mechanism to help 
households, especially vulnerable groups, develop sustainable alternative livelihoods and income 
sources. Revolving funds will be provided to local livelihood groups, including women's groups and/or 
micro, small and medium enterprises. The target groups of the program are low-income households, 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
226 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
women's groups and villagers in general. It is expected that these activities will help strengthen the 
resilience of local communities to climate change. 
4 COMMUNITY 
4.1 Without-Project Community Scenario  
4.1.1 Descriptions of Communities at Project Start (CCB, CM1.1) 
Administratively, the project area is located in Sungai Sembilan, Kota Dumai, Riau Province, with the 
geographical coordinates 001º49 ́31 ́ ́N - 002º05 ́29 ́ ́N and 101º03 ́46 ́ ́ E – 101º18 ́04 ́ ́E. The 
project area covers an area of approximately 34,792 hectares. The area is under the supervision of 
the Riau Provincial Forestry Department and mainly belongs to the Sungai Bulu Hala Forest group. It is 
surrounded by several water basins, including DAS Buluhala and DAS Rokan, and is adjacent to the 
Senepis Tiger Reserve. The project area has a humid climate with annual precipitation ranging from 
95 to 418 mm. It is 6 to 25 meters above sea level, with a flat terrain with a slope of 0-8%. The soil is 
mainly organic soil and alluvial soil. The main peat hydrological unit in the area is Gambut (KHG), with 
Rokan River and Siak Kecil River in its watershed. The project area covers three village areas in the 
Negeri Sembilan district of Dumai City, namely Batu Teritip Village, Sungai Geniot Village and Basilam 
Baru Village. 
4.1.1.1 Well-Being Information  
Livelihood  
The livelihood of local villages is mainly based on farming. The total number of households in the 
three villages related to the project area is 2056, and the distribution of various occupations shows 
obvious economic structure characteristics. Farmers and farm workers occupy an absolute dominant 
position, reflecting the economic characteristics of the region dominated by agriculture. Overall, these 
data reflect the economic characteristics of the region dominated by agriculture and low degree of 
occupational distribution diversity. 
A total of 53.7% (1,104 people) of the households surveyed were engaged in agriculture, with palm oil 
being the main product. The second largest occupation was farm worker, accounting for 25.7% (528 
people) of all respondents. 
Oil palm plantations are the main source of income for most people in the study area. The level of land 
management of plantations varies greatly, ranging from 2 hectares to more than 100 hectares. 
The following figure shows the detailed livelihood information of all households.

Income 
There are 343 poor households in the three villages in the project area, accounting for 16.68% of the 
total number of households. There are 1,157 households in the project area that earn income from oil 
palm planting, and their income is relatively high. The second largest group is the families that work 
on the farm, with 955 households. Some of these families earn income by planting oil palm and 
working on the farm at the same time. 
Education 
The education level of residents is relatively low, with 74.42% of residents not receiving higher 
education, mainly concentrated in primary and secondary schools, totalling 1,530 households. Among 
them, 340 people (16.54%) did not complete primary education, 701 people (34.10%) only completed 
primary education, 489 people (23.78%) completed junior high school education, 462 people 
(22.47%) completed high school education, and only 69 people (3.11%) received higher education.

Basilam Baru village. Each village also has a health post (Posyandu). Below is the table of available

The source of clean water in the project area villages is also related to the sanitation of residents.

canals and PAMSIMAS. 
The sources of water for bathing, washing and toileting (MCK) varied widely among the households in 
the three study areas. Most people used water from wells (dug and drilled). The percentage of use was 
89.9% (1,849 households). In general, the quality of the well water in the households of the 
respondents was poor, i.e., it had a foul smell, color, turbid taste, and was not drinkable.

4.1.1.2 Community characteristics 
Culture and Traditions 
There are eight ethnic groups living in the three villages in the project area, but not all ethnic groups 
are distributed in every village. The Javanese have the largest population, followed by the Batak and 
Nias. According to the ethnic population information in Table 4-7, in the study village, the Javanese are 
the absolute majority, with a total of 1,131 people (accounting for 55.00%), the Malays are 589 
(accounting for 28.64%), the Bataks are 281 (accounting for 13.67%), and the rest are Bugis, Minang, 
Bangar, Nias and Chinese. Among them, Sungai Geniot has the largest Javanese population, and the 
number of Malays and Bugis is not much different. In Batu Teritip, the Javanese are also the main 
ethnic group, and the proportion of Batak is also relatively high.

CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
In Batu Teritip village there is a sacred cemetery called the Batu Teritip founder's tomb, located by the 
Senepis River. The community considers this site sacred, but the site is located within the concession 
area of PT. SGP and is in a poor state of maintenance. Regarding traditions and customs, traditional 
activities in Batu Teritip village are still preserved and practiced. One of the important traditions is the 
"village cleaning ceremony" (semah kampung), which prays to Allah SWT to bless and protect the 
village and its people from disasters. The most recent village cleaning ceremony was held in response 
to wild animal attacks on indigenous lands. 
In Sungai Geniot village, traditional activities are still well preserved and practiced. These include 
taboos passed down from generation to generation and the "village cleaning ceremony" (semah 
kampung), which aims to cleanse the village and symbolically purify itself by praying to Allah SWT to 
stay away from disasters and misfortunes. In addition, there is a tradition called "bele kampung" that 
is also passed down from generation to generation. 
In the village of Basilam Baru, there is a historical cemetery, the tomb of the late Sheikh Akhmat 
Kasim. The Sheikh is known as the "village founder" and is the founder of Basilam Baru. He was the 
first person to teach basic religious knowledge and tarekat na'sabandiyah. Due to his teachings, 
Basilam Baru is now considered a place for religious ceremonies (suluk). In order to respect and 
commemorate his contribution, the historical cemetery of Sheikh Akhmat Kasim was established. 
Community members said that this historical cemetery is still regarded by some as a place of prayer, 
not only residents of Basilam Baru come here, but even outsiders come here to express their wishes. 
There are some taboo activities in all three villages in the project area, but the practice of taboos is 
currently decreasing, and these taboos are increasingly seen as irrelevant to modern conditions and 
affected by the limited nature of natural forests. Taboos in Batu Teritip village include: 1) All family 
members must return home before evening prayer; 2) It is forbidden to throw clothes from outside into 
the house, especially for people who have just returned home from get off work; 3) It is forbidden to 
chop wood with a machete; 4) It is forbidden to whistle while singing; 5) It is forbidden to go to the 
fields on Fridays; 6) It is forbidden to fetch water with a pot; 7) It is forbidden to throw turmeric into the 
river. Taboos in Sungai Geniot village include: 1) All family members must return home before evening 
prayer; 2) It is forbidden to curse in the forest; 3) It is forbidden to chop wood with a knife. But Basilam 
Baru village still follows the taboos passed down from generation to generation. These taboos include: 
1) All family members must return home before evening prayer; 2) It is forbidden to throw clothes from 
outside into the house; 3) It is forbidden to chop wood with a knife; 4) It is forbidden to clap while 
singing; 5) It is forbidden to go to the fields on Fridays; 6) It is forbidden to fetch water with a pot. In 
addition, the village cleansing ceremony (semah kampung) is still actively carried out in Basilam Baru 
Village, which is to cleanse the village and the community through prayers, asking for the grace of 
Allah SWT and keeping away disasters. 
Government Structure and Customs  
In terms of social institutions, this is evidenced by the village government, which is headed by a village 
chief and assisted by his staff, namely the secretary, the head of the government section, the head of 
the community empowerment section, and the head of the security and order section. Other social 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
231 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
institutions in Pasir Baru are the Village Community Empowerment Agency (LPMK), the Village 
Enterprise (BUMKel), the Village Economic Enterprise Savings and Loans (UEK-SP) and the PKK 
Driving Team (TP PKK). Then there is also an institution engaged in the cultural field, called the 
"United Malay Cluster". As for the religious field in this area, there is a wirid / recitation group that is 
held on a weekly basis.

4.1.2 Interactions between Communities and Community Groups (VCS, 3.19; CCB, 
CM1.1) 
The social groups described in Section 4.1.1 consist of groups based on ethnicity, religion and 
livelihood. The greatest differences in inter-group relations are based on ethnic groups and livelihood, 
as well as geographical distance between villages. Whether it is the ever-present village of Basilam 
Baru, the immigrant village of Batu Teritip, or the newly expanded village of Sungai Geniot, they are 
mainly Javanese who rely on agriculture for their livelihood. The relations between these groups are 
harmonious and consistent, there is mutual economic cooperation, and the main groups in the three 
villages share common religious beliefs. Other groups not mentioned in 4.1.1 include women's groups, 
youth groups, coffee growers' groups, honey seekers' groups, cattle breeders' groups, merchants' 
groups and religious groups. The women's groups in the villages are the Household Welfare 
Empowerment (Pemberdayaan Kesejahteraan Keluarga, PKK) and the Women Farmers' Group 
(Kelompok Wanita Tani, KWT). PKK is a group of housewives who are partners with the village 
government and are committed to community empowerment, while KWT is a women farmer group 
engaged in agricultural development by women in the village. The KWT is under the guidance of the 
village government and the Ministry of Agriculture. The village youth group is called Karang Taruna, a 
group that works with the village government to assist in youth activities and village ritual activities 
organized by the village government. Religious groups include the recitation group and the mosque 
committee, both of which play a role in religious activities in the village. Some of these groups are 
formally established, while others are informally established. The relationship between the groups is 
mutual cooperation for some joint activities, such as major religious events held in the village. 
4.1.3 High Conservation Values (CCB, CM1.2) 
The project aims to protect and enhance the quality of High Conservation Values (HCVs) to support 
community well-being in the project area. To this end, the project identified areas that provide critical 
ecosystem services (HCV 4), areas that are important for community livelihoods (HCV 5), and sites 
that are important for the community’s traditional cultural identity (HCV 6). These are shown below. 
High Conservation Value HCV 4 - Key Ecosystem Services  
Qualifying Attribute There are two components of HCV 4 that are found in the project 
area: 
HCV 4.1: Water supply and flood protection services for downstream 
communities. 
1.Hydrological Services: The ability of forests to reduce flooding, 
control river flow, and maintain water quality.

2.Water Resource Management: The protection and management of

ecological services. 
3.Community Dependence: The degree of community reliance on 
water resources, especially for drinking water, sanitation, and 
transportation. 
4.Ecological Function Maintenance: The importance of forests and 
habitats within a watershed in maintaining hydrological cycles and 
ecological functions. 
HCV 4.3: Natural barrier services to prevent the spread of forest or 
land fires. 
1.Fire Prevention Function: Original Forest vegetation maintains soil 
moisture and reduces the risk of fire occurrence. 
2.Ecological Integrity: Healthy ecosystems effectively prevent the 
spread of fires. 
3.Vegetation Types: Dense swamp shrubs and mixed plant 
communities are important for fire prevention. 
4.Human Activity Impact: Human activities (such as logging) increase 
fire risks, necessitating effective management to protect ecosystems. 
5.Buffer Zone Importance: The buffer zone (100 meters) between 
forest edges and non-forest vegetation plays a crucial role in fire 
prevention. 
Focal Area Areas covered by HCV 4.1 include: 
1. Riparian forests of major rivers: These areas are within 50 meters 
on either side of the river and serve as buffer zones as required by 
law. 
2. Narrow riparian zones in steep valleys. 
3. Upstream and downstream water catchment areas of the project 
site and surrounding villages. 
The areas covered by HCV 4.3 include: 
1. A buffer zone of 100 m from the forest edge bordering non-forest 
plant species

in sufficient supply in the village market.

not only reflect the local culture, but also carry the traditions and

Baru: This is the tomb of the religious leader who spread Islam and is 
now used as a Rumah Suluk (religious practice place). 
4.1.4 Without-Project Scenario: Community (CCB, CM1.3) 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
235 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
There are already areas of industrial acacia plantations in the PSPP area, and some residents are 
currently cutting down forests in the project area to plant palm trees. Therefore, without this project, 
this peatland ecosystem will continue with previous activities, namely fast-growing acacia plantations 
planted for pulp and paper, and the area of forest cut down by villagers to plant palm trees will 
continue to increase. These two types of activities will have a significant impact on the physical 
conditions of the PSPP area, which in turn will affect the agricultural and fishing communities 
operating within the PSPP, as well as other stakeholders who will be affected downstream by fires and 
deteriorating water quality. This will bring some risks to the community: 1. Increased migration and 
competition due to the presence of industrial plantations: The influx of a large number of migrant 
workers may lead to unhealthy competition among villagers, as companies usually prefer migrants 
with lower wages. This situation will hinder employment opportunities for villagers; 2. Low quality of 
education services: The education system is underfunded and of low quality. Coupled with difficult 
road access and limited government resources, it is unlikely that education will be improved, 
especially without external intervention; 3. Difficult access to medical services: Access to medical 
services is severely limited, and the nearest community health center is two hours away from the 
village. Again, inadequate road access and resources limit improvement efforts; 4. Environmental 
degradation: Issues such as forest fires, illegal logging, and coastal erosion threaten the environment. 
Without project intervention, the community’s ability to sustain livelihoods will be weakened, 
especially rice production, which could lead to a decline in villagers’ resilience and self-sufficiency.

consulted on the issues that may arise from the project. The presence of the project will have a direct 
impact on the livelihood groups that depend on the project area for income.These impacts are 
expected to be mostly positive and will affect the employment of 300 households, including 40% of 
women. As described below. As explained elsewhere, farmers and fishermen will be allowed to 
continue their livelihood activities in the project area.
