4.2.2 Negative Community Impact Mitigation (VCS, 3.19; CCB, CM2.2) 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
237 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
The project aims to provide net benefits for local populations. Nevertheless, potential negative 
community impacts could result from the project. Thus, it is necessary to design a plan to mitigate any 
negative impacts on the well-being of community groups. The potential negative impacts as well as 
their mitigation include the following:  
1) Illegal Loggers: As a result of the project, the local people will no longer be able to log illegally. 
Hence, their income will be reduced. Community members who were conducting these activities for 
their subsistence or main livelihood will be offered opportunities to participate in the project’s 
livelihood activities. They will also be considered for direct employment when opportunities emerge. 
Finally, they will benefit from the project’s other community benefits such as increased health access, 
and training to help them transition away from illegal activity. 
2) Local Community: During the project implementation, conflicts may occur owing to envy from local 
communities toward people coming from outside of the project zone. To mitigate this, the project 
proponent will ensure that the employment of local people is prioritized. Furthermore, the 
compensation given to the local workers should not differ significantly from that given to a worker from 
outside the project zone. In addition, potential conflicts may arise between the community and the 
project proponent regarding land use. To address this, all activities and decisions involving the land 
and resources in the project area will involve affected groups and the local and traditional leaders. 
3) Pollutants: During the project implementation, no pollutants will be generated because we avoid 
the continued felling and planting of acacia plantations and illegal palm plantation activities by local 
residents, as there will be no use of fertilizers and chemical pesticides. At the same time, the project 
sponsor will conduct regular forest patrols to ensure that no pollutants are generated in the project 
area. 
4) Safety of women, girls, children and other marginalized groups: During the project implementation, 
the project sponsor strongly supports the implementation of labor and anti-discrimination laws and 
regulations. The legitimate rights and interests of women, girls, children and other marginalized 
groups in the project area are protected. At the same time, the project has designed a complete 
litigation process, and the project party will promptly protect and safeguard the above groups in 
accordance with the process if the interests and safety of the above groups are threatened. 
At the same time, the positive impacts are expected to be: 
1. At least 300 people will participate in livelihood improvement training, and the proportion of women 
will account for 40% of the total number; 
2. In terms of providing jobs, the number of employees directly employed by the project is 90, and 40% 
of the employees are guaranteed to be women; 
3. The implementation of the project can directly or indirectly affect the livelihood activities of at least 
50% of the residents in the local community and improve at least 50% of the livelihood problems of 
women; 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
238 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
4. In terms of residents' health, all 2,056 residents of nearby villages and settlements and all 1,210 
female residents can get corresponding improvements; 
5. In terms of education, the project directly helped at least 8% of the residents of nearby villages and 
settlements to improve their corresponding academic qualifications, a total of 164 people. At least 8% 
of women and girls, a total of 97 people, were helped; 
6. In terms of clean water sources, at least 50% of residents in nearby villages and settlements can 
have their water supply problems improved accordingly, including 50% of women and children; 
7. It is expected that the total number of community members whose well-being will be improved 
through project activities will reach at least 50% of the current residents in the project area. 
4.2.3 Net Positive Community Well-Being (VCS, 3.19; CCB, CM2.3, GL1.4) 
Through the implementation of the project, we aim to create a significant net positive impact for the 
farming and fishing communities within the project site. The project will provide long-term livelihood 
security to the farming communities through agreements with local residents that guarantee their land 
tenure rights to existing lands that overlap with the project boundary. These stakeholders are also 
engaged and trained in fire monitoring and other forest patrol activities. It will also ensure that 
fishermen within the project site maintain access to their fishing grounds, and the project will also 
purchase fish to support the livelihoods of these people, which will also provide food for project staff. 
The project will also provide employment opportunities for this community if they are interested. Better 
hydrological management implemented by the project is expected to improve water quality in the 
project area, thereby creating more abundant and protected fishing grounds. 
The project will also help reduce fires in the wider project landscape through the deployment of well-
equipped firefighting teams and fire early warning systems (EWS). These efforts to protect 
concessions also protect important community assets and reduce the risk of fisheries or farms being 
destroyed. In addition, the well-being of nearby stakeholders will be improved as we develop 
community development plans and introduce community-centric activities to the PSPP and 
surrounding villages and settlements. The positive impacts of the PSPP community development 
program for other stakeholders will also affect fishermen and farmers who depend on the project 
area, as these communities live in villages and settlements surrounding the PSPP area. The benefits 
of this community development include improved access to clean water, effective waste management, 
healthcare, and education (see Section 4.3.1). Overall, ecosystem services in the project area will be 
significantly enhanced. Communities and other stakeholders in the area will benefit from an overall 
increase in quality of life and improved livelihoods resulting from project activities compared to what 
would have happened without the project. 
4.2.4 High Conservation Values Protected (CCB, CM2.4) 
For HCV 4 - Areas with Critical Ecosystem Services, the project area plays an important role in 
maintaining hydrological functions and water supply, preventing erosion and degradation risks caused 
by peat oxidation, and reducing fire risk by maintaining peat water levels and forest cover. In the 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
239 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
baseline scenario, the hydrological function will be permanently lost. This loss of hydrological function 
will lead to increased erosion, peat oxidation and reduced clean water supply. Fire risk will increase 
dramatically due to periodic loss of land cover. In the project scenario, peat water levels will be 
maintained and dry areas will be hydrologically restored. The remaining forest cover will be protected 
to reduce the risk of forest fires. 
For HCV 5 - Base Areas Meeting Basic Needs of Local Communities, the project area is used by the 
communities within the project area for non-timber forest products such as fish, clean water, etc., and 
timber to meet the needs of building boats and/or houses. In the baseline scenario, most of the 
project area will be converted to plantations. This will have an impact on increased drainage and will 
disturb the hydrological conditions in the area, therefore increasing the risk of fire. In the project 
scenario, forests in the project area will be protected and degraded areas will be restored.

4.4 Community Impact Monitoring  
4.4.1 Community Monitoring Plan (CCB, CM4.1, CM4.2, GL1.4, GL2.2, GL2.3, GL2.5) 
PSPP has developed a monitoring plan to track the impact of the project on the community and other 
stakeholders. The monitoring plan addresses the following core issues: improved livelihoods, 
educational support, and health. The plan considers the components required to successfully monitor 
all stakeholders and the impact of PSPP throughout the project period. Our community monitoring 
plan takes into account the overall requirements of the project as well as financial, logistical, and staff 
capacity constraints, starting with specific fishing and farming communities and expanding to other 
equally important stakeholder groups living in nearby settlements and villages. Data collection is 
carried out through community surveys, internal reports, and registration records. The target 
communities include nearby residents and small-scale farmers to ensure the comprehensiveness and 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
240 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
effectiveness of monitoring. The monitoring frequency is set every 3 months, every 6 months, and 
every 12 months depending on the needs of specific indicators. 
The monitoring plan aims to comprehensively assess the impact of the project on community 
livelihoods, environmental protection, gender equality, educational support, and health services. Key 
indicators include: 
1. Improved livelihoods: 
• The number of fishermen and farmers to assess the utilization of community livelihood resources. 
The utilization rate of fishermen is surveyed every 3 months and farmers are surveyed every 6 
months. 
• Improved community income, measured by the revenue from the sale of fish, with fisher income 
surveyed every 3 months. 
• The percentage of the food budget obtained from the PSPP community and other stakeholders, 
and the number of people directly employed, collect internal data and report every 12 months. 
2. Environmental Improvement: 
• Improvements in waste management, including the number of plastic recycling and waste 
collection projects implemented, are assessed every 12 months. 
• Frequency of fire safety training, record fire safety training every 3 months. 
• Gender Equality and Economic Success: 
• The number of investments and agreements with local women's groups, and the percentage of 
women directly employed by PSPP, are assessed every 12 months. 
3. Education Support: 
• The number of schools repaired or equipped, the number of university students supported, the 
number of students participating in after-school programs, and the number of students supported 
in adult education programs, are assessed every 12 months. 
• Survey the number of library books in each school in the surrounding villages. 
4. Improved Health Services: 
• The number of households with improved rainwater harvesting systems, and the number of 
improved sanitation facilities, are collected every 12 months.  
4.4.2 Monitoring Plan Dissemination (CCB, CM4.3) 
The community monitoring plan will be disseminated to the communities and other stakeholders. The 
project-related plans will be available in Indonesian along with the other project documents as 
mentioned in Section 2.3.4. In addition, the project team will also provide project progress information 
and results of the monitoring which will be displayed on the project website and disseminated in 
village meetings and information boards.

4.5.2 Short-term and Long-term Community Benefits (CCB, GL2.2) 
In the short term, the community benefits include:  
1. Improved access to health services through the village health service improvement program.  
2. Improved access to higher education (for their children) through scholarship assistance programs 
and cooperation with universities.  
3. Increased access to high quality education through education assistance programs in the village.   
4. Increased access to clean water through village water supply programs. 
5. Employment opportunities in the project through the company’s recruitment program. 
6. Hydrology system improvement will help prevent fires and floods in plantations and residential 
areas. 
In the long-term, the identified benefits are listed below:  
1. Protection of air and water quality: The project restores degraded wetlands and maintains forests 
that play an important role in maintaining water and air quality. They can act as natural filters, 
maintain the sustainability of the water cycle, prevent/suppress land and forest fires which will reduce 
the negative health impacts of fire smoke/haze to local communities. 
2. Improving the quality of life and resilience of the community through empowerment programs in 
education, health, and economy.  
4.5.3 Community Participation Risks (CCB, GL2.3)

and community participation. The FPIC process openly discussed risks for communities to participate 
in the project including potential impacts on livelihoods. Village meetings will be held regularly as a 
medium for socialization and evaluation of the project with the community by promoting several 
principles such as:  
1. Open dialog that provides opportunities for everyone to be actively involved.  
2. Ensuring that all community groups are represented in each meeting, so that all groups have equal 
opportunities and involvement.  
3. Ensuring that the decision-making process is carried out transparently and openly.

4.5.5 Net Impacts on Women (CCB, GL2.5) 
One of the objectives of the project is to strengthen the role of women in the community. To achieve 
this, several activities will be dedicated specifically to women’s groups in the community:  
1. Alternative livelihood program that provides training and financial assistance for business 
development of women’s groups.   
2. Training program on the involvement of women in decision-making. This training provides 
socialization on the importance of involving women in decision-making that often ignores issues 
concerning women.   
3. Women’s involvement in all village meetings, such as in FPIC meetings, Village Forums, and others.  
These activities are designed to promote the role of women in the villages. Based on social studies 
conducted at the village level, participation of women in decision-making is still low. Women-related 
issues such as domestic violence (DV) remain inadequately addressed in discussions at the village 
level. On the other hand, for some livelihoods, women are extensively involved in household income 
through trade, fisheries and wet-rice farming.  
4.5.6 Benefit Sharing Mechanisms (CCB, GL2.6)

meetings agreed upon by all community groups, in order to ensure transparency and fairness in the 
benefits provided. This process will be led and monitored by the project team and local community 
representatives.

4.5.7 Benefits, Costs, and Risks Communication (VCS, 3.18; CCB, GL2.7) 
The benefits, costs and risks of the project were discussed openly and transparently with all affected 
community groups during the free, prior and informed consent process. Project information was 
provided in a way that ensured all community members were aware of and understood the project. 
To ensure that the information provided is understood by community members, several methods were 
used, namely:  
1. Agreement through FPIC about the project and the benefits, costs and risks that will be faced by the 
communities. Ensure that FPIC is agreed by all community members through representatives of all 
groups in the villages. 
2. Collaborative implementation of project monitoring and evaluation with community members  
through regular village meetings, evaluations, and implementation of the grievance redress  
procedure. Ensure that the community provides feedback on the implementation of the project and 
that this feedback is followed up to build a high level of community trust in the project.   
3. Implementation of annual reporting on project achievements and benefit sharing as agreed. The 
meeting will be an annual communication event between the community and the project proponent. 
Reports will be submitted and received by all community members through group representatives 
throughout the community.  
4.5.8 Governance and Implementation Structures (CCB, GL2.8) 
The project will be implemented at the village level in collaboration with the village government and 
related institutions. Local community involvement in the project is developed through participatory 
planning, monitoring and evaluation and a grievance mechanism. By prioritizing the principles of FPIC, 
the project will ensure information disclosure and the involvement of all community groups in project 
planning and implementation.   
Community members can also be fully involved in project implementation through the recruitment of 
local staff, so that project planning and implementation initiatives can be monitored in depth. Where 
possible and in line with their work capacity and capability, local staff can fill middle and senior 
management positions, thereby further increasing the involvement of local staff in project planning 
and implementation. In the future, the number of community members employed in the project should 
increase in line with the project’s development of local human resources. Please also refer to Section 
2.4.1 regarding the project’s governance structure.

5 BIODIVERSITY 
5.1 Without-Project Biodiversity Scenario  
5.1.1 Existing Conditions (VCS, 3.19; CCB, B1.1) 
Biodiversity refers to the diversity of all life forms on Earth, including species diversity, genetic 
diversity, and ecosystem diversity. Biodiversity is crucial for maintaining ecosystem stability and 
adapting to environmental changes, serving as one of the foundational resources for human survival 
and development (Myers, 1997). Globally recognized biodiversity hotspots, such as the tropical 
rainforests of Southeast Asia and the Amazon Basin in South America, host an exceptionally rich 
variety of species and complex ecological networks, making them among the most concentrated 
regions of biodiversity on Earth (Mittermeier et al., 2004). 
In recent years, global biodiversity has faced unprecedented threats due to intensified human 
activities, including deforestation, habitat fragmentation, climate change, and overfishing (WWF, 
2020). According to the Global Biodiversity Outlook 5 (2020) report, approximately one million species 
are currently threatened with extinction, many of which could disappear within the next few decades. 
In addition, habitat loss and degradation have led to sharp declines in many species, with global 
terrestrial populations decreasing by 68% since 1970 (WWF, 2020). 
In Southeast Asia, the degradation of tropical peatlands and mangrove ecosystems is a major driver of 
biodiversity loss. These ecosystems provide critical habitats for many endangered species, such as the 
Bornean orangutan (Pongo pygmaeus) and the Sumatran tiger (Panthera tigris sumatrae), which play 
key roles in regional biodiversity (Harrison et al., 2020). According to data from the International Union 
for Conservation of Nature (IUCN), populations of these species have significantly declined, and they 
are classified as "Critically Endangered" (IUCN, 2022). 
To mitigate biodiversity loss, various conservation projects and policies are being implemented 
globally. Non-governmental organizations (NGOs) such as Conservation International and the World-
Wide Fund for Nature (WWF) advocate for the establishment of ecological corridors, restoration of 
degraded habitats, and increased community engagement to enhance the effectiveness of 
conservation efforts (WWF, 2020; Conservation International, 2018). Additionally, the Convention on 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
246 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
Biological Diversity (CBD) and its subsequent agreements provide a strategic framework for global 
biodiversity conservation, encouraging countries to implement biodiversity action plan. 
PT. Suntara Gajapati (SGP), a company located in Dumai, Riau Province, Indonesia, is one of the 
suppliers of Asia Pulp & Paper (APP). The project area lies within SGP's IUPHHK-HT concession, 
covering a total of 34,792 hectares, located at the geographical coordinates of 101°03'46"–
101°18'04" E and 01°49'31"–02°05'20" S. According to the revised RKUPH 2017–2026 document, 
and with reference to the Riau Provincial Spatial Plan and the Riau Provincial Forest and Water Area 
Map (in accordance with Minister of Environment and Forestry Decree No. 
SK.903/MenLHK/Setjen/PLA.2/12/2016, dated December 7, 2016), PT Suntara Gajapati’s IUPHHK-
HT area is classified as a Permanent Production Forest Area (HP), covering 34,232 hectares, with an 
Area for Other Purposes (APL) spanning 560 hectares. 
Based on biodiversity assessments conducted up until 2023, the project area's ecosystem types 
mainly include peat swamp forests and mangrove ecosystems. These ecosystems are rich in natural 
resources, providing habitat for at least 231 species. To date, 232 species have been identified in the 
project area, including 148 animal species (15 mammals, 109 birds, and 25 amphibians and reptiles) 
and 83 plant species. Of the 232 recorded species, 13 are listed as endangered on the IUCN Red List, 
including the Sumatran tiger (Panthera tigris sumatrae), the Lar Gibbon (Hylobates agilis), the lesser 
adjutant (Leptoptilos javanicus), the sambar deer (Cervus unicolor), and the pig-tailed macaque 
(Macaca nemestrina). Additionally, 31 species are included in Appendix I and II of the Convention on 
International Trade in Endangered Species of Wild Fauna and Flora (CITES), which prohibits their 
trade. 
5.1.1.1 Biodiversity baseline 
Based on the results of annual biodiversity monitoring within the project area, it has been shown that 
species and ecosystems of conservation value are present. Among the 15 identified mammal species, 
10 are classified as endangered, restricted, or protected (TTD), and these species are found within the 
concession area and surrounding landscapes. Of these species, 10 are listed in the IUCN Red List (3 
Endangered and 5 Vulnerable species), 7 species are included in the CITES appendices (5 in Appendix 
II and 2 in Appendix I), and are protected under the Republic of Indonesia's Government Regulation 
No. 7 of 1999 on the Preservation of Plant and Animal Species. The Sumatran tiger, which is endemic 
to Sumatra, is one of these species. 
Additionally, 109 bird species from 42 families were identified and confirmed within the concession 
area. Overall, based on direct bird observations or expert opinions on the landscape's potential 
carrying capacity, bird populations in the project area are found to have favorable survival conditions. 
Of these birds, a total of 27 species are protected under the IUCN Red List (1 Endangered species and 
1 Vulnerable species), CITES appendices (15 species in Appendix II and 2 species in Appendix I), and 
Indonesian government regulations (25 species). 
Furthermore, 25 species of amphibians and reptiles were observed within the project area, including 
10 amphibians and 15 reptiles. Among these species, 5 are protected under the IUCN Red List (2 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
247 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
Endangered species), CITES appendices (3 species in Appendix II and 2 species in Appendix I), and 
Indonesian government regulations (2 species). 
A total of 83 plant species have been identified within the project area, four of which are listed by the 
IUCN as threatened species: Gonystylus bancanus, Madhuca motleyana, Shorea teysmanniana, and 
Shorea uliginosa. Additionally, one plant species, Gonystylus bancanus, is listed in Appendix II of the 
Convention on International Trade in Endangered Species of Wild Fauna and Flora (CITES).

Most of the land in the project area has already been developed, with natural forests remaining only in 
the northern protected zone, while the rest of the area has been taken over by oil palm plantations 
and Acacia plantations. Based on natural environmental conditions, the types of natural ecosystems 
within the project area include two main types: peat swamp forest ecosystems and mangrove 
ecosystems, both of which are facing degradation risks. Thanks to the conservation and restoration 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
250 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
efforts by SGP over the past two years, the area of protected land has increased. As of 2024, the total 
area of the protected area in the project area has been reduced to about 1,800 hectares. 
5.1.1.2 Threats to Biodiversity 
The main threats to biodiversity in the project area include deforestation, land-use changes, and 
poaching. Large areas of primary forest have been cleared for plantations (such as palm oil and 
pulpwood plantations) and agricultural development. This deforestation has resulted in the loss of 
habitats for wildlife, putting many forest-dependent species at risk of extinction. Additionally, poaching 
of endangered animals, such as the Sumatran tiger, has exacerbated the risk of extinction for these 
species. 
Currently, the surrounding communities in the PT SGP area primarily rely on natural resources for 
plantation-based activities. In the 1990s, the relatively low land prices attracted many migrants who 
established oil palm plantations. The success of the oil palm business has encouraged locals to 
expand palm cultivation, and even today, oil palm plantations continue to replace rubber plantations, 
coconut groves, rice paddies, and secondary crops. 
Another livelihood activity that significantly impacts biodiversity in the project area is logging. Driven by 
community demand for timber and the potential for economic gain, loggers have been clearing 
vegetation in the peat swamp forest areas of the project site, leading to the destruction of large 
swaths of habitat. Logging activities in peat swamp forests disrupt the habitats of protected species 
that heavily rely on canopy cover, such as the Sumatran tiger (Panthera tigris sumatrae) and the agile 
gibbon (Hylobates agilis).

areas of enrichment in habitats associated with the survival of these species. Areas of HCV 1 have 
been identified as being present in the project area.

5.1.2.2 HCV 2: Landscapes and Dynamic Nature 
HCV 2 consists of relatively undisturbed, large-scale ecosystems and ecosystem mosaics at a level of 
scale and undisturbedness sufficient to support the survival of the vast majority of natural species 
populations and to contain other relevant environmental values embodied by the vast majority of 
these ecosystems. 
High conservation value HCV 2.1: Large landscapes with the capacity to sustain natural 
ecological processes and dynamics 
Qualifying attribute Based on the results of remote sensing analyses, interviews with 
management unit staff, and direct field observations, it is known 
that there are still large areas of landforms in and around the 
concession area that will ensure the occurrence of natural 
ecosystem processes in the long term. According to stakeholder 
consultation and Landsat image analysis, the concession area is 
bordered on the north and west by the PT Diamon Raya Timber 
Company and the Senepis Forest Conservation Forest Area. Landsat 
image analysis shows that the core forest area of the area is more 
than 20,000 hectares. Therefore, there is a natural landscape with a 
core area of more than 20,000 hectares within the concession area.

or manage a certain proportion of each type of functional ecosystem to achieve sustainability.

5.1.3 Without-project Scenario: Biodiversity (CCB, B1.3) 
Without project implementation, biodiversity in the project area will continue to face multiple threats, 
leading to further ecosystem degradation. The main threats include: 
(1). Deforestation and Land Use Change: Without the project, ongoing deforestation activities will 
persist, particularly for the expansion of oil palm plantations and pulpwood plantations. Primary 
forests will continue to be cleared for commercial agricultural development. Since forests are the 
primary habitat for many endangered species, such as the Sumatran tiger, white-handed gibbon, and 
many other forest-dependent species, habitat destruction will result in a significant decline in these 
populations and may lead to their local extinction. 
(2). Poaching and Illegal Logging: In the absence of project interventions, poaching and illegal logging 
activities will intensify. Endangered species like the Sumatran tiger, which holds high economic value, 
will remain prime targets for poachers. Additionally, illegal logging activities will not only degrade peat 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
255 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
swamp forest areas but also further reduce habitat for these species, posing severe threats to forest-
dependent wildlife. 
(3). Peatland Degradation and Greenhouse Gas Emissions: Logging activities in peat swamp forests 
will not only damage vegetation cover but also release significant amounts of carbon dioxide, 
exacerbating climate change. In the “without-project scenario,” the degradation of peatlands will 
continue, leading to increased carbon emissions, which will further negatively impact the global 
climate. 
(4). Further Decline in Biodiversity: With the continued loss of habitats and increased human activity, 
biodiversity in the project area will continue to decline. Habitat fragmentation will reduce genetic 
diversity among species, making them more vulnerable to environmental changes and other external 
threats. 
Overall, the “without-project scenario” will lead to severe degradation of ecosystems and species 
within the project area. Most of the project area will be developed into oil palm plantations and Acacia 
plantations. The protected area in the northern part of the project area will disappear, failing to 
provide sustainable habitats for forest-dependent flora and fauna. Species such as the Sumatran tiger 
and white-handed gibbon will face extinction within the project area. 
5.2 Net Positive Biodiversity Impacts  
5.2.1 Expected Biodiversity Changes (VCS, 3.19; CCB, B2.1) 
Under the project scenario, the forest in the project area will be protected from the threat of planned 
deforestation and forest degradation. The expected biodiversity changes as a result of project 
implementation are summarized below:  
Biodiversity element Natural Ecosystem  
Estimated change Reduced factors that contribute to species extinction and 
biodiversity loss. 
Justification of change As a part of the project, there will be forest protection initiatives, 
which include several programs as listed in Section 2.1.17. These 
programs are expected to have a significant positive impact on the 
ecosystem within the nearby project area, which will create a 
positive domino effect for the biodiversity in the area due to 
improved habitat conditions and connectivity.  
 
Biodiversity element Flora and Fauna 
Estimated change Gradual dispersal and increase of flora and fauna species in the 
project area.

                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
256 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
Justification of change It is anticipated that the abundance of flora and fauna in the project 
area will increase due to the project activities. The positive impact of 
the project can be determined by comparing the abundance of 
threatened plants and animals in the project area to the number 
previously identified. This will be accomplished by a regular patrol 
team that will patrol the project area. In addition, monitoring of 
fauna will be conducted to monitor the populations of threatened 
species.  
 
Biodiversity element Forest Cover 
Estimated change Reduced deforestation and forest degradation. 
Justification of change The project has prevented deforestation and forest degradation in 
the project area. This was achieved by avoiding continued felling of 
industrial acacia plantations and discouraging residents of nearby 
villages from clearing forests for palm plantations and other forest 
conservation activities. Over time, restoration efforts will lead to 
greater forest cover.  
5.2.2 Mitigation Measures (VCS, 3.19; CCB, B2.3) 
The project seeks to avoid any negative offsite impacts on biodiversity and other social aspects by 
devoting significant efforts and resources to detecting the presence, understanding the 
characteristics, and identifying the sensitivity of all project activities. As discussed in Section 5.2.1.1, 
the PSPP is designed to bring positive impacts and does not anticipate any negative impacts to 
biodiversity.  
Although the project is not expected to have any negative impacts on biodiversity, the project will 
implement the following mitigation measures to increase species abundance and diversity within the 
project area: forest patrols, fire prevention and fire suppression, and canal blocking activities. An 
active and regular biodiversity monitoring program will also be implemented to assess the regular 
increase of specific species and provide information for management activities throughout the PSPP 
area. Key biodiversity management activities include investing significant resources to detect, identify 
and understand the characteristics, distribution, sensitivity and vulnerability of HCVs. And because the 
prescribed project activities successfully lead to an increase in biodiversity, this may also lead to the 
emergence of activities such as illegal logging or poaching. To protect the project area from illegal 
activities, the community development team also conducts regular consultations to maintain 
appropriate monitoring of the area. 
5.2.3 Net Positive Biodiversity Impacts (CCB, B2.2, GL1.4) 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
257 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
The project scenario will have a higher net positive impact on biodiversity compared to the conditions 
under the no-project land use scenario. During the project implementation period, biodiversity in the 
project area has three main components, namely (i) ecosystems, (ii) plant and animal species, 
especially critically endangered, endangered and vulnerable plant and animal species, and (iii) forest 
cover. The remaining forests in both parts of the project area are essential to ensure the viability of 
plant and animal diversity, especially protected species. In addition, some ecosystems have multiple 
functions, such as peat forests, which not only provide habitats for protected species but also act as 
water cycle regulators and carbon storage. 
The biodiversity impact target of the project is to minimize or, where possible, eliminate disturbances 
to the presence of threatened species (habitat encroachment, fire and hunting) through planned 
conservation work and regular patrols. At the same time, the project will implement peatland rewetting 
activities, through canal plugging activities to slow down the rate of subsidence and maintain the peat 
water level, maintaining the average groundwater level at about 0.4 meters below the peat surface for 
one year. This rewetting plan is essential to maintain the physical stability and productivity of the 
peatlands, allowing restoration work to proceed. All of these efforts will provide habitat expansion and 
enhance connectivity of the project area to larger surrounding forest areas, which will benefit the long-
term viability of threatened species populations. 
5.2.4 High Conservation Values Protected (CCB, B2.4) 
Four categories of biodiversity-related HCV areas were identified in the project area, as described in 
Section 5.1.2. They are namely:  
HCV 1 - Concentrations of diversity of global, regional or national importance 
• Biodiversity in protected or conserved areas. 
• Endangered species. 
• Viable populations of endangered, restricted or protected species. 
• Species or groups of species that temporarily use habitats. 
HCV 2 - Landscapes and natural dynamics 
• Large landscapes capable of sustaining natural ecological processes and dynamics. 
• Natural areas containing two or more ecosystems with uninterrupted (contiguous) boundaries. 
• Areas containing representative populations of species. 
HCV 3 - Rare or endangered ecosystems 
HCV 4 - Key Ecosystem Services 
• Water supply and flood protection services for downstream communities. 
• Natural barrier services to prevent the spread of forest or land fires. 
Protecting biodiversity is a key focus in the project activities. The project contributes to preserving and 
improving the area and quality of forests and natural ecosystems, as well as connecting each HCV 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
258 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
area. This emphasis on preserving and improving forests and natural ecosystems to protect them will 
have a significant positive impact on HCVs 1-4. Infrastructure such as basecamps, guard posts, fire 
towers, and nurseries will be built outside of the critical ecosystems.  
5.2.5 Species Used (VCS, 3.19; CCB, B2.5, B2.6)

5.2.6 Invasive Species (VCS, 3.19; CCB, B2.5)

5.2.7 GMO Exclusion (CCB, B2.7) 
No genetically modified organisms (GMOs) will be used in the project. In addition, planting activities do 
not make use of seeds derived from GMOs.  
5.2.8 Inputs Justification (VCS, 3.19; CCB, B2.8) 
No fertilizers, chemical pesticides, biological control agents nor other similar inputs will be used in the 
project’s restoration efforts.  
5.2.9 Waste Products (VCS, 3.19; CCB, B2.9) 
There are some potential waste products from the project activities. Thus, it is necessary to reduce the 
waste products that are generated. Waste generated by human activities (domestic waste) in field 
offices, basecamps, and guard posts is one example of potential waste generated by project activities. 
Domestic waste includes solid waste such as plastic waste, used cans, used plastic packaging, 
Styrofoam, paper, and food waste, as well as liquid waste such as soapy water, detergent water, oil, 
and faeces. Moreover, there is the possibility of waste from operational activities such as used 
generator oil, used vehicle or engine oil, gasoline or diesel spills, and used batteries. In line with the 
company’s Environmental Management and Monitoring Plan (UKL/UPL), the project will develop a 
Standard Operating Procedure (SOP) for waste management. The project will apply the 3R concept 
(Reduce, Reuse, Recycle), which involves reusing waste that can still be used or has other functions, 
reducing everything that causes or generates waste, and recycling waste into a product or item that 
can be useful.

B3.2) 
The project is designed not to have negative impacts on biodiversity outside the project area, as 
described in Section 5.2.1. The project will continue to monitor activities occurring at the project site

closely and will address potential threats to biodiversity through adaptive management systems to

5.3.2 Net Offsite Biodiversity Benefits (VCS, 3.19; CCB, B3.3) 
The project focuses on biodiversity conservation and biodiversity habitat improvement, especially for 
key species such as the Sumatran tiger, white-handed gibbon, and Sumatran otter. Compared with the 
no-project scenario, the project scenario is considered to have a positive impact on biodiversity in the 
project area and its surrounding areas. Considering the project's activities in species and habitat 
patrol and monitoring, the project will provide more favorable conditions for species and habitat 
conservation. 
5.4 Biodiversity Impact Monitoring  
5.4.1 Biodiversity Monitoring Plan (CCB, B4.1, B4.2, GL1.4, GL3.4) 
Biodiversity monitoring is a long-term process that involves a range of elements, including the setting 
of targets, indicator selection, sampling design, data collection methods, biodiversity description, data 
analysis and interpretation of results. Therefore, the development of a professional and detailed 
program is essential for the implementation of monitoring as well as the evaluation of the project. In 
order to successfully evaluate the biodiversity benefits of the project and to ensure that the scientific 
evidence required for the subsequent validation of the CCB criteria is adequately obtained, the main 
objectives of this monitoring work include: 
1. Monitor and assess changes in biodiversity in the project area. 
2. Ensure that projects have positive impacts on ecosystems and minimise negative impacts. 
3. Protect and restore the ecological integrity of the area through long-term monitoring and 
assessment. 
4. Provide information to communities, conservation organizations and relevant stakeholders to 
support sustainable resource management. 
To integrate project considerations and to ensure that project impacts on biodiversity can be 
effectively assessed, the following monitoring indicators have been developed: 
Table 5-2 Monitoring indicators 
Monitoring Indicator Content 
Species Diversity 
Monitors species richness within the project area 
(including plants, birds, mammals, amphibians, reptiles, 
and invertebrates).

CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4

Focuses on the quantity and distribution of endangered or 
threatened species within the project area. 
Habitat Health 
Assesses ecosystem health through vegetation coverage, 
habitat connectivity, and habitat degradation conditions. 
Invasive Species 
Monitors invasive species within the project area and 
evaluates their threat to native species. 
Biodiversity Utilization 
Assesses the community or other stakeholders' usage of 
natural resources to determine if there is overexploitation. 
Before monitoring begins, three key phases must be completed. First, an assessment team must be 
established, with clear division of responsibilities for each task to ensure the orderly execution of the 
project’s monitoring work. Second, a desktop assessment of biodiversity value and potential 
biodiversity impacts should be conducted. This is the starting point for the biodiversity monitoring 
plan, determining the nature of biodiversity values that may exist and be affected. The participation of 
multiple stakeholders in this assessment is crucial. Participants should include government bodies 
(such as competent authorities and local governments), protected area management agencies, 
operational companies (including tourism-related companies managing protected areas and their 
surroundings), local communities, and other social organizations (such as voluntary organizations, 
research institutions, biodiversity conservation NGOs, and other public groups or individuals). Local, 
national, or international biodiversity conservation NGOs can act as partners, bringing together 
stakeholders for the consultation process. Many of these organizations have rich experience in 
collaborating with local stakeholders (such as communities) and possess extensive knowledge of 
biodiversity and conservation measures. Joint participation by stakeholders helps provide a more 
accurate understanding of the value of biodiversity in protected areas and various potential impacts. 
Lastly, a baseline must be established to understand the current state of biodiversity in the project 
area. Future monitoring results can be compared to this baseline. Existing literature (such as the IUCN 
Red List, China's Red List of Species, national/local biodiversity conservation action plans, biodiversity 
hotspots, WWF ecoregions, endemic bird areas, important bird areas, plant diversity centers, etc.) can 
assist in identifying key habitats/species at risk and their current status. 
For the convenience and scientific rigor of monitoring, the primary monitoring methods will include 
field surveys, remote monitoring, and community participation. To that end, all permanent monitoring 
points (camera trap sites and listening stations), transects (for plant, bird, mammal, reptile, amphibian 
surveys, and patrol areas), and the SMART patrol system must be established. After setting up the 
permanent listening stations, a well-trained monitoring team will conduct comprehensive surveys. The 
SMART system will be used to monitor project activities at multiple levels. 
Vegetation surveys will focus on secondary forests and areas of old/young shrublands, with monitoring 
targets including trees, shrubs, and herbaceous plants. A rapid assessment method will be used to 
observe vegetation characteristics along transects. In each representative ecosystem or habitat, 
transects will be set up, and surveyors will walk along these lines, observing and recording 
encountered plants and other ecological elements. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
261 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
For amphibians and reptiles, appropriate transects should be established based on the habitat within 
the project area. Monitors can directly observe vegetation and ground surfaces, lift fallen logs and 
stones, and examine crevices in stones, bark, and stumps. After the survey, all items must be returned 
to their original positions, and efforts should be made to minimize habitat disturbance, thereby 
preserving microhabitats. Each site should be observed for at least three days, including morning, 
afternoon, and nighttime periods. 
Bird monitoring will use a combination of transect and fixed-point methods. Monitoring areas should 
cover as much of the project area as possible to maximize species observation. Surveys should be 
conducted in fair weather, without strong winds or heavy rain. Monitoring should begin after sunrise 
and avoid periods of low bird activity, such as midday. While walking, monitors should record all bird 
individuals within a specific range on both sides of the transect, including both sightings and 
vocalizations. To avoid double-counting, only birds flying from the front of the transect to the back 
should be recorded, not those flying from behind to the front of the monitor. At each sampling point, 
the duration of the survey should be sufficient to detect all bird individuals in the area. Short 
observation periods may overlook some bird species, while extended periods increase the likelihood of 
double-counting individuals. Typically, a duration of 5 to 10 minutes per point is recommended, and 
after arriving at the point, monitors should wait for one minute before starting to record observations. 
For mammal surveys, the Recce method will be used, which is an improved version of the line transect 
method, designed to facilitate movement and save travel time. The goal of this survey is to collect 
objective data on mammal species with consistent encounter rates, in order to estimate the presence 
probabilities of Sumatran tigers and other key wildlife species, and to check for various threats such 
as poaching and logging. To increase the chances of encounters, surveys will also be conducted by 
boat along canals and rivers. Additionally, fixed infrared camera monitoring points and gibbon listening 
stations will be established, with checks and monitoring conducted once a month.

5.4.2 Biodiversity Monitoring Plan Dissemination (CCB, B4.3) 
The results of biodiversity monitoring are disseminated through two main channels. First, an annual 
report is distributed to relevant government stakeholders, including the Ministry of Environment, 
BKSDA143, the Provincial Forestry Office, and the Environmental Agency. Second, information is 
continuously shared via social media and the project website. Additionally, to reach rural communities, 
the project team recommends regularly publishing information sheets and displaying them on village 
information boards. Information will also be communicated verbally at community meetings and in 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
264 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
local schools. Sharing information with stakeholders is one of the mitigation measures; this data will 
assist stakeholders in implementing appropriate mitigation actions. 
5.5 Optional Criterion: Exceptional Biodiversity Benefits  
The project aims to achieve special biodiversity benefits and to be verified as Gold Level based on the 
presence of species listed on the IUCN Red List (GL3) with significant ecological importance in the 
project area. 
5.5.1 High Biodiversity Conservation Priority Status (CCB, GL3.1) 
Throughout the project area, multiple species with high biodiversity conservation priority status have 
been identified, including mammals, birds, reptiles, and plants. Of the 232 species recorded, 13 are 
considered endangered according to the IUCN Red List, such as the Sumatran tiger (Panthera tigris 
sumatrae), Agile gibbon (Hylobates agilis), Lesser adjutant stork (Leptoptilos javanicus), Sambar deer 
(Cervus unicolor), and Pig-tailed macaque (Macaca nemestrina). Additionally, 31 species are listed in 
Appendix I and II of the Convention on International Trade in Endangered Species of Wild Fauna and 
Flora (CITES), prohibiting their trade, and one species is endemic to Sumatra. 
5.5.2 Trigger Species Population Trends (CCB, GL3.2, GL3.3) 
According to the Climate, Community & Biodiversity (CCB) Standards, trigger species must meet the 
criteria of "vulnerability" or "irreplaceability." "Vulnerability" is defined as the frequent presence of 
species in the IUCN Red List within the project area (species listed as Critically Endangered (CR), 
Endangered (EN), or Vulnerable (VU)). "Irreplaceability" is defined as the presence of 1-5% of the 
global population of a species in the project area (at any stage of its life cycle). The project’s 
preliminary biodiversity survey found that at least 13 species (8 mammals, 1 bird, 1 reptile, and 3 
plants) in the project area are listed as endangered on the IUCN Red List, meeting the vulnerability 
criteria and therefore identified as trigger species. 
Trigger species Sumatran tiger (Panthera tigris sumatrae) 
Population trend at start of project The Sumatran tiger (Panthera tigris sumatrae) is an 
endemic species of Sumatra Island. The International Union 
for Conservation of Nature (IUCN) has listed it as Critically 
Endangered, noting that its population is declining. 
Without-project scenario Without this project, biodiversity is highly likely to be 
negatively impacted by the increasing development and 
land-use changes in the project area, leading to a reduction 
in species numbers and potentially even local extinction 
within the project area.

Bird species Lesser Adjutant Stork (Leptoptilos javanicus) 
Population trend at start of project The Lesser Adjutant Stork (Leptoptilos javanicus) is listed 
as an endangered species on the IUCN Red List, and its 
population is declining, primarily distributed in South and 
Southeast Asia. The global population is estimated to be 
between 6,500 and 8,000 individuals, and they are 
generally threatened by habitat loss. The stork tends to 
forage and nest in wetlands, rice fields, and similar areas, 
especially in Nepal, India, and Southeast Asia, where they

Reptiles’ species False gharial (Tomistoma schlegelii) 
Population trend at start of project The False gharial (Tomistoma schlegelii) is listed as an 
endangered species on the IUCN Red List. It primarily 
inhabits lowland swamps, peat swamp forests, and slow-
moving freshwater environments in the Malay Peninsula, 
Borneo, and Sumatra. However, due to the destruction of 
wetland habitats (especially the reclamation of peatlands, 
logging, fires, and agricultural expansion), the species' 
range has been significantly reduced. 
Without-project scenario Without this project, biodiversity is highly likely to be 
negatively impacted by the increasing development and 
land-use changes in the project area, leading to a reduction 
in species numbers. 
With-project scenario After the project's implementation, the core goal is to 
restore and protect the wetlands, peatlands, and lowland 
forest habitats that the False gharial relies on for survival. 
This can be achieved by limiting agricultural expansion, 
logging, and other human activities that destroy wetland 
habitats, ensuring the integrity of swamps and forests, and 
thereby increasing species numbers.

APPENDIX 5: PERFORMANCE 
BENCHMARK CALCULATION  
The methodology implemented in this appendix follows the requirements specified in Appendix 1: 
Performance Methods of VM0047 v1.0. This section aims to explain the methods used to calculate 
the change in loading index and determine the performance benchmark. 
Selection of Stocking Index (SI) 
For this project, it was decided to use a vegetation index, the Normalized Difference Fraction Index 
(NDFI). NDFI is a Normalized Difference Fraction Index that measures the differences in spectral 
components between different types of land cover, and is particularly suitable for monitoring wetlands, 
peatlands and degraded areas in remote sensing images. It is based on surface components such as 
green vegetation (GV), non-photosynthetic vegetation (NPV), soil (Soil) and shadow (Shade), and is 
calculated through spectral mixture analysis (LSMA). NDFI is considered to be a sensitive and reliable 
ecological status index that can effectively reflect vegetation degradation and health. The study by 
Handayani et al. (2020) used the NDFI index to estimate aboveground carbon stocks in Indonesian 
peatland forests and compared their results with field carbon measurement data
20
. The results 
showed a significant positive correlation between NDFI and aboveground carbon stocks, showing good 
accuracy and reliability. The study confirmed that NDFI is an effective remote sensing tool for 
monitoring changes in carbon stocks and degradation in peatlands. Therefore, the project used NDFI 
as a stocking index for performance benchmarking and as a parameter for estimating the correlation 
between stocking index and aboveground biomass (AGB). 
NDFI was generated using Landsat 8 surface reflectance data through remote sensing analysis using 
the Google Earth Engine platform. The collection period is from 2015 to 2025 and is used to obtain 
historical stocking index status. 
Project plot selection 
To select the project plots, we divided the project area into 1-hectare grids and selected 30 plots 
based on a systematic random approach. The 1-hectare grid was created based on spatial analysis 
and land use/land cover (LULC) information in the project area and screened. All of the selected 30 
plots were located in non-forest areas. 
Defining donor pool areas for control plots 
To determine the donor pool area, first a 100 km buffer zone was created based on the centroid 
points of the project plots. Second, it was gridded into 1-hectare plots. Third, the plots were screened 
 
20
 Handayani, D. E., Krisnawati, H., & Adinugroho, W. C. (2020). Estimating aboveground biomass and 
carbon stocks in tropical peat swamp forests using Landsat-derived indices. International Journal of 
Remote Sensing, 41(15), 5636–5658. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template

279 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
based on different raster and vector geospatial layers. The screening of donor pool area plots was 
based on the factors detailed in Table A1 of VM0047 v1.0 and land use/land use change (LULC) 
conditions. 
Once the donor pool areas were screened based on all these criteria, donor pool areas of control plots 
with similar biophysical and political characteristics to the project plots were obtained. 
Stocking index time series for plots 
Once the project plots and screened donor pool areas were identified, the annual stocking index time 
series for each plot during the target period from t-10 to t0 was obtained, where t0 was the project 
start year. At least three time points were used to select control plots and evaluate the control plots 
matched to the project plots through stocking index (SI) regression. The three time points included: t-
10 to t-9, between t-6 and t-5, and at t0. This project used 3 time points, including t-10, t-6, and t0. 
Selecting control plots, assessing match quality, and finalizing matches 
Control plots were selected from the donor pool area and matched to the project plots by K-nearest 
neighbor matching (without replacement). Matching was done using the Spatial Analyst tool, using 
Euclidean distance as the distance metric and 1 as K (the number of nearest neighbors for each 
project plot).The nearest neighbors were determined based on the Euclidean distance of each project 
plot, and the five control plots with the smallest Euclidean distance values were matched. The SI 
values used as covariates were the same as those shown in step 4: t-10, t-6, and t0. Based on this 
process, 30 control plots were selected. 
After the control plots were selected, the quality of the match was assessed by calculating the 
standardized mean difference (SDM). The SDM for each covariate was calculated as in Formula A2 
(VM0047 v1.0, see Section 3.1.4.2 for a detailed description of SDM). The SDM calculation results 
showed a mean value of 0.001899, which is less than 0.25. Accordingly, 30 control plots matching 
the 30 project plots were considered valid. 
Ex-ante Estimations  
The calculation of ex-ante estimates involves:  
a) Estimating performance benchmark from historical period (t-10 -  t0)  
b) Generating a regression of SI on above-ground biomass  
c) Estimating the SI increase 40 years ahead, for project (ΔSI
wp,t
) and control plots (ΔSI
control,t
).  
d) Calculating ex-ante estimation of Performance Benchmark 
 
a) Estimating performance benchmark from historical period (t-10 -  t0)  
Performance benchmark from the historical period was calculated based on ΔSI
wp,t
 and ΔSI
control,t. 
Both parameters were estimated across the sample populations of project and control plots. The rate

explanation of the formula). The z-test requires the standard error of the average annual rate of

control,t
 were considered not to be significantly 
different (Z value < 1.96, no significant difference in parameters). 
b) Generate a regression model between SI and aboveground biomass 
According to the VM0047 v1.0 standard, to generate ΔSI
wp,t
  for ex ante estimation, a regression 
model between aboveground biomass and stocking index should be established. Linear regression 
analysis was only conducted for non-forested areas that met the ARR project conditions. Its formula is as 
follows: 
Y=0.0241∗ln(x)+0.3084 
Where y is the NDFI, or stocking index, and x is the aboveground biomass value (in tonnes of dry matter 
per hectare).

The ex-ante value of ΔSI
control,t
 for the period t0 to t40 was estimated from the slope of linear regression 
of the SI over the last 10 years on the control plots. For example, if the slope of the regression is -
0.0176 over the last 10 years, then the control plots are expected to maintain the same growth rate 
over the next 40 years.
